What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Dalalat al-Tadamun: Refined Definition and Refutation of Objections
Author defines tadamun as signification of part via whole, addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dalalat al-tadamun from dalalat al-iltizam and explain why the refined view avoids the objection of double understanding.

Core Idea
Tadamun is not a transition but a single understanding viewed from two perspectives: whole and part.

This passage examines the nature of tadamun (signification by implication). After presenting an illustrative example and two objections, the author offers a refined definition that resolves the difficulties and distinguishes tadamun from iltizam. The discussion uses the classic example of 'insan' (human) composed of 'hayawan' (animal) and 'natiq' (rational).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
From the passage, note the three types: mutabaqa (whole meaning), tadamun (part within whole), and iltizam (necessary concomitant). The author defines tadamun as understanding the part without a transition.
2
Understand al-Malawi's example
You see a distant shape and are told it is a human. You immediately understand it is an animal without focusing on the rational part. This illustrates how the part (animal) is understood from the whole (human) without a separate step.
The example shows a natural transition from whole to part.
3
Extract the two objections
First objection: The whole is mentally prior to the part, but logicians agree the part is prior in both mental and external existence. Second objection: The part would be understood twice (once in the whole, once separately), contradicting experience.
These objections target the idea of a transition.
4
Learn the refined definition
The muhaqqiqun propose that tadamun is simply understanding the part within the whole in one act. There is no transition; relative to the whole it is mutabaqa, relative to the part it is tadamun.
This avoids the double understanding objection.
5
Analyze Abd al-Hakim's response
He answers the first objection by distinguishing the part's priority in essence (bi dhatihi) from its priority in understanding from the word. The whole is prior in understanding from the word, even if the part is prior in essence.
This reconciles the conflicting priorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجُزْنِهِ : «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ الْتِزَامُ إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمُ

وَصَوَّرَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ بِمَا إِذَا رَأَيْتَ شَبَحاً مِنْ بُعْدِ، وَشَكَكْتَ فِيهِ هَلْ هُوَ حَيَوَانُ أَوْ لَا؟ فَقِيلَ لَكَ : هُوَ إِنْسَانٌ، فَفَهِمْتَ أَنَّهُ حَيَوَانُ، وَلَمْ تَلْتَفِتْ إِلَى كَوْنِهِ نَاطِقاً، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَقَعُ فِي الذِّهْنِ أَوَّلاً المَعْنَى بِتَمَامِهِ؛ قَالَ : فَهَذَا مِثَالٌ يَظْهَرُ فِيهِ الانْتِقَالُ مِنْ مَعْنَى اللَّفْظِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ ، إِذْ لَا مَانِعَ مِنْ أَنْ يُفْهَمَ المَعْنَى إِجْمَالاً ، ثُمَّ يَنتَقِلُ الذِّهْنُ إِلَى جُزْنِهِ.

وَيُحَثُّ فِيهِ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ تَقَدُّمَ الكُلِّ عَلَى الجُزْءِ ذِهْناً ، مَعَ اتَّفَاقِهِمْ عَلَى تَقَدُّمِ الجُزْءِ عَلَى الكُلِّ في الوُجُودَيْنِ ؛ أَعْني : الوُجُودَ الذهني وَالوُجُودَ الخَارِجِيَّ.

وَالثَّانِي : أَنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنْ يُفْهَمَ الجُزْءُ مَرَّتَيْنِ؛ مَرَّةٌ فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ وَأُخْرَى مُنْفَرِداً ، وَالوُجْدَانُ يُكَذِّبُه.

وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : الأَحْسَنُ مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ مِنْ أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ التَّضَمُّنِ فَهُمُ الجُزْءِ فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ، وَلَا شَكَ أَنَّهُ إِذَا فُهِمَ المَعْنَى فُهِمَتْ أَجْزَاؤُهُ مَعَهُ، فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ إِلَّا فَهُمْ وَاحِدٌ يُسَمَّى بِالقِيَاسِ إِلَى المَعْنَى بِتَمَامِهِ : دَلَالَةَ مُطَابَقَةٍ، وَبِالقِيَاسِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ : دَلَالَةَ تَضَمُّنٍ، وَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ انْتِقَالُ مِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى جُزْئِهِ، بِخِلَافِ دَلَالَةِ الالتزامِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهَا مِنَ الانْتِقَالِ مِنَ المَعْنَى إِلَى لَازِمِهِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الوَضْعِ أَصْلاً.

وَأُجِيبَ عَنِ الوَجْهِ الأَوَّلِ بِمَا قَالَهُ عَبْدُ الحَكِيمِ : مِنْ أَنَّ اتِّفَاقَهُمْ عَلَى تَقَدُّمِ الجُزْءِ فِي الوُجُودِ الذِّهْنِيِّ إِنَّمَا هُوَ مِنْ حَيْثُ فَهُم الجُزْءِ فِي ذَاتِهِ، وَهُوَ لَا يُنَافِي تَقَدُّمَ الكُلِّ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ فَهْمُهُ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ، فَيَكُونُ فَهُمُ الجُزْءِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ مُتَأخّراً عَنْ فَهْمِ الكُلِّ مِنْهُ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الجُزْءُ فِي ذَاتِهِ مُتَقَدِّماً عَلَى الكُلِّ.

أقوال : قوله : (وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ) هو العلامة العَدَوِيُّ.
For its part: 'tadamun', and what is entailed is iltizam if by reason it is obligated. Al-Malawi illustrated it with: when you see a figure from afar and doubt whether it is an animal or not, and it is said to you: it is a human, then you understand that it is an animal, and you do not pay attention to it being rational, although the meaning in its entirety first occurs in the mind. He said: this is an example in which the transition from the meaning of the word to its part appears, since there is no obstacle to understanding the meaning in summary, then the mind transitions to its part. And it is discussed from two aspects: First: it entails the priority of the whole over the part mentally, despite their agreement on the priority of the part over the whole in both modes of existence: I mean mental existence and external existence. Second: it entails that the part is understood twice: once within the whole and another separately, and experience disproves it. Therefore, some said: the better [view] is that which some of the investigators (muhaqqiqun) arrived at: that signification of implication (dalalat al-tadamun) is the understanding of the part within the whole, and there is no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood with it. So there is only one understanding, called in relation to the meaning in its entirety 'signification of correspondence' (moutabaqa), and in relation to its part 'signification of implication' (tadamun), and there is no transition from the meaning to its part, unlike signification of entailment (iltizam), for in it there must be a transition from the meaning to its necessary concomitant, because the necessary concomitant has no role in the original stipulation. And the first objection was answered by what Abd al-Hakim said: that their agreement on the priority of the part in mental existence is only from the aspect of understanding the part in its essence, and it does not contradict the priority of the whole over it from the aspect of its understanding from the word. Thus, the understanding of the part from the word is posterior to the understanding of the whole from it, even though the part in its essence is prior to the whole. Sayings: His statement: 'And therefore some said' is the scholar al-Adawi.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the types of linguistic signification (dalalat), focusing on tadamun (implication by inclusion) and the objections raised against a particular understanding of it. The passage begins with a matn line from al-Sullam: a word's signification of its part is called tadamun, and what is entailed by the meaning (a necessary concomitant) is called iltizam, provided the entailment is rational. The commentator al-Bajuri then relates an illustration from al-Malawi: if you see a distant shape and doubt whether it is an animal, and someone tells you it is a human (insan), you immediately understand that it is an animal (hayawan) without necessarily attending to the other part 'rational' (natiq). This example is meant to show a transition from the whole meaning to its part. Two objections follow. First, this view implies the whole is mentally prior to the part, but logicians agree that the part is prior to the whole in both mental and external existence. Second, it implies the part is understood twice—once within the whole and once separately—but our experience (wijdan) contradicts this. In response, some scholars (muhaqqiqun) propose a refined view: tadamun is not a transition but simply the understanding of the part within the whole. There is only one act of understanding; relative to the whole meaning it is called mutabaqa (correspondence), relative to the part it is called tadamun. This avoids the double understanding. Additionally, Abd al-Hakim answers the first objection by distinguishing between the part's priority in its essence (bi dhatihi) and its priority in terms of understanding from the word. The part may be prior in essence, but when understood from the word, the whole is conceptually prior because we grasp the whole first and then analyze its parts. Thus the objections are refuted. The passage ends with an attribution of the refined view to al-Adawi. Throughout, the discussion uses Islamic logical vocabulary and examples from the concept of 'insan' (human), which is composed of 'hayawan' (animal) and 'natiq' (rational).

Key Terms

دلالة المطابقة
دلالة الالتزام
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الجزء

Checkpoint
According to the refined view in this passage, what is dalalat al-tadamun?
A) A transition from the whole meaning to its part.
B) Understanding the part within the whole without a separate transition.
C) The same as dalalat al-iltizam.
D) Only occurs when the part is external.

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-tadamun is a single act of understanding that encompasses both the whole meaning and its parts, considered from two perspectives, thereby avoiding the objections of priority and double understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'salat' in fiqh: it signifies a set of specific actions and conditions (e.g., takbir, ruku, sujud). Does 'salat' signify these parts by tadamun or iltizam? Explain your answer using the refined definition from this lesson.
?
Using the example from the passage, identify the whole (insan) and the parts (hayawan, natiq). Then test whether understanding 'insan' involves a transition from whole to part according to the refined view. Why or why not?

Why Define the Compound Before the Simple?
Author justifies why the compound definition precedes the simple definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why positive definitions (ījāb) take precedence over negative definitions (salb) in Islamic logic texts, using the example of mufrad and murakkab.

Core Idea
Positive definitions are nobler than negative ones, so the compound is defined before the simple.

This passage addresses a methodological puzzle: in Mantiq textbooks, the compound term (murakkab) is often defined before the simple term (mufrad), even though the simple is a part of the compound. The commentator provides two justifications: first, the compound is defined positively (e.g., 'what indicates part of its meaning'), while the simple is defined negatively (e.g., 'what does not indicate part of its meaning'), and affirmation is superior to negation. Second, negation of something requires prior conceptualization of that thing. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objector's argument: the simple (mufrad) is a part, the compound (murakkab) is the whole, and parts logically precede wholes, so the simple should be defined first.
This objection relies on the principle that parts are prior to their wholes.
2
Extract the first justification
The compound is defined by affirmation (ījāb, stating what it is), while the simple is defined by negation (salb, stating what it is not). Affirmation is considered nobler (ashraf) than negation, so the compound definition takes priority.
This reflects a value judgment in Islamic logic: positive knowledge is superior to negative knowledge.
3
Extract the second justification
Negation of something is inconceivable except after that thing has been conceptualized (ta'aqqul). To understand 'the simple does not indicate part of its meaning', you must first understand what it means for a term to indicate part of its meaning—which is exactly the compound definition.
This is a logical priority: the positive concept is needed before the negative one can be grasped.
4
Consider the alternative view
Some scholars define the simple first, relying on the idea that non-existence (adam) precedes existence (wujud). The author notes that both approaches have their subtle points (nikāt) and are not mutually exclusive.
This shows awareness of different methodological preferences.
5
Synthesize the rationale
The author's order is justified because positive definitions are more informative and conceptually prior. Even though parts precede wholes ontologically, definitions can follow pedagogical or epistemic priority.
This highlights that order in textbooks does not always mirror ontological order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قِيلَ : كَانَ المُنَاسِبُ لِلمُصَنِّفِ تَقْدِيمَ تَعْرِيفِ المُفْرَدِ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ المُرَكَّبِ؛ لِأَنَّ المُفْرَدَ جُزْءٌ وَالمُرَكَّبَ كُلٌّ، وَالجُزْءُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى الكُلِّ . أُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ المُرَكَّبِ بِالإِيجَابِ وَتَعْرِيفِ المُفْرَدِ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَالإِيجَابُ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ السَّلْبِ، وَأَيْضاً : لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ سَلْبُ شَيْءٍ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقلِه. وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَدَّمَ تَعْرِيفَ المُفْرَدِ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ المُرَكَّبِ؛ نَظَراً لِسَبْقِ العَدَمِ عَلَى الوُجُودِ، وَالنِّكَاتُ لَا تَتَزَاحَمُ.
If it is said: The appropriate thing for the author would have been to present the definition of the simple before the definition of the compound, because the simple is a part and the compound is a whole, and the part precedes the whole. The answer is: The definition of the compound is by affirmation and the definition of the simple is by negation, and affirmation is nobler than negation. Also, negation of something is inconceivable except after its conceptualization. And some have presented the definition of the simple before the definition of the compound, looking to the precedence of non-existence over existence, but subtle points are not mutually exclusive.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural question: why does the author define the compound (murakkab) before the simple (mufrad)? The objection is that the simple is a part of the compound, so logically it should be defined first. The response gives two justifications. First, the compound is defined positively (e.g., 'what indicates part of its meaning'), while the simple is defined negatively (e.g., 'what does not indicate part of its meaning'). Since affirmation is nobler than negation, it is fitting to begin with the positive definition. Second, negation presupposes knowledge of the thing negated; to understand what it means for the simple not to indicate part of its meaning, one must first understand what it means for a compound to do so. Some scholars have nevertheless preferred to start with the simple, viewing non-existence as prior to existence, but the author notes that such subtle points do not conflict—each approach has its merit.

Key Terms
مُفْرَد (mufrad): A simple term whose part does not indicate part of its meaning independently. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
مُرَكَّب (murakkab): A compound term whose part indicates part of its meaning. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
إِيجَاب (ījāb): Affirmation, used here to mean a positive definition that states what something is. [Note: In logic, often refers to affirmative proposition; here used in a broader sense of positive statement.]
سَلْب (salb): Negation, used here to mean a negative definition that states what something is not. [Note: In logic, often refers to negative proposition; here used in a broader sense of negative statement.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author define the compound before the simple?
A) Because the compound is more important than the simple.
B) Because the compound is defined positively, which is nobler than the negative definition of the simple.
C) Because the simple is a part of the compound and the part is known first.
D) Because the author made a mistake.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, positive definitions (ījāb) take priority over negative definitions (salb), which explains why the compound term (murakkab) is defined before the simple (mufrad).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Open a Mantiq textbook (e.g., al-Sullam al-Munawraq) and identify the definitions of mufrad and murakkab. Determine which is positive and which is negative. Write a short explanation of why the order you found matches or differs from the reasoning in this passage.
?
Task 2: Suppose you are designing a beginner's Mantiq curriculum. Would you define 'mufrad' or 'murakkab' first? Justify your choice using at least one of the two justifications given in the passage.

The Scope of 'Al-Kull': Literal and Metaphorical Usage
Definition of al-kull and its literal/metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the term 'kull' (the whole) in a given proposition is used literally (referring to all individuals) or metaphorically (referring to some), and correctly interpret its scope in affirmative and negative judgments.

Core Idea
Al-kull is a judgment upon the aggregate — but it can refer to all or some, depending on context.

In Islamic logic, the term 'al-kull' (the whole) is not always straightforward. While it might seem to always mean 'every individual', the tradition distinguishes between a literal usage (all individuals collectively) and a metaphorical usage (some individuals collectively). This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting Quranic verses, hadith, and legal rulings. In this lesson, we will unpack the definition from al-Bajuri's commentary and learn to apply this tool to real Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'al-kull'
Read the source definition: 'al-kull is our judgment upon the aggregate.' Note that the author clarifies that originally the term refers to the subject (the aggregate), and calling the judgment 'kull' is a metonymy that became the technical reality.
The key is that 'kull' is a judgment, not necessarily the set itself.
2
Distinguish literal vs metaphorical usage
The commentary explains that 'upon the aggregate' can mean either all individuals collectively (literal) or some individuals collectively (metaphorical). Study the two examples: the Quranic verse about eight angels (literal) and the statement 'The people of al-Azhar are scholars' (metaphorical).
Context determines which meaning is intended.
3
Interpret negative judgments with 'kull'
The passage notes that in negative judgments, e.g., 'I did not give the whole ten,' the negation applies to the aggregate, not to each individual. This means it is compatible with giving some of the ten.
This prevents misinterpretation: negation of 'kull' does not imply total absence.
4
Apply the concept to a new Islamic example
Take a statement like 'The believers are victorious' from the Quran. Consider whether it is literal (every believer) or metaphorical (some believers) based on context. Then test with a negative: 'Allah does not guide the unjust' — does it mean no unjust person is ever guided? Use the distinction to clarify.
These distinctions are frequently used in tafsir and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الكُلِّ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْءِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ
الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ كَـ: «كُلِّ ذَاكَ لَيْسَ ذَا وُقُوعِ»
قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْل فِي الكُلِّ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْءِ وَالجُزْنِيَّةِ) وَشَارَكَ الأَوَّلَيْنِ فِي البَدَاءَةِ بِـ «الكَافِ : الكُلّيُّ ، وَالأَخِيرَيْنِ فِي البَدَاءَةِ بـ «الجيم»: «الجُزْنِيُّ ، فَجُمْلَةُ الأَلْفَاظِ سِتَّةٌ : ثَلَاثَةٌ مَبْدُقَةٌ بِالكَافِ، وَثَلَاثَةٌ مَبْدُقَةٌ بِالجِيمِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (الكُلُ : حُكْمُنَا ... إلخ) الكُلُّ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ هُوَ : المَوْضُوعُ الَّذِي هُوَ المَجْمُوعُ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ، فَتَسْمِيَةُ الحُكْمِ : «كُلًّا مِنْ بَابِ تَسْمِيَةِ الشَّيْء بِاسْمِ مُتَعَلَّقِهِ، لَكِنْ هَذَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَصْلِ، وَإِلَّا فَقَدْ صَارَ حَقِيقَةٌ اصْطِلَاحِيَّةٌ ، كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ) أَيْ: عَلَى الأَفْرَادِ المُجْتَمِعِ جَمِيعِهَا ، كَمَا هُوَ الحَقِيقَةُ فِي إِطْلَاقِ المَجْمُوعِ، أَوْ عَلَى بَعْضِ الأَفْرَادِ المُجْتَمِعَةِ كَمَا هُوَ المَجَازُ فِيهِ . فَالأَوَّلُ ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَيَحْمِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ ثَمَانِيَةٌ﴾ [الحاقة : ١٧] .
وَالثَّانِي ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ أَهْلُ الأَزْهَرِ عُلَمَاءُ.
قَولُهُ : (كَمَا هُوَ الحَقِيقَةُ فِي إِطْلَاقِ المَجْمُوع ... إلخ) هَذَا حُكْمُ الكُلِّ فِي الإِيجَابِ، أَمَّا فِي النَّفْيِ فَهُوَ النَّفْيُ عَنِ المَجْمُوعِ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: مَا أَعْطَيْتُ كُلَّ العَشْرَةِ، فَلَا يُنَافِي الثُّبُوتَ فِي البَعْضِ، ذَكَرَهُ شَيْخُنَا العَدَوِيُّ. اهـ
A chapter on the whole, the universal, the part, and the particular. The whole: our judgment upon the aggregate, like: 'All of that was not taking place.' His saying: (A chapter on the whole, the universal, the part, and the particular): and the first two share in beginning with 'kaf': the universal, and the last two in beginning with 'jim': the particular. So the total number of words is six: three beginning with 'kaf' and three beginning with 'jim'. His saying: (The whole: our judgment... etc.) The whole in reality is the subject which is the aggregate judged upon. So naming the judgment 'whole' is from the category of naming something by the name of its related entity. But this is with consideration of the original meaning; otherwise it has become a technical reality, as al-Mallawi mentioned in his larger [commentary]. His saying: (Upon the aggregate) i.e., upon all the individuals collectively, as is the reality in its usage of the aggregate, or upon some individuals collectively, as is the metaphorical usage in it. The first: as in His saying, exalted: 'And the angels will bear the throne of your Lord above them that Day, eight [angels]' [69:17]. The second: as in your saying: 'The people of al-Azhar are scholars.' His saying: (As it is the reality in the usage of the aggregate... etc.) This is the ruling of the whole in affirmative [judgments]. As for negative [judgments], it is negation of the aggregate, like our saying: 'I did not give the whole ten,' so it does not contradict affirmation of some. Our teacher al-`Adawi mentioned it. End.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-kull' (the whole) in the context of logical predication. It begins by stating the chapter's scope: the whole, the universal, the part, and the particular. The author notes a mnemonic: the first two terms start with 'kaf' (كلي and كل) and the last two with 'jim' (جزء and جزئي). The core definition is that 'al-kull' is our judgment upon the aggregate (المجموع). The commentary clarifies that originally the whole refers to the subject of the judgment (the aggregate), and calling the judgment 'whole' is a metonymy, but it has become a technical term. The phrase 'upon the aggregate' is then expanded: it can mean all individuals collectively (literal usage) or some individuals collectively (metaphorical usage). Two examples are given: the Quranic verse about angels bearing the throne (where 'eight' refers to all eight collectively) and the statement 'The people of al-Azhar are scholars' (which likely means some, not all, are scholars). Finally, the passage distinguishes between affirmative and negative judgments: in negation, 'not all' (e.g., 'I did not give the whole ten') does not rule out giving some—it only negates the entire aggregate. This clarifies that the negative judgment about the whole is compatible with partial affirmation.

المجموع
الحكم
الكلية

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-kull' in Islamic logic has both a literal meaning (all individuals) and a metaphorical meaning (some individuals), and its interpretation depends on context and the type of judgment (affirmative or negative).

What Is Mantiq?

Universal and Particular Judgments
Author defines universal and particular judgments with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish universal and particular judgments in logical propositions using Quranic and legal examples.

Core Idea
Universal judgment covers every individual; particular covers some.

In logic, a judgment (ḥukm) is classified by its scope: universal (kulliyya) if it applies to every individual of the subject, or particular (juz'iyya) if it applies to some. This distinction is essential for constructing valid syllogisms and precise definitions in Islamic sciences. The commentator uses a Quranic verse and the shahada as examples to anchor the concept in sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the judgment in the proposition
Find the part of the proposition that asserts or denies something about the subject. This is the ḥukm. In 'Every soul shall taste death', the judgment is 'taste death' applied to 'every soul'.
2
Identify the quantifier
Check if the subject is preceded by 'every' (kull) or 'some' (baʿḍ). For universal, look for words like 'kull', 'jamīʿ', or the absence of a quantifier in universal negatives. For particular, look for 'baʿḍ' or implicit limitation.
The particle 'lām' in 'li-kulli fard' means 'upon', not 'for'.
3
Determine the scope
If the judgment holds for every single individual of the subject, it is universal. If it holds for at least one (or some), it is particular. The passage clarifies that 'some' can be one or more.
Example from the passage: 'Some animals are human' is particular.
4
Apply the label to the proposition
Just as the judgment is called universal or particular, the entire proposition (qaḍiyya) is also called universal or particular. For instance, the proposition 'Every soul shall taste death' is a universal proposition.
This dual usage is clarified by the commentator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ حُكِمَا فَإِنَّهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ» قَدْ عُلِمَا . اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى، وَهِيَ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «حُكِمَا»، وَذَلِكَ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : ﴿ كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ ﴾ . وَكَمَا فِي الكَلِمَةِ المُشَرَّفَةِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنَّهُ : «كُلِّيَّةٌ» ... إلخ) الضَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِلْحُكْمِ المَفْهُومِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : «حُكِمَا»، فَهُوَ عَلَى حَدِّ قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿ اعْدِلُواْ هُوَ أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى﴾. وَكَمَا يُسَمَّى الحُكْمُ المَذْكُورُ : «كُلِّيَّةٌ»، تُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ : كُلِّيَّةٌ. وَالحُكْمُ لِلْبَعْضِ هُوَ : «الجُزْئِيَّةُ» وَالجُزْءُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ جَلِيَّةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحُكْمُ لِلْبَعْضِ ... إلخ) «اللَّامُ» فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى كَالَّذِي قَبْلَهُ، وَذَلِكَ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانُ، وَلَا فَرْقَ فِي ذَلِكَ البَعْضِ بَيْنَ أَنْ يَكُونَ وَاحِداً أَوْ أَكْثَرَ. قَوْلُهُ : (هُوَ : «الجُزْئِيَّةُ») وَكَمَا يُسَمَّى الحُكْمُ المَذْكُورُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ، تُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَالجُزْءُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ جَلِيَّةٌ) أَيْ: وَاضِحَةٌ.
And whenever the judgment is upon every individual, then indeed it is known as 'universal'. The 'lam' in it means 'upon', and it is attached to His statement: 'judged', and that is like His statement: 'Every soul shall taste death'. And like the blessed word, based on its being a universal negative. His statement: ('For indeed it is universal') the pronoun refers to the judgment understood from His statement 'judged', so it is on the order of His statement: 'Be just, that is nearer to piety'. And just as the mentioned judgment is called 'universal', the proposition containing it is called 'universal'. And the judgment for some is 'particular', and the meaning of 'part' is clear. His statement: ('And the judgment for some...') the 'lam' in it means 'upon', like the previous, and that is as in your saying: 'Some animals are human', and there is no difference in that some between being one or more. His statement: ('It is particular') and just as the mentioned judgment is called 'particular', the proposition containing it is called 'particular'. His statement: ('And the meaning of part is clear') i.e. obvious.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line defining universal and particular judgments in logic. The commentator begins by explaining the word 'lam' in the phrase 'li-kulli fard' (upon every individual), clarifying that it means 'upon' (على) and not 'for'. This is supported by the Quranic example 'Every soul shall taste death' and the shahada, which is considered a universal negative judgment. The commentator then notes that the pronoun in 'innahu' (it) refers to the judgment, so the phrase 'kulliyya' (universal) is a predicate for the judgment, similar to the Quranic construction. Furthermore, just as the judgment itself is called universal, the proposition containing that judgment is also called universal. The definition of a particular judgment follows: when the judgment applies to some individuals (e.g., 'Some animals are human'), it is called 'juz'iyya' (particular). The 'lam' again means 'upon'. There is no difference between 'some' referring to one or more individuals. Finally, the commentator explains that the word 'juz'' (part) is inherently clear in meaning, emphasizing that its definition is obvious and requires no further explanation. The passage thus provides a precise definition of two key logical terms: universal and particular judgments, with attention to linguistic nuances.

Universal and Particular Judgments
Commentary defines universal and particular judgments and propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal and particular judgments and identify how the proposition is named after the judgment.

Core Idea
A judgment's scope defines the proposition: universal for every individual, particular for some.

In logic, propositions are classified based on the scope of the judgment they contain. The commentary clarifies that 'universal' and 'particular' primarily refer to the judgment (hukm), and derivatively to the proposition (qadiyya). Understanding this distinction is fundamental for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
Find in the text where al-Bajuri states that a judgment covering every individual is called 'kulliyya' and a judgment covering some individuals is called 'juz'iyya'. Note that the propositions containing these judgments are also named accordingly.
2
Identify the primary referent
Observe that the judgment itself is primary; the proposition is named after it. The commentator uses an analogy with the Quranic verse 'Be just, that is nearer to piety' where the pronoun 'that' refers to the concept of justice, not the act.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Take a proposition like 'Every intoxicant is haram'. The judgment applies to each individual intoxicant, so it is universal. Conversely, 'Some animals are permissible to eat' is particular because it covers only some animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
وحيثما لكل فرد حكما فإنه: «كلية» قد قدعلما
الباجوري
وإن كان خارجاً منه بحسب الإرادة؛ لأنه يجب على المتكلم بالكلمة المذكورة أن يريد بالمنفي غير الذات العلية من الآلهة، وإلا لزم الكفر، والعياذ بالله تعالى.
قوله: (فإنه: «كلية» ... إلخ) الضمير عائد للحكم المفهوم من قوله: «حكما»، فهو على حد قوله تعالى: ﴿اعدلوا هو أقرب للتقوى﴾ [المائدة: 8].
وكما يسمى الحكم المذكور: «كلية»، تسمى القضية المشتملة عليه: كلية.
قوله: (والحكم للبعض ... إلخ) «اللام» فيه بمعنى: على كالذي قبله، وذلك كما في قولك: بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا فرق في ذلك البعض بين أن يكون واحداً أو أكثر. قوله: (هو: «الجزئية») وكما يسمى الحكم المذكور: جزئية)، تسمى القضية المشتملة عليه: جزئية.
قوله: (والجزء معرفته جلية) أي: واضحة.
وإنما وصف المعرفة بكونها جلية، مع أنه لا يتصف بذلك إلا معنى الجزء - وهو: ما تركبت منه ومن غيره كلٌّ؛ محسوساً كان كـ: السمار بالنسبة للحصير، أو معقولا كـ: الحيوان بالنسبة للإنسان؛ إذ المعرفة هي الإدراك، ولا معنى لاتصافه بذلك -؛ مبالغة في ظهور معنى الجزء.
وقد يقال: المراد: أنها جلية من حيث متعلقها.
ويمكن أن يقال: مراده بكونها جلية: حصولها من غير احتياج إلى فكر وتأمل.
الشرشيمي: قوله: (والجزء ... إلخ) لم يذكر الكل الذي تركيب من الأجزاء، وإنما ذكر الكل بمعنى المجموع.
الشرشيمي: قوله: (مبالغة) أي: وصفها نفسها مبالغة.
الشرشيمي: وقوله: (من حيث متعلقها) أي: إن وصفها لا لذاتها، بل لذات متعلقها، فهو مغاير للجواب الأول.
Chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. 'And wherever a judgment is made for every individual, it is 'universal' as has been known.' al-Bajuri: And if it is external from it by way of intention, because the speaker of the mentioned word must intend by the negated [something] other than the supreme essence from among the gods, otherwise disbelief would follow, and refuge is with Allah Almighty. His statement: '(Then it is: 'universal' ... etc.)' The pronoun refers back to the judgment understood from his saying 'judged', so it is like His statement: 'Be just, that is nearer to piety' [5:8]. And just as the mentioned judgment is called 'universal', the proposition that includes it is called universal. His statement: 'And the judgment for some ... etc.' The 'lam' in it has the meaning of 'upon' like the one before it, as in your saying: 'Some animals are humans', and there is no difference in that 'some' between being one or more. His statement: 'It is the 'particular'. And just as the mentioned judgment is called 'particular', the proposition that includes it is called particular. His statement: 'And the part, its knowledge is clear' i.e., obvious. He only described the knowledge as being clear, although nothing is described with that except the meaning of 'part' — which is: that from which and from other than it a whole is composed, whether sensible like the palm-fiber in relation to the mat, or intelligible like 'animal' in relation to 'human'; because knowledge is perception, and there is no meaning to its being described with that — as an exaggeration in the obviousness of the meaning of part. And it is said: The intended meaning is that it is clear from the perspective of its object. And it can be said: His intention in its being clear is its attainment without need for thought and reflection. al-Sharshimi: His statement: ('And the part ... etc.') He did not mention the whole that is composed of parts, but rather mentioned the whole in the sense of the aggregate. al-Sharshimi: His statement: ('exaggeration') i.e., describing the knowledge itself is an exaggeration. al-Sharshimi: And his statement: ('from the perspective of its object') i.e., its description is not for itself, but for the essence of its object, so it is different from the first answer.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (al-Sullam) that states: 'And wherever a judgment is made for every individual, it is universal.' The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by clarifying a potential issue: if the judgment in a proposition like 'No god is worthy of worship except Allah' negates attributes that are external to the essence (like mercy or anger), the speaker must intend negation of other than the supreme essence, not the attributes of the essence itself. He then explains that the pronoun in 'fainnahu' refers to the judgment, not the individual. The judgment that covers every individual is called 'universal', and the proposition containing it is also called universal. Conversely, a judgment about some individuals (e.g., 'Some animals are humans') is called 'particular', and its proposition is particular. The discussion then moves to the term 'part' (juz'). The text states that knowledge of the part is 'clear' (jaliyya). The commentator explains that this is an exaggeration: the part itself is not knowledge, but the knowledge of it is obvious. The part is defined as that from which, together with something else, a whole is composed—either sensible (like palm-fiber for a mat) or intelligible (like animal for human). The comment also offers interpretations for why it is called clear: either due to its object being clear, or because such knowledge arises without need for reflection. Marginal notes by al-Sharshimi refine these points: he notes that the whole here means the aggregate, not the composite whole, and clarifies that describing knowledge as clear is an exaggeration, and that the second interpretation (from the perspective of its object) differs from the first.

Key Terms
حكم
كلية
جزئية
قضية

Key Takeaway
A judgment is universal if it covers every individual of the subject, and particular if it covers only some; the proposition is named after the judgment.

The Concept of 'Part' (Juz) in Logic
Commentary explains the concept of 'part' (juz) with examples and refinements by the super-commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'juz' and distinguish between sensible and intelligible parts, and explain why knowledge of the part is described as 'clear'.

Core Idea
A part is that from which a whole is composed, known clearly without need for reflection.

The term 'juz' (part) is fundamental in logic for analyzing definitions and compositions. Al-Bajuri's commentary and al-Sharshimi's marginal notes clarify that a part can be sensible or intelligible, and that our knowledge of a part is described as 'clear' as an exaggeration. This concept appears in many Islamic sciences when discussing analytical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of 'juz'
Find in the text where al-Bajuri defines 'juz': it is that from which, together with something else, a whole is composed. Note the two types: sensible (e.g., palm-fiber in a mat) and intelligible (e.g., animal in human).
2
Understand the description 'clear'
Al-Bajuri explains that knowledge of the part is called 'jaliyya' (clear) as an exaggeration (mubalagha). The part itself is not knowledge, but the knowledge of it is obvious. Two interpretations: either the object of knowledge is clear, or the knowledge arises without reflection.
3
Analyze al-Sharshimi's refinement
Al-Sharshimi notes that al-Bajuri did not mention the whole composed of parts but used 'whole' in the sense of aggregate. This clarifies that the part-whole relation here is not about a composite structure but about constituents forming an aggregate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
وحيثما لكل فرد حكما فإنه: «كلية» قد قدعلما
الباجوري
وإن كان خارجاً منه بحسب الإرادة؛ لأنه يجب على المتكلم بالكلمة المذكورة أن يريد بالمنفي غير الذات العلية من الآلهة، وإلا لزم الكفر، والعياذ بالله تعالى.
قوله: (فإنه: «كلية» ... إلخ) الضمير عائد للحكم المفهوم من قوله: «حكما»، فهو على حد قوله تعالى: ﴿اعدلوا هو أقرب للتقوى﴾ [المائدة: 8].
وكما يسمى الحكم المذكور: «كلية»، تسمى القضية المشتملة عليه: كلية.
قوله: (والحكم للبعض ... إلخ) «اللام» فيه بمعنى: على كالذي قبله، وذلك كما في قولك: بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا فرق في ذلك البعض بين أن يكون واحداً أو أكثر. قوله: (هو: «الجزئية») وكما يسمى الحكم المذكور: جزئية)، تسمى القضية المشتملة عليه: جزئية.
قوله: (والجزء معرفته جلية) أي: واضحة.
وإنما وصف المعرفة بكونها جلية، مع أنه لا يتصف بذلك إلا معنى الجزء - وهو: ما تركبت منه ومن غيره كلٌّ؛ محسوساً كان كـ: السمار بالنسبة للحصير، أو معقولا كـ: الحيوان بالنسبة للإنسان؛ إذ المعرفة هي الإدراك، ولا معنى لاتصافه بذلك -؛ مبالغة في ظهور معنى الجزء.
وقد يقال: المراد: أنها جلية من حيث متعلقها.
ويمكن أن يقال: مراده بكونها جلية: حصولها من غير احتياج إلى فكر وتأمل.
الشرشيمي: قوله: (والجزء ... إلخ) لم يذكر الكل الذي تركيب من الأجزاء، وإنما ذكر الكل بمعنى المجموع.
الشرشيمي: قوله: (مبالغة) أي: وصفها نفسها مبالغة.
الشرشيمي: وقوله: (من حيث متعلقها) أي: إن وصفها لا لذاتها، بل لذات متعلقها، فهو مغاير للجواب الأول.
Chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. 'And wherever a judgment is made for every individual, it is 'universal' as has been known.' al-Bajuri: And if it is external from it by way of intention, because the speaker of the mentioned word must intend by the negated [something] other than the supreme essence from among the gods, otherwise disbelief would follow, and refuge is with Allah Almighty. His statement: '(Then it is: 'universal' ... etc.)' The pronoun refers back to the judgment understood from his saying 'judged', so it is like His statement: 'Be just, that is nearer to piety' [5:8]. And just as the mentioned judgment is called 'universal', the proposition that includes it is called universal. His statement: 'And the judgment for some ... etc.' The 'lam' in it has the meaning of 'upon' like the one before it, as in your saying: 'Some animals are humans', and there is no difference in that 'some' between being one or more. His statement: 'It is the 'particular'. And just as the mentioned judgment is called 'particular', the proposition that includes it is called particular. His statement: 'And the part, its knowledge is clear' i.e., obvious. He only described the knowledge as being clear, although nothing is described with that except the meaning of 'part' — which is: that from which and from other than it a whole is composed, whether sensible like the palm-fiber in relation to the mat, or intelligible like 'animal' in relation to 'human'; because knowledge is perception, and there is no meaning to its being described with that — as an exaggeration in the obviousness of the meaning of part. And it is said: The intended meaning is that it is clear from the perspective of its object. And it can be said: His intention in its being clear is its attainment without need for thought and reflection. al-Sharshimi: His statement: ('And the part ... etc.') He did not mention the whole that is composed of parts, but rather mentioned the whole in the sense of the aggregate. al-Sharshimi: His statement: ('exaggeration') i.e., describing the knowledge itself is an exaggeration. al-Sharshimi: And his statement: ('from the perspective of its object') i.e., its description is not for itself, but for the essence of its object, so it is different from the first answer.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (al-Sullam) that states: 'And wherever a judgment is made for every individual, it is universal.' The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by clarifying a potential issue: if the judgment in a proposition like 'No god is worthy of worship except Allah' negates attributes that are external to the essence (like mercy or anger), the speaker must intend negation of other than the supreme essence, not the attributes of the essence itself. He then explains that the pronoun in 'fainnahu' refers to the judgment, not the individual. The judgment that covers every individual is called 'universal', and the proposition containing it is also called universal. Conversely, a judgment about some individuals (e.g., 'Some animals are humans') is called 'particular', and its proposition is particular. The discussion then moves to the term 'part' (juz'). The text states that knowledge of the part is 'clear' (jaliyya). The commentator explains that this is an exaggeration: the part itself is not knowledge, but the knowledge of it is obvious. The part is defined as that from which, together with something else, a whole is composed—either sensible (like palm-fiber for a mat) or intelligible (like animal for human). The comment also offers interpretations for why it is called clear: either due to its object being clear, or because such knowledge arises without need for reflection. Marginal notes by al-Sharshimi refine these points: he notes that the whole here means the aggregate, not the composite whole, and clarifies that describing knowledge as clear is an exaggeration, and that the second interpretation (from the perspective of its object) differs from the first.

Key Terms
جزء
كلية
جزئية
محسوس
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'juz' (part)?
A) That which is known without reflection
B) That from which and from other than it a whole is composed
C) A sensible object like palm-fiber
D) An intelligible concept like animal

Key Takeaway
A part (juz) is that which, together with something else, composes a whole, and its knowledge is clear either because its object is clear or because it is grasped without reflection.

The Three Types of Definition: Hadd, Rasm, and Lafzi
Author classifies definitions into three types and explains the linguistic origin of hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as hadd, rasm, or lafzi and explain the linguistic basis of hadd as 'prevention'.

Core Idea
Definitions protect meaning: hadd prevents intrusion, rasm sketches properties, lafzi clarifies synonyms.

This lesson introduces the threefold classification of definitions (mu'arrafat) in Islamic logic. You will learn how each type serves a distinct function: the essential definition (hadd) captures the essence, the descriptive definition (rasm) lists accidental properties, and the verbal definition (lafzi) clarifies via synonymy. The passage also resolves a subtle objection about self-reference in the term 'rasm'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the first line of the verse: 'A definition is divided into three: hadd, and rasm, and lafzi.' Note that the term 'mu'arraf' (definition) is being classified.
2
Understand the linguistic meaning of hadd
The author explains that 'hadd' in language means 'prevention'. It prevents non-instances from entering the definition and prevents instances from leaving. This ensures the definition is neither too broad nor too narrow.
Think of a fence that keeps the correct individuals inside and outsiders out.
3
Resolve the objection about rasm
An objection is raised: if 'rasm' is itself a type of definition, then saying 'a definition is rasm' would be attributing a thing to itself. The author answers that 'rasm' here refers to its linguistic meaning ('trace'), not the technical term, so no self-reference occurs.
This shows the importance of distinguishing technical from ordinary language.
4
Recognize the role of lafzi definition
The verbal definition (lafzi) is attributed to 'lafz' (word). It clarifies a term by a more familiar expression. The author notes that it is only needed when the meaning of the word is known but its application to the thing is unknown.
Example: 'al-insan' (human) means 'al-bashar' (mankind).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُعَرِّفٌ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ قُسِمْ: حَدٌ، وَرَسْمِيٌّ، وَلَفْظِيٌّ عُلِمْ
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الحَدَّ فِي اللُّغَةِ : المَنْعُ ; أُطْلِقَ عَلَى مَا يَأْتِي ; لِمَنْعِهِ مِنْ دُخُولِ أَفْرَادِ غَيْرِ المُعَرَّفِ فِيهِ، وَمِنْ خُرُوجِ أَفْرَادِهِ مِنْهُ.
فَإِنْ قِيلَ : يَلْزَمُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ نِسْبَةُ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ ; لِأَنَّهُ مَنْسُوبٌ لِلرَّسْمِ الَّذِي هُوَ هُوَ . أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ مَنْسُوبٌ لِلرَّسْمِ اللُّغَوِيّ ( وَهُوَ : الأَثَرُ)، لا المُصْطَلَحِ عَلَيْهِ حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ مَا ذُكِرَ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَفْظِيٌّ) مَنْسُوبٌ لِلَّفْظِ ، مِنْ نِسْبَةِ الخَاصٌ لِلعَامّ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ لِزِيَادَةِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى التَّحْقِيقِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (عُلِمْ) تَكْمِلَةٌ لِلبَيْتِ، وَكَأَنَّهُ نَبَّهَ بِهِ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللَّفْظُ المُعَرَّفُ بِهِ عُلِمَ مَعْنَاهُ، وَإِنَّمَا جُهِلَ كَوْنُهُ مُسَمًّى بِاللَّفْظ الآخَرِ.
A definition is divided into three: hadd, and rasm, and lafzi (verbal). Know that hadd in language is 'prevention'; it is applied to what follows because it prevents individuals of other than the defined from entering it, and prevents its individuals from exiting it. If it is said: This necessitates attributing a thing to itself, because it is attributed to the rasm which is itself, the answer is: (it is) attributed to the linguistic rasm (which is 'trace'), not the technical one, so that what was mentioned would follow. His saying: (and lafzi) is attributed to lafz (word), as an attribution of the specific to the general, and you have known that there is no need for adding that upon verification. His saying: (is known) is a completion of the verse, and it seems he alerted by it that the word by which definition is made must have its meaning known, but only its being named by another word was unknown.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion of definitions (mu'arrafat) by presenting their threefold classification. The author states that every definition is one of three types: essential (hadd), descriptive (rasm), and verbal (lafzi). The term 'hadd' is taken from the literal meaning of prevention, because a proper definition prevents extraneous individuals from entering and prevents authentic individuals from leaving. A subtle objection arises: calling a definition 'rasm' seems to involve self-referential attribution, since 'rasm' is itself a type of definition. The author answers that the attribution is to the linguistic meaning of rasm (a trace), not the technical term. Finally, the author clarifies that a verbal definition (lafzi) is a definition by a synonym or clearer expression; its name comes from being related to 'lafz' (word). The phrase 'is known' in the verse indicates that the defining term must have a well-known meaning, but the listener may not know that it is a name for the thing in question.

Key Terms
مُعَرِّف
حَد
رَسْم
لَفْظِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that calling a definition 'rasm' is self-referential?
A) It is impossible for a definition to be self-referential.
B) The term 'rasm' is used in its linguistic sense of 'trace', not the technical term.
C) The objection is invalid because definitions are not self-referential.
D) The author did not address this objection.

Key Takeaway
Definitions are of three types: essential (hadd), descriptive (rasm), and verbal (lafzi); hadd prevents incorrect inclusion and exclusion, while rasm and lafzi serve other clarifying roles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What is a Proposition (Qadiyya)? Definition and Key Properties
Definition of qadiyya with morphological analysis and preview of its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qadiyya logically and identify its two properties: contradiction and simple conversion.

Core Idea
A proposition is the relation between subject and predicate, not the mental act of judgment.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of a proposition in logic. The author clarifies that qadiyya derives from qada' (judgment) but specifically refers to the linguistic relation, not the psychological act of assenting. This distinction is crucial for understanding later discussions on truth, contradiction, and inference. The passage also introduces two key properties that will be explored in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of qadiyya in the Arabic. What is the literal meaning and the technical meaning? Note the distinction between passive and active interpretation.
2
Distinguish the proposition from the judgment
The text says the proposition contains the relation (nisba) but not the mental act of assent (tasdiq). Why is this distinction important?
3
Examine the morphological derivation of the plural 'qadaya'
Walk through the four phonetic changes to understand the form from original 'qadayiy' to 'qadaya'.
4
List the two properties of propositions
What are the properties (ahkam) mentioned? Why does the author use the plural for two items?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ فِي القَضَايَا وَأَحْكَامِهَا
قَوْلُهُ : بَابٌ فِي القَضَايَا جَمْعُ: «قَضِيَّةٍ»، «فَعِيْلَةٍ بِمَعْنَى : مَفْعُولَةٍ»؛ أَيْ: مَقْضِيٌّ فِيهَا أَوْ بِمَعْنَى فَاعِلَةٍ» ؛ أَيْ: قَاضِيَّةٍ، عَلَى الإِسْنَادِ المَجَازِيِّ
وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهَا تَتَضَمَّنُ القَضَاءَ ؛ بِمَعْنَى : الحُكْمِ ، المُرَادُ بِهِ : النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، لا الإيقاع وَالانْتِزَاعَ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكَ الوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الوُقُوعِ ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَمْ تَتَضَمَّنُ ذَلِكَ؛ لأَنَّهُ قَائِمٌ بِنَفْسِ المُدْرِكِ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ وَزْنَ قَضَايَا) بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَصْلِ : «فَعَايِلَ» ؛ لِأَنَّ أَصْلَهَا : «قَضَايي» بِيَاءَيْنِ، فَأُبْدِلَتِ الأُوْلَى هَمْزَةٌ عَلَى القِيَاسِ فِي نَحْوِ : (صَحَائِفِ ، ورَسَائِلِ»، ثُمَّ فُتِحَتِ الهَمْزَةُ) لِلتَّخْفِيفِ، ثُمَّ قُلِبَتِ الثَّانِيَةُ أَلِفاً لِتَحَرُّكِهَا وَانْفِتَاحِ مَا قَبْلَهَا ، ثُمَّ قُلِبَتِ الهَمْزَةُ يَاءَ» ؛ لِوُقُوعِهَا بَيْنَ أَلِفَيْنِ، فَصَارَ : قَضَايَا» بَعْدَ أَرْبَعَةِ أَعْمَالٍ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَأَحْكَامِهَا) أَي : الَّتِي هِيَ : التَّناقُضُ، وَالعَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي . وَإِنَّمَا جَمَعَهَا المُصَنِّفُ؛ لأَنَّ الجَمْعَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الاثْنَيْنِ كَثِيراً خُصُوصاً فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ، أَوْ لأَنَّهُ اعْتَبَرَ الأَفْرَادَ .
Chapter on propositions and their properties. His statement: 'Chapter on propositions' plural of 'qadiyya' (feminine form) meaning 'something that is judged' or 'that judges' in a metaphorical attribution. It is so named because it contains a judgment (al-qada') meaning a ruling (hukm), intended as the relation between the two terms, not the affirmation or negation (i.e., the awareness of occurrence or non-occurrence) because it does not contain that; rather that exists in the mind of the perceiver, as will come. Know that the meter of 'qadaya' according to the original is 'fa'aila', because its original is 'qadayiy' with two ya's, then the first was changed to hamzah by analogy like 'saha'if' and 'rasa'il', then the hamzah was opened for ease, then the second was changed to alif because of its movement and openness of what precedes it, then the hamzah was changed to ya' because it occurs between two alifs, so it became 'qadaya' after four operations. And his statement: 'and their properties' meaning which are: contradiction (tanaqud) and simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi). The author pluralized them because the plural is often applied to two, especially in this craft, or because he considered the individuals.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on propositions (qadaya). The author first defines the term 'qadiyya' by its etymology: it can be either passive (something that is judged) or active (something that judges) in a metaphorical sense—the proposition itself does not judge, but the speaker uses it to judge. The name derives from its containing a judgment (qada'), which here means a relation (nisba) between its two terms (subject and predicate), not the psychological act of affirming or denying occurrence. The author distinguishes the proposition's content (the relation) from the mental act of assenting (tasdiq), which is performed by the mind. He then gives a detailed morphological analysis of the plural 'qadaya', showing its derivation from an original form 'qadayiy' through four phonetic changes. Finally, he states that the 'properties' (ahkam) of propositions are two: contradiction (tanaqud) and simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi). He explains why the author used the plural 'ahkam' even though there are only two: either because in this discipline the plural is often used for two, or because he considered each property individually.

Key Terms
قضية
القضاء
حكم
تناقض
عكس مستوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mental act of affirming or denying.
B) The relation between the subject and predicate.
C) The legal ruling.
D) The truth value of the proposition.

Key Takeaway
The proposition (qadiyya) is defined as a linguistic expression containing a relation between subject and predicate, not the mental act of judging, and its two chief properties are contradiction and simple conversion.

What Is Mantiq?

What is a Proposition? The Definition and Its Conditions
Author defines qadiyya/khabar with conditions of truth admission by essence, excluding insha' and non-propositional expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an expression is a proposition (qadiyya) by checking the two conditions: admitting truth and doing so by its essence.

Core Idea
A proposition is any expression that can be true or false by its very wording.

In logic, we need a precise definition of what counts as a proposition—the basic building block of arguments. The author gives a definition that includes the key phrase 'by its essence' (li-dhatihi). Commentator al-Bajuri unpacks each condition, showing what is included and excluded. This lesson walks through that definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
The definition uses 'ma' (what) as the genus covering all expressions. The differentia is 'admits truth by its essence'. Break down 'admits truth' and 'by its essence'.
The differentia has two components: possibility of truth and the cause being the essence.
2
Apply the condition 'admits truth'
Remove expressions that cannot be true at all: proper names like 'Zayd' and incomplete phrases like 'ghulam Zayd'. They lack a predicate to be true or false.
Single words and incomplete phrases are not propositions.
3
Apply the condition 'by its essence'
Remove performative utterances (insha') like commands ('Give me water'). They can be considered true indirectly (the command implies a desire), but that truth is not in the command itself.
Insha' includes commands, prohibitions, questions, etc.
4
Include propositions known to be true or false by other means
The condition 'by its essence' does NOT exclude propositions whose truth/falsehood is certain from revelation or reason. They still admit truth/falsehood by their essence; the certainty is from external evidence.
Examples: Allah's reports, self-evident truths, false claims known to be false.
5
Relate to falsehood
The definition mentions 'truth' but implies falsehood, since admitting truth necessarily entails admitting falsehood. Every proposition can be both true and false in potential.
If an expression can be true, it must be able to be false; otherwise it would not be a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ جَرَى بَيْنَهُمُ: «قَضِيَّةٌ» و«خَبَرًا». الباجوري: قَوْلُهُ : (مَا احْتَمَلَ ... إلخ) «مَا » : وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الشَّامِلِ لِجَمِيعِ الْأَلْفَاظِ ، فَهِيَ جِنْسٌ. وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ : «احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ : مَا لَمْ يَحْتَمِلُهُ كَـ زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرٍو، وَكَ: (غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ). وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «لِذَاتِهِ» : مَا احْتَمَلَهُ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِلَازِمِهِ كَـ الإِنْشَاءَاتِ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ وَالنَّهْيِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا فَإِنَّ قَوْلَكَ : «اسْقِنِي مَثَلاً وَإِنِ احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِمَا اسْتَلْزَمَهُ مِنْ قَوْلِكَ : «أَنَا طَالِبٌ لِلسُّقْيَا مِنْكَ». وَدَخَلَ بِهَذَا القَيْدِ : مَا قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ أَوْ بِكَذِبِهِ : فَالأَوَّلُ كَـ: أَخْبَارِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى، وَأَخْبَارِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ، وَالأَخْبَارِ المَعْلُومِ صِدْقَهَا بِضَرُورَةِ العَقْلِ ؛ نَحْوِ : «الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاِثْنَيْنِ». وَالثَّانِي كَـ أَخْبَارِ مُسَيْلِمَةَ الكَذَّابِ فِي دَعْوَاهُ النُّبُوَّةِ، وَالأَخْبَارِ المَعْلُومِ كَذِبَهَا بِضَرُورَةِ العَقْلِ؛ نَحْوِ : «الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الأَرْبَعَة»؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ، وَإِنْ قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ أَوْ كَذِبِهِ لِشَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَبِهَذَا تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ القَيْدَ المَذْكُورَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْإِخْرَاجِ وَالإِدْخَالِ. قَوْلُهُ : (الصِّدْقَ) أَيْ: وَالكَذِبَ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ لِلعِلْمِ بِهِ؛ إِذْ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلصِّدْقِ كَوْنُهُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلكَذِبِ.
What admits truth by its essence, between them [two] runs: 'proposition' and 'assertion'. Al-Bajuri: His saying: (what admits truth... etc.) 'what': refers to the expression encompassing all expressions, so it is a genus. And by his saying 'admitted truth' comes out that which does not admit it, like Zayd and Amr, and like 'ghulam Zayd'. And by his saying 'by its essence' comes out that which admits it not by its essence but by its concomitant, like constructions (insha') of command, prohibition, and others; for your saying 'give me a drink' for example, though it admits truth, that is not by its essence but by what it entails, from your saying 'I am a seeker of drink from you'. And by this condition enters what is known with certainty to be true or false: the first like the reports of Allah Most High and the reports of His messengers, peace be upon them, and reports known to be true by necessity of reason, such as 'one is half of two'; and the second like the reports of Musaylima the liar in his claim of prophethood, and reports known to be false by necessity of reason, such as 'one is half of four'; because that admits truth by its essence, even if its truth or falsehood is known with certainty due to something else. And by this you know that the mentioned condition is for both exclusion and inclusion. His saying: (truth), i.e., and falsehood, and he only did not explicitly mention it because it is known, since from its admitting truth necessarily follows its admitting falsehood.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) or assertion (khabar) as 'what admits truth by its essence.' The commentator al-Bajuri breaks down each phrase. 'What' (ma) is a genus covering all expressions. 'Admits truth' excludes expressions that cannot be true, such as proper names like Zayd or incomplete phrases like 'ghulam Zayd' (Zayd's slave) because they do not form a complete statement. 'By its essence' excludes performative utterances (insha') like commands and prohibitions, because they can be considered true only indirectly—e.g., saying 'Give me a drink' implies 'I am a seeker of drink,' but the truth is not in the command itself. Al-Bajuri then explains that 'by its essence' includes statements whose truth or falsehood is known with certainty, such as reports from Allah and His messengers (known to be true by revelation) or self-evident truths like 'one is half of two.' It also includes statements known to be false by necessity, like Musaylima's false prophethood claims or 'one is half of four.' These are still propositions because they have the potential for truth or falsehood in their essence, even if we know their actual truth value from other sources. Finally, he notes that mentioning 'truth' implies 'falsehood' as well, because a proposition that can be true can also be false.

Key Terms
قَضِيَّة (qadiyya)
خَبَر (khabar)
إِنْشَاء (insha')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is a proposition (qadiyya/khabar)?
A) The command 'Give me water'
B) 'One is half of two' (known to be true by necessity)
C) The proper name 'Zayd'
D) The incomplete phrase 'Ghulam Zayd'

Key Takeaway
A proposition is any expression that can be true or false by its own meaning, regardless of whether we actually know which it is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Determine whether each of the following is a proposition (qadiyya/khabar) according to this definition: (a) 'Allah is one', (b) 'Do not steal', (c) 'Zayd', (d) 'Every action has a purpose.' Explain your reasoning.
?
2. In Usul al-Fiqh, the statement 'Zakat is obligatory' is a khabar. Show that it meets the two conditions: it admits truth (it can be true or false) and by its essence (the truth is in the statement itself, not dependent on something else).

The Absolute Necessary Proposition: Definition and Key Distinction
Author defines the first of the seven necessary propositions, the absolute necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of the absolute necessary proposition (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) and distinguish between the majority view and Ibn Sina's restriction.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity means the judgement holds without any time or description condition, but its scope differs between scholars.

This lesson introduces the first of the seven necessary propositions: the absolute necessary. Understanding this foundation is crucial because all modal logic builds upon these propositions. The author defines it concisely, then commentators clarify its range—whether it applies only to eternal subjects or includes temporal ones. This nuance has real consequences for theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the phrase 'التي حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مع الإطلاق عن التقييد بوصف أو وقت'. This is the core: necessity of the relation, unrestricted by description or time.
2
Analyse the naming
'ضرورية' because the quality is necessity; 'مطلقة' because it's free from restriction. Understand the two components.
3
Examine the examples
Affirmative: 'كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة'; Negative: 'لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة'. Note that the subject ('insān') is temporal yet the necessity is unconditional while the subject exists.
4
Contrast the views
The majority (jumhūr) includes both eternal and non-eternal subjects. Ibn Sina restricts 'absolute necessary' to eternal subjects only. See the difference in scope.
5
Relate to the seven-fold division
Understand why the absolute necessary is one of seven: because the cause of necessity can be the essence (this one), description, or time, each with variants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَدَّدَ المُتَأَخِّرُونَ القَضَايَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ إِلَى خَمْسَةَ عَشَرَ : الضَّرُورِيَّاتُ السَّبْعُ، وَهِيَ: - (١) الضَّرُورِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : (الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ مَعَ الإِطْلَاقِ عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ وَقْتٍ)؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةً، لأَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الضَّرُورَةُ، وَمُطْلَقَةً لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ وَقْتٍ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي.
الأنبابي: قوله : (الضَّرُورِيَّاتُ السَّبْعُ) وجه كونها سبعاً : أنَّ علَّة الضَّرورة إمَّا أن تكون ذات الموضوع، أو وصفه، أو وقته المعيَّن، أو غير المعيَّن، وكلٌّ مِنَ الثلاثة الأخيرة إمَّا مقيَّد بما ينفي احتمال دوامه، وهو لا دائماً أو لا، أفاده الصبان.
قوله : (الضَّرُورِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ) هي عند الجمهور متى أطلقت شملت ما كان موضوعها أزليا؛ نحو : «الله تعالى عالم بالضَّرورة» أو غير أزلي كمثال المحشي، واصطلح ابن سينا على أنَّها متى أطلقت انصرفت للأزليَّة، فإن أريد غيرها قيَّد بدوام ذات الموضوع.
(١) الشرشيمي : قوله : (المُتَأَخِّرُونَ) وأمَّا غيرهم فعدُّوها ثلاثة عشر؛ بإسقاط : الوقتية المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة.
(٢) الشرشيمي : قوله : (الضَّرُورِيَّاتُ السَّبْعُ) وجه كونها سبعاً : أنَّ علة ضرورة النِّسبة : إمَّا الذَّات؛ أي: ذات الموضوع، وذلك في الضرورية المطلقة؛ لأنَّ ضرورة نسبتها ما دامت الذات موجودةٌ، وإما الوصف، وإما الوقت المعيَّن، أو الوقت غير المعيَّن، والثَّلاثة الأخيرة : إمَّا مع لفظ «لا دائماً»، أو لا؛ فتمتِ السَّبعة.
(٣) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي (ص: ٣٦٧).
The later scholars enumerated propositions according to the mentioned quality into fifteen: the seven necessary, and they are: (1) The absolute necessary, which is: that in which the necessity of the relation is judged with absoluteness from restriction by description or time; its affirmative example: every human is an animal by necessity, and its negative: no human is a stone by necessity. It is called 'necessary' because the quality of its relation is necessity, and 'absolute' because of its absoluteness from restriction by description or time, and it is simple as will be known from what follows. Al-Anbabi said: His statement: 'the seven necessary' — the reason for them being seven is that the cause of necessity is either the essence of the subject, or its description, or its specified time, or its unspecified time, and each of the last three is either restricted by what negates the possibility of its permanence, i.e. 'not always', or not, as al-Sabban conveyed. His statement: 'the absolute necessary' — according to the majority, when used absolutely it includes what has an eternal subject, like 'God is knowing by necessity', or non-eternal like the example of the commentator; and Ibn Sina's convention is that when used absolutely it refers to the eternal; if other is intended, it is restricted by the permanence of the subject's essence. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'the later scholars' — as for others, they counted them as thirteen, dropping the absolute temporal and the absolute spread. (2) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'the seven necessary' — the reason for them being seven is that the cause of the necessity of the relation is either the essence, i.e., the essence of the subject, and that is in the absolute necessary, because the necessity of its relation lasts as long as the essence exists; or the description; or the specified time; or the unspecified time; and the last three are either with the phrase 'not always' or not; so the seven are complete. (3) See: al-Sabban's gloss on al-Mallawi's small commentary, p. 367.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the seven necessary propositions: the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). It begins by stating that later scholars classified propositions by modality into fifteen types, among which are seven necessary propositions. The first of these is the absolute necessary: a proposition where the relation (e.g., predication) is judged as necessary, but without any restriction by a description or a time. That is, the necessity holds unconditionally. The author gives two examples: an affirmative (every human is an animal by necessity) and a negative (no human is a stone by necessity). The reason for calling it 'necessary' is that the quality of the relation is necessity; and 'absolute' because it is free from being tied to any description or time. It is also described as 'simple', meaning it is not composed of more basic modal elements, as will be explained later. Then al-Anbabi's commentary explains why there are seven such propositions: the cause of necessity can be the subject's essence itself (giving the absolute necessary), the subject's description, or its time (specified or unspecified). Each of the latter three can be either restricted by the phrase 'not always' (to indicate non-permanence) or left unrestricted, yielding six plus the first equals seven. He clarifies that the absolute necessary is understood by the majority (al-jumhur) to apply to both eternal subjects (e.g., God is knowing by necessity) and temporal subjects; but Ibn Sina restricted the term to eternals, requiring a qualification for temporal subjects. The marginal notes from al-Shirshimi add that other scholars counted thirteen instead of fifteen (dropping the absolute temporal and absolute spread), and they further explain the seven-fold division in terms of the cause of necessity being essence, description, or time, with the latter three having two variants each.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة
جهة
موجبة
سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the absolute necessary proposition is defined as:
A) A proposition where the necessity of the relation is conditioned by the permanence of the subject's description.
B) A proposition where the necessity of the relation is unconditioned by any description or time.
C) A proposition where the necessity of the relation is conditioned by a specified time.
D) A proposition where the necessity of the relation is conditioned by the negation of permanence.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition expresses necessity that is free from any temporal or descriptive condition, with its scope (eternal vs. temporal subjects) debated among logicians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'The world is temporally originated by necessity', determine whether it is an absolute necessary proposition according to the majority and according to Ibn Sina. Explain your reasoning.
?
Write an absolute necessary proposition (affirmative and negative) using a subject from fiqh, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden by necessity' and 'No intoxicant is permissible by necessity'. Check if 'intoxicant' is a temporal subject and how that affects the scope.

Subject and Predicate in Categorical Propositions
Defines subject and predicate in categorical propositions with commentary explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in any categorical proposition and verify the condition of equal mention (bis-sawiyya).

Core Idea
Subject and predicate must both be mentioned in a categorical proposition.

In categorical propositions, the subject is the first part mentioned and the predicate is the last. But the logic tradition requires that both be explicitly stated—a condition called 'bis-sawiyya' (equal mention). This lesson unpacks this foundational distinction from the matn and its commentary by al-Bajuri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first and last parts
Read the proposition and locate the first term mentioned and the last term mentioned. These are your candidates for subject and predicate respectively.
The text clarifies that 'first' and 'last' refer to rank, not necessarily temporal order.
2
Check the context: categorical or conditional?
The matn specifies that this rule applies to categorical propositions. Conditional propositions have a different structure (antecedent and consequent).
If the proposition is conditional, subject and predicate analysis does not apply directly.
3
Apply the bis-sawiyya condition
Ensure both the subject and predicate are explicitly mentioned. Neither should be omitted. The commentary stresses that they are 'clothed with equality in mention'.
This prevents incomplete propositions like 'The man is' without a predicate.
4
Name the parts correctly
Once verified, label the first as subject (mawḍūʿ) and the last as predicate (maḥmūl). The predicate is so called because it is 'carried upon' the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : (بِالحَمْلِيَّة) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِمَحْذُوفٍ صِفَةٌ لِـ «الأَوَّلِ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَالأَوَّلُ الكَائِنُ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ المَوْضُوعُ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالآخِرُ) - بِكَسْرِ الخَاءِ - بِمَعْنَى : المُتَأخّرِ، لَا بِفَتْحِهَا بِمَعْنَى : المُغَايرِ ؛ بِدَلِيلِ مُقَابَلَتِهِ بـ «الأَوَّلِ ، وَالمُرَادُ : الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً ، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (المَحْمُولُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الآخِر»، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ حُمِلَ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ عِبَارَةِ ابْنِ يَعْقُوبَ. قَوْلُهُ : (بِالسَّوِيَّة) أَيْ : حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا مُلْتَبِسَيْنِ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ بِمَعْنَى : الاسْتِوَاءِ فِي الذِّكْرِ؛ بِحَيْثُ لَا يُذْكَرُ أَحَدُهُمَا دُونَ الآخَرِ .
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. And the first [part] is the subject in the categorical, and the last [part] is the predicate with equality. And if in it a judgment is made on the mode of conditional, then it is conditional, and it divides. Al-Bajuri: His statement: 'in the categorical' is attached to an omitted [word] as an adjective for 'the first', and the estimation is: the first that occurs in the categorical is the subject. His statement: 'and the last' with kasra on the kha', meaning the later, not with fatha meaning the different; evidence is its opposition to 'the first', and what is intended is the last in rank even if mentioned first, as you know. And his statement: 'the predicate' is a predicate of 'the last', and it is so named because it is imagined as something carried upon another, as is taken from Ibn Ya'qub's expression. His statement: 'with equality' i.e., with both being clothed with equality in mention such that neither is mentioned without the other.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the subject and predicate in categorical propositions. The matn states that the first part mentioned is the subject (mawdu') in a categorical proposition, and the last part mentioned is the predicate (mahmul), with the condition that both are mentioned equally (bis-sawiyya). The commentator al-Bajuri explains that 'in the categorical' is an adjective describing the first part, meaning the subject that occurs in a categorical proposition. He clarifies that 'the last' (al-akhir) means the later in rank, not the different, and is contrasted with 'the first'. The predicate is so called because it is imagined as something carried upon the subject, as derived from Ibn Ya'qub. 'With equality' means both are mentioned together, without one being omitted.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المحمول
بالسَّوِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'bis-sawiyya' mean in the context of categorical propositions?
A) Both subject and predicate are equally important
B) Both subject and predicate are mentioned together without omission
C) The predicate is always the same as the subject
D) The subject is sometimes omitted

Key Takeaway
In a categorical proposition, the subject is the first part in rank, the predicate is the last, and both must be explicitly stated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the subject and predicate in these kalam propositions: 'All human actions are created by Allah', 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause', 'The Quran is the word of Allah'.
?
Check each proposition for the bis-sawiyya condition: are both subject and predicate explicitly mentioned? If not, rewrite them properly.
