Definition and Two Interpretations of Man' al-Jam'
Author defines exclusive disjunction with two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of man' al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) and identify the conditions each imposes on the truth values of its parts.

Core Idea
Man' al-jam' is more than just 'not both true'—it may also require 'not both false'.

The author begins the discussion of disjunctive propositions by identifying three types. The first is man' al-jam' (exclusive disjunction). There is a fundamental disagreement over what 'incompatibility' between the two parts means. One view requires incompatibility in both truth and falsehood; another only in truth. This lesson walks you through the definition and its two interpretations using concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'man' al-jam'' (مانع الجمع) in the source. Note that it is the first type of disjunctive proposition mentioned.
The term appears in the matn and is the focus of the discussion.
2
Extract the genus
Man' al-jam' is a type of disjunctive proposition (qadiyya munfasila). This places it in the broader category of propositions that express choice between alternatives.
3
Identify the differentia
The distinguishing feature is 'incompatibility' (al-tanafi) between the two parts. The text defines this as non-compatibility (adam tasahub) even if merely conventional.
Incompatibility is the core property that makes it exclusive.
4
Distinguish the two interpretations
The well-known (mashhur) view requires incompatibility in both truth (sidq) and falsehood (kadhib), meaning the parts cannot be both true and cannot be both false. The other view requires incompatibility in truth only, allowing both parts to be false together.
The second view only applies when the parts are not contradictories but a thing and a more specific contradictory.
5
Apply the distinction to examples
Examine the example 'the number is either even or not even' (contradictories) — under the first view, both parts cannot be true and cannot be false. For 'this is either white or black' (not contradictories), the first view still says they cannot be both true, but could they be both false? The second view says yes, they can be false together.
This shows how the nature of the parts affects which interpretation holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ أَسْودُ»، فَإِنَّ أَسْودَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيض أَبْيَضَ وَهُوَ لَا أَبْيَضُ ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ الأَسْوَدَ، وَالأَحْمَرَ، وَغَيْرِهِمَا .
- وَقِيلَ : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزْئِينِ صِدْقاً فَقَط (۱) ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ، نَحْرُ مَا ذُكِرَ .

Chapter on propositions and their rulings. (61) 'What necessitated mutual incompatibility between them? Their divisions are three, so let them be known. (62) Man' of conjunction, or of vacuity, or both, and it is the real, the more specific, so know.' Al-Bajuri: And it was answered: that what is meant by 'incompatibility' between them is non-compatibility, even if merely in agreement, or that the author treated compatibility as non-existent, as previously in the conditional. His statement: 'Their divisions' means the divisions of the disjunctive proposition. His statement: 'Man' of conjunction' – the apparent requirement was to say 'man'at jam', but the author made it masculine considering that the proposition is a report, and there is no need for some to say that the feminine 'ta' was omitted for necessity. End. And there is disagreement over the interpretation of man'at al-jam': Some say – and it is the well-known – it is: 'that in which is ruled the incompatibility between the two parts in truth and falsehood, or in truth only.' The first: when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or its equivalent to its contradictory; like your saying: 'The number: either even or not even', and your saying: 'The number: either even or odd.' The second: when it is composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory; like your saying: 'This: either white or black', for black is more specific than the contradictory of white which is not white, because it includes black, red, and others. And some said: it is: 'that in which is ruled the incompatibility between the two parts in truth only' (1); when it is composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory, like what was mentioned. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'in truth and falsehood... etc.' means in existence and non-existence, or existence only; i.e., they do not both exist and do not both cease, or they both cease.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of disjunctive proposition: man' al-jam' (exclusive disjunction). It begins with the matn lines from al-Sullam stating that the divisions of disjunction are three, and they are: man' jam', man' khuluw, and man'uhuma (the true disjunction). Al-Bajuri first clarifies that 'incompatibility' means non-compatibility, even if merely conventional; the author treats compatibility as non-existent for the purpose of disjunction. He then explains that 'its divisions' refers to divisions of the disjunctive proposition. He notes a grammatical point: the matn uses the masculine 'مانع' instead of feminine 'مانعة' because the proposition is considered a report (khabar). Following this, the author presents the disagreement over the interpretation of man' al-jam'. The well-known view (mashhur) is that it rules incompatibility in both truth and falsehood (i.e., the parts cannot both be true and cannot both be false) or in truth only (i.e., cannot both be true). The first case applies when the parts are a thing and its contradictory (e.g., 'either even or not even') or equivalent to its contradictory (e.g., 'either even or odd', since odd is equivalent to not even). The second case applies when the parts are a thing and a more specific contradictory (e.g., 'either white or black': black is more specific than 'not white'). Another view limits it to incompatibility in truth only, applying to the same second case. Al-Anbabi glosses the terms: 'truth and falsehood' mean existence and non-existence of the parts; the two views differ on whether both parts can be false simultaneously.

Key Terms
مانع الجمع
التنافي
صدقاً وكذباً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known interpretation (mashhur) in the passage, what two conditions does man' al-jam' impose on its two parts?
A) They cannot be true together, but may be false together.
B) They cannot be true together and cannot be false together.
C) They cannot be false together, but may be true together.
D) They cannot be true together, and if false together, it is also ruled.

Key Takeaway
Man' al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) is defined by incompatibility between its parts, with the well-known view extending it to both truth and falsehood, while another view limits it to truth only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Construct a fiqh-based disjunctive sentence that exemplifies man' al-jam' under the second interpretation (truth only). Identify the two parts and explain why they cannot both be true but can both be false.

Definition and Types of Man' al-Khuluw
Author defines inclusive disjunction with two interpretations and a gloss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of man' al-khuluw and apply them to example propositions.

Core Idea
Man' al-khuluw ensures the disjuncts cannot both be false; the well-known view also forbids both true.

Having learned about man' al-jam' (exclusive disjunction), we now turn to man' al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction). This passage defines it and presents a well-known disagreement: does it only forbid both false, or also both true? Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'man' al-khuluw', a type of disjunctive proposition. Note the opening phrase clarifies the matn's 'aw khulu' means 'man' al-khuluw'.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
The well-known (mashhur) view rules incompatibility in falsehood and truth, meaning both parts cannot be false and cannot be true together. Another view limits incompatibility to falsehood only, allowing both true.
The first interpretation has two subcases: when parts are contradictory (both false and true incompatible) or when one part is more general than the other's contradictory (only falsehood incompatible).
3
Analyze the example 'not-white or not-black'
According to the well-known interpretation, this example falls under the second subcase: 'not-black' is more general than the contradictory of 'not-white' (which is 'white'). Hence, the parts can both be true (e.g., the object is green) but cannot both be false (as that would be white and black simultaneously). Thus only falsehood is incompatible.
This illustrates that the well-known interpretation is not uniform—it depends on the relationship between the parts.
4
Test with a new example
Consider 'This number is either even or odd'. This is a contradictory pair, so according to the well-known interpretation, both cannot be false and both cannot be true (exclusive). This shows the subcase difference.
Practice by constructing your own example from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ خُلُو) أَيْ: أَوْ مَانِعُ خُلُو . وَاخْتُلِفَ أَيْضاً فِي تَفْسِيرِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ :
- فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ :- : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأين كَذِباً وَصِدْقاً ، أَوْ كَذِباً
فَالأَوَّلُ : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا : إِمَّا غَيْرُ أَبْيْضَ، أَوْ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ (۱) ، فَإِنَّ غَيْرَ أَسْوَدَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ غَيْرِ أَبْيَضَ وَهُوَ أَبْيَضُ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ فَرْداً مِنْهُ. - وَقِيلَ : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزْاينِ كَذِباً فَقَط» ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ مَا ذُكِرَ .
His statement: 'or khulu' meaning or man' khuluw. And there is disagreement also over the interpretation of man'at al-khuluw: Some said – and it is the well-known – it is: 'that in which is ruled the incompatibility between the two parts in falsehood and truth, or in falsehood only.' The first: when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or equivalent to its contradictory; like what preceded. The second: when it is composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory; like your saying: 'This: either not-white, or not-black' (1), for not-black is more general than the contradictory of not-white which is white, because it is an individual of it. And some said: it is: 'that in which is ruled the incompatibility between the two parts in falsehood only'; when it is composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory; like what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of disjunctive proposition: man' al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction). It opens by clarifying the matn phrase 'aw khulu' meaning 'or man' khuluw'. The author then presents the disagreement over its interpretation, mirroring the structure of the previous section on man' al-jam'. The well-known view (mashhur) states that man' al-khuluw rules incompatibility in falsehood and truth (i.e., the parts cannot both be false and cannot both be true) or in falsehood only (i.e., cannot both be false). The first subcase applies when the parts are a thing and its contradictory or equivalent (e.g., 'either even or not even' – same as man' al-jam' here). The second subcase applies when the parts are a thing and a more general contradictory; the example given is 'this is either not-white or not-black': 'not-black' is more general than the contradictory of 'not-white' (which is 'white'), since white is a specific instance of not-black (i.e., the set of things that are not-black includes white and other colors). Another view limits man' al-khuluw to incompatibility in falsehood only, i.e., they cannot both be false, but they may be true together; this applies to the same 'more general' scenario. Thus man' al-khuluw ensures at least one part is true, but may allow both to be true depending on the interpretation.

Key Terms
مانع الخلو
الأعمّ من النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'this is either not-white or not-black', which condition does man' al-khuluw apply according to the well-known interpretation?
A) The two parts cannot be true together.
B) The two parts cannot be false together.
C) The two parts cannot be true nor false together.
D) The two parts must be both true.

Key Takeaway
Man' al-khuluw prohibits both parts from being false; the well-known interpretation may additionally prohibit both true when the parts are contradictory.

What Is Mantiq?

The True Exclusive Disjunction: Man'uhuma
Author defines man'uhuma as the true exclusive disjunction and clarifies its relation to the other two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an exclusive disjunction (man'uhuma) in a logical proposition, distinguish it from man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw, and explain why it is called 'al-haqiqi' and 'al-akhass'.

Core Idea
The true disjunction prohibits both conjunction and vacuity — exactly one part is true.

In this passage, the author defines the third type of disjunctive proposition: man'uhuma, the 'exclusive or'. This is the strictest disjunction, ruling out both truth together and falsehood together. The author explains its grammatical derivation, its logical definition, and why it is considered the 'real' (haqiqi) and most specific (akhass) type. Understanding this type is essential for analysing arguments that require exactly one option to be true, as is common in legal and theological rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'man'uhuma' (مانعهما) in the Arabic. Note its grammatical construction: originally 'man'u-huma' (the prohibitor of both), but the noun was deleted and the pronoun stood alone. The matn phrase 'aw huma' means 'or man'uhuma'.
2
Extract the logical definition
Read the definition: man'uhuma is that in which incompatibility (tanafi) between the two parts is ruled in both truth and falsehood. This means the parts cannot be both true nor both false – exactly one must be true. This occurs when the proposition is composed of a thing and its contradictory (e.g., 'either even or not even') or an equivalent.
3
Understand why it is called 'al-haqiqi'
The author states it is called 'the real' because the incompatibility is more complete (atamm) than in the other two types. It covers both sides of truth and falsehood, unlike man' al-jam' (only truth) and man' al-khuluw (only falsehood).
4
Determine its specificity
The author notes it is 'the more specific' (al-akhass) because its condition is stricter – it imposes both incompatibilities. Therefore it is a subset of both man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw: every man'uhuma proposition is also man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw, but not vice versa.
5
Map the relationship with man' al-jam'
The relation between man'uhuma and man' al-jam' is absolute generality and specificity (al-umum wa al-khusus bi al-itlaq). They overlap in propositions composed of a thing and its contradictory, but man' al-jam' includes additional cases where only truth incompatibility is required, while man'uhuma always requires both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ هُمَا أَيْ: أَوْ مَانِعُهُمَا ، فَالضَّمِيرُ فِي الأَصْلِ مُضَافٌ إِلَيْهِ، فَلَمَّا حُذِف المُضَافُ انْفَصَلَ الضَّمِيرُ وَقَامَ مَقَامَ المُضَافِ. وَمَا نِعَتُهُمَا هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأينِ صِدْقاً وَكَذِباً ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْء وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ أَيْ: لِأَنَّ التَّنَافِي فِيهِ أَتَمُّ مِنْهُ فِي الآخَرَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ فِيهِ مِنْ جَانِبَي الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ، بِخِلَافِهِ فِيهِمَا.
وَقَوْلُهُ : (الأَخَصُّ) أَيْ: مِنْ مَانِعِ الجَمْعِ وَمِنْ مَانِعِ الخُلُو.
فَالنِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَتِهِمَا وَمَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ :
- لاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَو المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضه.
His statement: 'or both' i.e., or man'uhuma. The pronoun originally had a genitive construction; when the genitive noun was deleted, the pronoun became separate and took its place. And man'uhuma is: that in which is ruled the incompatibility between the two parts in truth and falsehood; when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or equivalent to its contradictory; like what preceded. His statement: 'and it is the real' i.e., because the incompatibility in it is more complete than in the other two, for it is from both sides of truth and falsehood, unlike them. And his statement: 'the more specific' i.e., than man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw. So the relation between man'uhuma and man' al-jam' is absolute generality and specificity: because they agree in the proposition composed of a thing and its contradictory, or equivalent to its contradictory.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of disjunctive proposition: man'uhuma (literally 'the prohobitor of both'), which combines the conditions of man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw. It begins by explaining the grammar: the matn phrase 'aw huma' means 'or man'uhuma', where the suffix pronoun was originally in a genitive construct (i.e., man'u-huma) but the noun was deleted and the pronoun stood alone. The definition: man'uhuma rules incompatibility in both truth and falsehood, i.e., the parts cannot be both true nor both false. This occurs when the proposition is composed of a thing and its contradictory (e.g., 'either even or not even') or an equivalent (e.g., 'either even or odd'). The author then explains why it is called 'the real' (haqiqi): because the incompatibility is more complete (atamm) – it applies to both truth and falsehood, unlike the other two types which may only apply to one side. Furthermore, it is 'the more specific' (al-akhass) because its condition is stricter; it is a subset of both man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw. The relationship between man'uhuma and man' al-jam' is one of absolute generality and specificity (al-umum wa al-khusus bi al-itlaq): they overlap in the case of a thing and its contradictory, but man' al-jam' is more general because it includes cases where only truth incompatibility is ruled, while man'uhuma always requires both.

Key Terms
مانعهما (māni‘uhumā): The disjunction that prohibits both conjunction and vacuity; i.e., the parts cannot be true together and cannot be false together. [Note: This is the standard term for the 'exclusive or' in the logical sense of exactly one true; stable across the tradition.]
الحقيقي (al-ḥaqīqī): The 'real' disjunction, where incompatibility is most complete, covering both truth and falsehood. [Note: In some later texts, 'haqiqi' may refer to the exclusive or in a broader sense, but here it is specifically the combination of both conditions.]
الأخص (al-akhaṣṣ): The more specific type, i.e., man'uhuma, because it imposes both conditions (truth and falsehood incompatibility), making it a subset of the other two. [Note: Standard comparative term for specificity in classification.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is man'uhuma called the 'more specific' (al-akhass) type?
A) Because it is the rarest in Arabic speech.
B) Because it is a subset of both man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw.
C) Because it only applies to necessary propositions.
D) Because the author invented the term.

Key Takeaway
Man'uhuma is the exclusive disjunction that prohibits both conjunction and vacuity, making it the most complete and specific type of disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a man'uhuma proposition in fiqh: pick a topic like 'wudu is valid or invalid' and verify that exactly one option can be true. Write the proposition and indicate why it fits the definition.
?
Take the proposition 'either prayer is obligatory today or it is not' – which disjunction type does it represent? Explain your reasoning by checking truth and falsehood conditions.

What Is Mantiq?

The Definition of Contradiction (Tanāquḍ)
Author defines contradiction and clarifies its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and identify its two essential conditions: opposition in quality and necessary truth of one.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires both quality opposition and necessary truth-value exclusivity.

Contradiction is a foundational concept in logic, used to identify genuine opposition between propositions. In this lesson, we unpack the classical definition given by the logicians, focusing on its two key components. Understanding this definition will equip you to distinguish true contradictions from mere differences in subject, predicate, or time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions in the definition
The author defines contradiction as 'opposition of two propositions in quality, and truth of one is a necessary matter.' Extract these two conditions: (1) khulf fi al-kayf (difference in affirmation/negation), (2) sidq wahid amr qafi (it is necessary that one is true).
Quality here means affirmative or negative.
2
Understand the scope of propositions
The author mentions that contradiction can occur not only between simple categorical propositions but also between imperative composites ('Stand, do not stand'), relational composites ('Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant'), and even between simple terms ('Zayd, not Zayd'). However, logicians primarily focus on propositions.
The inclusion of simples is debated; the author notes some scholars allow it.
3
Recognize what the definition excludes
The phrase 'in quality' excludes opposition in subject, predicate, modality, time, or place. For example, 'Zayd is standing, Amr is standing' differ in subject, not quality, so it is not contradiction.
The author provides examples: subject, predicate, modality, and time differences.

وَالمُفْرَدَاتِ؛ كَـ: «زَيْدٌ، لَا زَيْدٌ». وَمُقْتَضَى ذَلِكَ: أَنَّ اخْتِلَافَ المُفْرَدَاتِ لَا يُسَمَّى تَنَاقُضاً كَـ: فِي اصْطِلاحِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، وَهُوَ مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ، لَكِنْ فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مَا يُفِيدُ أَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ فِي اصْطِلاحِهِمْ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَخْصِيصُ المُصَنِّفِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالذِّكْرِ؛ لِكَوْنِ القَضَايَا هِيَ المَقْصُودَةُ لَهُمْ بِالأَصَالَةِ، لَا لِلِاحْتِرَازِ عَنِ المُفْرَدَيْنِ. وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ: فِي كَيْفَ خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي غَيْرِهِ مِنْ مَوْضُوعِ، أَوْ مَحْمُولٍ، أَوْ عُدُولٍ، أَوْ تَحْصِيلٍ، أَوْ غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ: - فَالأَوَّلُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ، عَمْرُو قَائِمٌ. - وَالثَّانِي كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ كَاتِبٌ. - وَالثَّالِثُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدُ هُوَ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا قَائِمٌ. - وَالرَّابِعُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمُ الآنَ، زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ أَمْسَ، وَكَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: «زَيْدٌ جَالِسٌ فِي الدَّارِ، زَيْدٌ جَالِسٌ فِي المَسْجِدِ»، إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ. وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ بِأَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ غَيْرُ مَانِعِ؛ لِصِدْقِهِ بِخُلْفِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي الكَيْفِ: - مَعَ جَوَازِ صِدْقِهِمَا وَكَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، عَمْرُو لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِكَاتِبٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ صَائِمُ الآنَ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِصَائِمٍ أَمْسَ، إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ. - وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ صِدْقِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ. - وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ كَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ. - وَمَعَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبِ الأُخْرَى اتِّفَاقاً، لَا اطْرَاداً؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَيَوَانِ، وَقَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ. وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ اتِّفَاقاً؛ لِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ لَازِمٍ فِي كُلِّ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتَلَفَا فِي الكَيْفِ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ أَمْرٌ اتَّفَقَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ الَّتِي فِيهَا المَحْمُولُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ المَوْضُوعِ؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِهِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَفِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ.
Section on contradiction. (63) 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and truth of one is a necessary matter.' al-Bajuri - From the verbal composites like 'Stand, do not stand.' - Or the relational composites like 'Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant.' - And from simples like 'Zayd, not Zayd.' The implication of that is that the difference of simples is not called contradiction in the terminology of the logicians, and this is what al-Mallawi stated in his major commentary, but in the speech of some of them is what indicates that it is called that in their terminology, and accordingly the author's specification of the two propositions is for the sake that propositions are their primary concern, not to exclude the two simples. And by his statement 'in quality' there is excluded the opposition of the two propositions in other than it, such as subject, predicate, modality, time, or other than that: - The first as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Amr is standing. - The second as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing. - The third as in your saying: Zayd himself is standing, Zayd himself is not standing. - The fourth as in your saying: Zayd is standing now, Zayd was standing yesterday, and as in your saying: 'Zayd is sitting in the house, Zayd is sitting in the mosque,' etc. And it was objected against the author that this definition is not exclusive, because it applies to the opposition of two propositions in quality: - With the possibility of both being true and both false, as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Amr is not standing, and your saying: Zayd is standing, Zayd is not writing, and your saying: Zayd is fasting now, Zayd is not fasting yesterday, etc. - And with necessary truth of both, as in your saying: Some animal is human, some animal is not human. - And with necessary falsehood of both, as in your saying: Every animal is human, and nothing animal is human. - And with truth of one and falsehood of the other coincidentally, not universally, as in your saying: Every human is animal, and nothing human is animal, and your saying: Some human is animal, some human is not animal. And that was only coincidental because it is not necessary in every pair of universals or particulars that differ in quality, rather it is a matter that coincidentally occurs due to the specificity of the matter in which the predicate is more general than the subject; evidence of its absence in examples like your saying: Every animal is human, nothing animal is human, and in examples like your saying: Some animal is human, some animal is not human.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logic. The author begins by defining it as the opposition (khulf) of two propositions in quality (kayf), with the additional condition that exactly one of them is true (truth of one is a necessary matter). This definition distinguishes genuine contradiction from mere difference in other respects. The author then clarifies that contradiction occurs not only between simple propositions but also between imperative composites ('Stand, do not stand'), relational composites ('Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant'), and even between two simple terms like 'Zayd' and 'not Zayd', though logicians typically reserve the term for propositions; some allow it for simples as well, but the focus remains on propositions. Next, the author explains what is excluded by the condition 'in quality': opposition in subject, predicate, modality, time, or place is not contradiction. For example, 'Zayd is standing, Amr is standing' differ in subject; 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing' differ in predicate; 'Zayd himself is standing, Zayd himself is not standing' differ in modality? Actually the third example involves affirmation and negation but with a different modality—the author treats it as excluded because the opposition is in something other than quality per se. The fourth excludes temporal differences like 'Zayd is standing now, Zayd was standing yesterday'. Then the author presents an objection: the definition is not exclusive because it would also apply to cases where the two propositions differ in quality but can both be true or both false, etc. The objector gives four categories: (1) both can be true or false (e.g., differing subjects or times), (2) both must be true (e.g., subcontraries like 'Some animal is human, some is not'), (3) both must be false (e.g., contraries like 'Every animal is human, no animal is human'), (4) one true and one false coincidentally but not universally (e.g., where predicate is wider than subject). The author responds that these cases are not genuine contradictions because the truth condition is not necessary; it only coincidentally occurs due to the specific matter. In true contradiction, the truth difference is necessary and universal across all pairs of propositions opposite in quality, quantity, subject, and predicate. The examples given show that when the predicate is not broader, the truth condition fails. Thus the definition is defended as exclusive when the condition 'truth of one is necessary' is properly understood as requiring necessary truth-value difference, not coincidental.

تناقض
خلف
كيف
قضية
صدق واحد أمر قفي

B) 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is animal'

D) 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human'

Key Takeaway
The definitive condition for contradiction is not just difference in affirmation/negation but the necessary truth of exactly one proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What Is Mantiq?

The Objection and Defense of the Definition's Exclusivity
Author responds to an objection that the definition is not exclusive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection that the definition of contradiction is not exclusive and reconstruct the author's defense by distinguishing necessary truth from coincidental truth.

Core Idea
The definition's second condition ('truth of one is necessary') excludes cases where truth difference is merely coincidental.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated objection raised against the standard definition of contradiction. The objector provides several counterexamples intended to show that the definition is too broad. We then see how the author defends the definition by clarifying that the truth difference must be necessary and universal, not coincidental and material-dependent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims the definition is not exclusive (mane') because it applies to pairs that are not genuine contradictions. He lists four categories of pairs that differ in quality but do not have necessary truth exclusivity: (1) both can be true or false, (2) both must be true, (3) both must be false, (4) one true one false coincidentally.
These examples involve differences in subject, time, or quantity that prevent necessary exclusivity.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that in true contradiction, the truth difference is necessary and holds for all pairs of that form. The objection's examples fail because they rely on specific material conditions (e.g., predicate being broader than subject). When the material is changed, the truth difference disappears.
The author uses counterexamples to show that the objector's cases are not universally necessary.
3
Distinguish necessary vs. coincidental truth
The key distinction is between 'ittifaq' (coincidental) and 'itTirad' (universal/regular). Genuine contradiction requires the truth difference to be universal across all substitutions of subject and predicate that preserve the logical form. The objector's examples only work due to specific subject-predicate relations.
Understanding this distinction is crucial for using the definition in logical analysis.

مَعَ جَوَازِ صِدْقِهِمَا وَكَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، عَمْرُو لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِكَاتِبٍ، وَقَوْلِكَ: زَيْدٌ صَائِمُ الآنَ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِصَائِمٍ أَمْسَ، إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ. - وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ صِدْقِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ. - وَمَعَ وُجُوبِ كَذِبِهِمَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ. - وَمَعَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبِ الأُخْرَى اتِّفَاقاً، لَا اطْرَاداً؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَيَوَانِ، وَقَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ. وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ اتِّفَاقاً؛ لِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ لَازِمٍ فِي كُلِّ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتَلَفَا فِي الكَيْفِ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ أَمْرٌ اتَّفَقَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ الَّتِي فِيهَا المَحْمُولُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ المَوْضُوعِ؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِهِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَفِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ.
Section on contradiction. (63) 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and truth of one is a necessary matter.' al-Bajuri - From the verbal composites like 'Stand, do not stand.' - Or the relational composites like 'Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant.' - And from simples like 'Zayd, not Zayd.' The implication of that is that the difference of simples is not called contradiction in the terminology of the logicians, and this is what al-Mallawi stated in his major commentary, but in the speech of some of them is what indicates that it is called that in their terminology, and accordingly the author's specification of the two propositions is for the sake that propositions are their primary concern, not to exclude the two simples. And by his statement 'in quality' there is excluded the opposition of the two propositions in other than it, such as subject, predicate, modality, time, or other than that: - The first as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Amr is standing. - The second as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing. - The third as in your saying: Zayd himself is standing, Zayd himself is not standing. - The fourth as in your saying: Zayd is standing now, Zayd was standing yesterday, and as in your saying: 'Zayd is sitting in the house, Zayd is sitting in the mosque,' etc. And it was objected against the author that this definition is not exclusive, because it applies to the opposition of two propositions in quality: - With the possibility of both being true and both false, as in your saying: Zayd is standing, Amr is not standing, and your saying: Zayd is standing, Zayd is not writing, and your saying: Zayd is fasting now, Zayd is not fasting yesterday, etc. - And with necessary truth of both, as in your saying: Some animal is human, some animal is not human. - And with necessary falsehood of both, as in your saying: Every animal is human, and nothing animal is human. - And with truth of one and falsehood of the other coincidentally, not universally, as in your saying: Every human is animal, and nothing human is animal, and your saying: Some human is animal, some human is not animal. And that was only coincidental because it is not necessary in every pair of universals or particulars that differ in quality, rather it is a matter that coincidentally occurs due to the specificity of the matter in which the predicate is more general than the subject; evidence of its absence in examples like your saying: Every animal is human, nothing animal is human, and in examples like your saying: Some animal is human, some animal is not human.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logic. The author begins by defining it as the opposition (khulf) of two propositions in quality (kayf), with the additional condition that exactly one of them is true (truth of one is a necessary matter). This definition distinguishes genuine contradiction from mere difference in other respects. The author then clarifies that contradiction occurs not only between simple propositions but also between imperative composites ('Stand, do not stand'), relational composites ('Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant'), and even between two simple terms like 'Zayd' and 'not Zayd', though logicians typically reserve the term for propositions; some allow it for simples as well, but the focus remains on propositions. Next, the author explains what is excluded by the condition 'in quality': opposition in subject, predicate, modality, time, or place is not contradiction. For example, 'Zayd is standing, Amr is standing' differ in subject; 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing' differ in predicate; 'Zayd himself is standing, Zayd himself is not standing' differ in modality? Actually the third example involves affirmation and negation but with a different modality—the author treats it as excluded because the opposition is in something other than quality per se. The fourth excludes temporal differences like 'Zayd is standing now, Zayd was standing yesterday'. Then the author presents an objection: the definition is not exclusive because it would also apply to cases where the two propositions differ in quality but can both be true or both false, etc. The objector gives four categories: (1) both can be true or false (e.g., differing subjects or times), (2) both must be true (e.g., subcontraries like 'Some animal is human, some is not'), (3) both must be false (e.g., contraries like 'Every animal is human, no animal is human'), (4) one true and one false coincidentally but not universally (e.g., where predicate is wider than subject). The author responds that these cases are not genuine contradictions because the truth condition is not necessary; it only coincidentally occurs due to the specific matter. In true contradiction, the truth difference is necessary and universal across all pairs of propositions opposite in quality, quantity, subject, and predicate. The examples given show that when the predicate is not broader, the truth condition fails. Thus the definition is defended as exclusive when the condition 'truth of one is necessary' is properly understood as requiring necessary truth-value difference, not coincidental.

Key Terms
مانع
صدق واحد أمر قفي
اتفاق
اطراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why are the objector's examples not genuine contradictions?
A) Because they involve different subjects or times
B) Because they are all false
C) Because the definition only applies to propositions about animals
D) Because the objector did not understand the definition

Key Takeaway
The exclusivity of the definition is defended by showing that apparent counterexamples lack necessary truth difference once all relevant conditions are fixed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the pair of propositions: 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is animal'. Explain why this is a genuine contradiction according to the definition. Then take the pair 'Some human is animal' and 'Some human is not animal'. Explain why this is not a contradiction, referring to the author's distinction between necessary and coincidental truth.
?
Identify which of the following pairs satisfies the definition of contradiction and which does not, and explain why using the author's response: (a) 'Zayd is praying' and 'Zayd is not praying' (b) 'Zayd is praying' and 'Amr is not praying'.

Definition of Contradiction and the Eight Unities
Author defines contradiction and lists the eight necessary unities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two propositions form a true contradiction by verifying the eight unities.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than opposite truth values—it demands unity in every aspect except quality.

This lesson unpacks the classical definition of contradiction (tanaqud) from al-Bannani's text and al-Bajuri's commentary. You will learn that contradiction is not simply one true and one false statement; it requires that the two propositions share the same subject, predicate, time, place, and other conditions. Without these unities, the two statements cannot be said to contradict each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the core definition: contradiction is opposition in quality (affirmation vs negation) with the truth of one being a universal rule (amr qufi).
The phrase 'amr qufi' indicates that this rule is conditional.
2
Understand the role of 'amr qufi'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'amr qufi' (a matter followed) means the definition holds universally only when the eight unities are present.
Without these unities, the truth-falsity relation is not guaranteed.
3
Extract the eight unities
List the unities mentioned: subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, whole/part.
These ensure the two propositions refer to the same situation.
4
Apply to an example
Check why 'Zayd is standing; 'Amr is not standing' is not a contradiction: different subjects violate unity of subject.
Same reasoning applies to other unities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التناقض
(٦٣) «تناقض»: خلف القضيتين في كيف، وصدق واحد أمر قفي.
الباجوري: وأجيب بأن جميع ذلك خرج بقوله: وصدق واحد أمر قفي، فإنه وإن كان محتملاً للاستئناف، جعله حالاً أولى، فيكون قيداً في التعريف، فتأمل. قوله: (في كيف) أي: في الإيجاب والسلب، وكذا في الكم؛ أعني: الكلية والجزئية إن كانت القضية الأصلية مسورة، كما سيذكره المصنف بقوله: وإن تكن محصورة بالسور... إلخ، وإنما اقتصر على ذكر الكيف هنا؛ لاطراده في جميع القضايا حتى الشخصية والمهملة، بخلاف الكم، كما هو ظاهر. قوله: (وصدق واحد) أي: وكذب الآخر، ففي كلامه اكتفاء. وكان مقتضى الظاهر أن يقول: «وصدق واحدة... إلخ»، لكنه نظر إلى كون القضيتين بمعنى القولين. وقوله: (أمر قفي) أي: تبع، وذلك كناية عن كونه مطرداً، ولا يكون كذلك إلا عند ثبوت الوحدات المشهورة، وهي: وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة المحمول، ووحدة الزمان، ووحدة المكان، ووحدة الإضافة، ووحدة الشرط، ووحدة القوة أو الفعل، ووحدة الكل أو الجزء..... إلى غير ذلك؛ ألا ترى أنه ليس كذلك في نحو: قولنا: زيد قائم، عمرو ليس بقائم.
Chapter on Contradiction. (63) 'Contradiction': opposition of the two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter followed. Al-Bajuri: And it was answered that all of that is excluded by his saying: 'and the truth of one is a matter followed', for although it is possible as a resumption, making it a circumstantial clause is better, so it becomes a condition in the definition, so reflect. His saying: 'in quality' means: in affirmation and negation, and likewise in quantity; I mean: universality and particularity if the original proposition is quantified, as the author will mention in his saying: 'And if it is quantified by the quantifier... etc.', and he only restricted mention of quality here because it applies in all propositions even particular and indefinite, unlike quantity, as is apparent. His saying: 'and truth of one' means: and falsity of the other, so in his speech there is economization. The apparent requirement was that he say: 'and truth of one [feminine]... etc.', but he considered that the two propositions are in the sense of two statements. And his saying: 'a matter followed' means: followed, and that is a metaphor for its being universal, and it is not like that except with the establishment of the well-known unities, which are: unity of subject, unity of predicate, unity of time, unity of place, unity of relation, unity of condition, unity of potentiality or actuality, unity of whole or part... etc.; do you not see that it is not like that in examples like: 'Zayd is standing, 'Amr is not standing'.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on contradiction (al-tanaqud). The author first provides the definition: contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in quality (i.e., one affirmative, one negative) such that one is true and the other false, and this is a universal rule (amr qufi). The commentator al-Bajuri explains that the phrase 'amr qufi' (a matter followed) indicates that this property is consistent only when certain unities are fulfilled. The condition of unity in quality applies universally, even to particular and indefinite propositions, whereas quantity (universal/particular) only applies to quantified propositions. The definition implicitly requires unity of subject, predicate, time, place, relation, condition, potentiality/actuality, whole/part, and so on. Without these, as in the example 'Zayd is standing, 'Amr is not standing' (different subjects), there is no contradiction because the subjects differ.

Key Terms
تناقض
خلف
كيف
كم
وحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs is a true contradiction?
A) Zayd is standing; 'Amr is not standing.
B) Zayd is standing; Zayd is not sitting.
C) Zayd is standing in the mosque; Zayd is not standing in the market.
D) Zayd is standing; Zayd is not standing.

Key Takeaway
For a true contradiction, two propositions must differ only in quality while sharing all other conditions (the eight unities).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the pair 'Zayd's sale is valid; 'Amr's sale is not valid', determine if it is a contradiction. Check the unities: different subjects, so no contradiction.
?
Given the pair 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; Some intoxicant is not forbidden', verify if it is a contradiction. Assume unity of subject, predicate, time, etc. It is a contradiction because they differ in quality and quantity but with same subject and predicate.

The Logical Qiyas: Definition and Exclusion Clauses
Author logically defines qiyas and explains its exclusion clauses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the logical definition of qiyas to classify arguments as valid syllogisms, sterile moods, or qiyas al-musawa.

Core Idea
The logical qiyas is a self-sufficient argument form that excludes single propositions, sterile moods, and equality syllogisms.

After covering qiyas in language and usul al-fiqh, the author gives the precise logical definition and carefully excludes three things that might be mistaken for syllogisms. Understanding these exclusions is crucial for identifying valid deductive arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the logical definition
Learn the definition: 'A statement composed of propositions, entailing by itself another statement.' This is the standard logical qiyas (syllogism).
2
Parse the three exclusion clauses
The definition has three parts: 'composed of propositions' excludes single propositions; 'entailing' excludes sterile moods; 'by itself' excludes qiyas al-musawa.
Each clause eliminates a common misconception.
3
Understand each exclusion
(a) A single proposition, even compound modal, is not qiyas. (b) Sterile moods (invalid syllogistic forms) do not produce a conclusion. (c) Qiyas al-musawa requires an unstated premise, so it is not self-sufficient.
4
Apply the definition
Given an argument, check if it meets all three conditions: composed of propositions, entails a conclusion, and self-sufficient.
Use Islamic examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ فِي القِيَاسِ) (۱) قَوْلُهُ : (بَابُ فِي القِيَاسِ) أَيْ : فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ، وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ القِيَاسَ فِي اللُّغَةِ : تَقْدِيرُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى مِثَالِ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ كَ : تَقْدِيرِ نَحْوِ «القُمَاشِ» عَلَى الآلَةِ الحِرِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِثَالٌ لِلذَّرَاعِ الكُلِّيّ. وَفِي اصْطَلَاحِ الأُصُولِيِّينَ : حَمْلُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ فِي الحُكْمِ بِجَامِعِ بَيْنَهُمَا»؛ كَـ: حَمْلِ النَّبِيذِ عَلَى الخَمْرِ فِي الحُرْمَةِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ الإِسْكَارِ فِيهِمَا. وَفِي اصْطِلَاحِ المَنَاطِقَةِ مَا يُعْلَمُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مِنْ أَنَّهُ : قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا مُسْتَلْزِمٌ بِذَاتِهِ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ : - بِقَوْلِنَا : مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا»: مَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ كَـ : القَضِيَّةِ الوَاحِدَةِ، وَلَوْ كَانَتْ مِنَ المُوَجَّهَاتِ المُرَكَّبَةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ لَا دَائِماً» ; لِأَنَّهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ فِي قُوَّةِ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ، لَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهَا اسْمُ القَضِيتَيْنِ . وَبِقَوْلِنَا : مُسْتَلْزِم ... إلخ»: مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ كَـ: الضُّرُوبِ العَقِيمَةِ ; لأَنَّهَا لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً - وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : «بِذَاتِهِ»؛ لِيَخْرُجَ : قِيَاسُ المُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ: «مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ
Chapter on qiyas. (1) His saying: 'Chapter on qiyas' means: concerning its definition, its rulings, its divisions, and what pertains to that. Know that qiyas in language is: measuring something according to the example of another thing, like measuring something like 'cloth' upon the manual instrument which is an example for the universal cubit. And in the terminology of the usuliyyun: applying something to something else in the ruling due to a commonality between them; like applying nabidh to khamr in prohibition due to the commonality of intoxication in both. And in the terminology of the logicians: that which is known from the words of the author, that it is: a statement composed of propositions, entailing by itself another statement. And it is not hidden that what is excluded: - by our saying 'composed of propositions': that which is not like that, like the single proposition, even if it is from the compound modal propositions, like 'Zayd is standing absolutely, not always'; because although it is in the force of two propositions, the name of 'two propositions' is not applied to it. And by our saying 'entailing ... etc.': that which is not like that, like the sterile moods; because they do not entail a statement. And we only said 'by itself' in order to exclude: qiyas al-musawa, which is: 'that which is composed of two propositions ...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism/analogy) in three ways: linguistically, in usul al-fiqh, and in logic. Linguistically, qiyas means measuring something by an example, like measuring cloth by a cubit. In usul al-fiqh, qiyas is a legal analogy: applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a common effective cause ('illa). The example given is applying the prohibition of wine (khamr) to nabidh (date wine) because both cause intoxication. In logic, qiyas is defined as a statement composed of propositions that by itself entails another statement (the conclusion). The author then explains what is excluded by each part of the definition: 'composed of propositions' excludes single propositions, even compound modal ones; 'entailing' excludes sterile moods (invalid syllogistic forms) that do not produce a conclusion; and 'by itself' excludes qiyas al-musawa (syllogism of equality), which requires an external premise to entail the conclusion. The text is cut off while describing qiyas al-musawa.

القياس
قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا
مُسْتَلْزِمٌ بِذَاتِهِ
قِيَاسُ المُسَاوَاةِ
الضُّرُوبِ العَقِيمَةِ

Checkpoint
In the logical definition of qiyas, the phrase 'by itself' (bidhatihi) excludes which of the following?
A) A single proposition
B) Sterile moods
C) Qiyas al-musawa
D) Compound modal propositions

Key Takeaway
A valid logical qiyas is a self-contained composite of propositions that necessarily yields a conclusion, excluding single propositions, sterile moods, and qiyas al-musawa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Check each part of the logical definition: (a) Is it composed of propositions? (b) Does it entail a conclusion? (c) Is it self-sufficient? Does it match the definition of logical qiyas?
?
Now consider: 'Every act either is obligatory or prohibited; this act is not obligatory; therefore it is prohibited.' Identify if this is a valid logical qiyas or if it falls under an exclusion.

8 min
Qiyas Across Disciplines: Linguistic, Legal, and Logical
Author introduces three definitions of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of qiyas and correctly classify any given qiyas as linguistic, usuli, or mantiqi.

Core Idea
Qiyas shifts from a general notion of measurement to a specific legal analogy and finally to a formal deductive argument.

The same word 'qiyas' carries three distinct meanings in Islamic sciences: a linguistic sense of measuring, a legal sense of analogical reasoning (qiyas usuli), and a logical sense of syllogism. Confusing them leads to category errors, especially when studying texts from different disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Learn the linguistic definition
Linguistically, qiyas means 'measuring something by an example' – like measuring cloth with a cubit. It is a general notion of comparison.
2
Learn the usuli definition
In usul al-fiqh, qiyas is applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a common effective cause ('illa). Example: nabidh is prohibited like khamr due to intoxication.
This is a legal analogy.
3
Learn the logical definition
In logic, qiyas is a statement composed of propositions that by itself entails another statement (the conclusion). This is a deductive syllogism.
4
Compare and contrast
Note that legal qiyas is a type of analogical reasoning, while logical qiyas is deductive. They have different structures and purposes.
5
Practice classification
Given an example, decide which discipline's qiyas it belongs to. Use the definitions as criteria.

بَابٌ فِي القِيَاسِ) (۱) قَوْلُهُ : (بَابُ فِي القِيَاسِ) أَيْ : فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ، وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ القِيَاسَ فِي اللُّغَةِ : تَقْدِيرُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى مِثَالِ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ كَ : تَقْدِيرِ نَحْوِ «القُمَاشِ» عَلَى الآلَةِ الحِرِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِثَالٌ لِلذَّرَاعِ الكُلِّيّ. وَفِي اصْطَلَاحِ الأُصُولِيِّينَ : حَمْلُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ فِي الحُكْمِ بِجَامِعِ بَيْنَهُمَا»؛ كَـ: حَمْلِ النَّبِيذِ عَلَى الخَمْرِ فِي الحُرْمَةِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ الإِسْكَارِ فِيهِمَا. وَفِي اصْطِلَاحِ المَنَاطِقَةِ مَا يُعْلَمُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مِنْ أَنَّهُ : قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا مُسْتَلْزِمٌ بِذَاتِهِ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ : - بِقَوْلِنَا : مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا»: مَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ كَـ : القَضِيَّةِ الوَاحِدَةِ، وَلَوْ كَانَتْ مِنَ المُوَجَّهَاتِ المُرَكَّبَةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ لَا دَائِماً» ; لِأَنَّهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ فِي قُوَّةِ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ، لَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهَا اسْمُ القَضِيتَيْنِ . وَبِقَوْلِنَا : مُسْتَلْزِم ... إلخ»: مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ كَـ: الضُّرُوبِ العَقِيمَةِ ; لأَنَّهَا لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً - وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : «بِذَاتِهِ»؛ لِيَخْرُجَ : قِيَاسُ المُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ: «مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ
Chapter on qiyas. (1) His saying: 'Chapter on qiyas' means: concerning its definition, its rulings, its divisions, and what pertains to that. Know that qiyas in language is: measuring something according to the example of another thing, like measuring something like 'cloth' upon the manual instrument which is an example for the universal cubit. And in the terminology of the usuliyyun: applying something to something else in the ruling due to a commonality between them; like applying nabidh to khamr in prohibition due to the commonality of intoxication in both. And in the terminology of the logicians: that which is known from the words of the author, that it is: a statement composed of propositions, entailing by itself another statement. And it is not hidden that what is excluded: - by our saying 'composed of propositions': that which is not like that, like the single proposition, even if it is from the compound modal propositions, like 'Zayd is standing absolutely, not always'; because although it is in the force of two propositions, the name of 'two propositions' is not applied to it. And by our saying 'entailing ... etc.': that which is not like that, like the sterile moods; because they do not entail a statement. And we only said 'by itself' in order to exclude: qiyas al-musawa, which is: 'that which is composed of two propositions ...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism/analogy) in three ways: linguistically, in usul al-fiqh, and in logic. Linguistically, qiyas means measuring something by an example, like measuring cloth by a cubit. In usul al-fiqh, qiyas is a legal analogy: applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a common effective cause ('illa). The example given is applying the prohibition of wine (khamr) to nabidh (date wine) because both cause intoxication. In logic, qiyas is defined as a statement composed of propositions that by itself entails another statement (the conclusion). The author then explains what is excluded by each part of the definition: 'composed of propositions' excludes single propositions, even compound modal ones; 'entailing' excludes sterile moods (invalid syllogistic forms) that do not produce a conclusion; and 'by itself' excludes qiyas al-musawa (syllogism of equality), which requires an external premise to entail the conclusion. The text is cut off while describing qiyas al-musawa.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Linguistic and Usuli Definitions of Qiyas
Author gives the linguistic and usuli definitions of qiyas, distinguishing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic, usuli, and mantiqi definitions of qiyas and explain the key difference between them.

Core Idea
Qiyas shifts from measurement to juristic analogy based on a shared cause, distinct from the mantiqi syllogism.

This lesson opens the chapter on qiyas by clarifying its two primary definitions: the linguistic meaning (measuring one thing against another) and the usuli meaning (analogical extension of a ruling due to a shared cause). Understanding this distinction is crucial because the later mantiqi definition differs significantly, and confusion between them can lead to errors in both fiqh and logic. The author also signals that the chapter will cover rulings and divisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition
The author defines qiyas in language as 'measuring something according to the pattern of another thing' (تقدير شيء على مثال شيء آخر). Note the example: measuring cloth against a cubit-stick, which serves as a pattern for the universal cubit.
This is a broad, non-technical meaning.
2
Identify the usuli definition
In usul al-fiqh, qiyas is defined as 'applying something to another thing in ruling due to a commonality between them' (حمل شيء على شيء آخر في الحكم بجامع بينهما). The classic example: applying the prohibition of wine to nabidh because both share intoxication as the common cause.
The term 'jāmi‘' refers to the effective cause ('illah).
3
Compare the two definitions
The linguistic definition is about measurement in general (e.g., physical measurement). The usuli definition is a legal operation: extending a ruling (hukm) from an original (asl) to a branch (far‘) due to a shared attribute. The key difference is that the usuli definition is specific to jurisprudence and requires a common cause.
The linguistic definition does not involve rulings or legal causation.
4
Recognize the shift to mantiq
The passage signals that the chapter covers definitions, rulings, and divisions of qiyas. The mantiqi definition (syllogism) will be introduced later. This lesson prepares you to notice that the term 'qiyas' is polysemous, and each discipline uses it differently.
In mantiq, qiyas means a composed statement entailing another by itself, not analogy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (بَابُ فِي القِيَاسِ) أَيْ : فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ، وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ القِيَاسَ فِي اللُّغَةِ : تَقْدِيرُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى مِثَالِ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ كَ : تَقْدِيرِ نَحْوِ «القُمَاشِ» عَلَى الآلَةِ الحِرِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ مِثَالٌ لِلذَّرَاعِ الكُلِّيّ. وَفِي اصْطِلَاحِ الأُصُولِيِّينَ : حَمْلُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ فِي الحُكْمِ بِجَامِعِ بَيْنَهُمَا»؛ كَـ: حَمْلِ النَّبِيذِ عَلَى الخَمْرِ فِي الحُرْمَةِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ الإِسْكَارِ فِيهِمَا.
His statement: 'Chapter on Qiyas' means: regarding its definition, its rulings, its divisions, and what relates to that. And know that qiyas in language is: measuring something according to the pattern of another thing, like measuring cloth against the cubit-stick which is a pattern for the universal cubit. And in the terminology of the usulis it is: applying something to another thing in ruling due to a commonality between them; like applying nabidh to wine in prohibition due to the common cause of intoxication in both.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qiyas by clarifying the term's meanings. The author first explains that the chapter covers definition, rulings, divisions, and related matters. Then he gives two definitions. The linguistic definition: qiyas is measuring something against a pattern, like measuring cloth with a cubit-stick that represents the mental universal cubit. The usuli definition: qiyas is applying the ruling of one thing to another because they share an effective cause (jāmi‘). He illustrates with the classic example: applying the prohibition of wine to nabidh (date wine) because both share intoxication as the cause. This distinction is important because the mantiqi definition, coming later, is different from the usuli one.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
15 min
The Mantiqi Definition of Qiyas and Its Exclusions
Defining qiyas and excluding four invalid types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the definition of qiyas to identify invalid syllogistic forms and explain why each fails the conditions.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism entails a new conclusion by its own logical structure, not by an external premise.

The logicians' definition of qiyas is precise and guards against common errors. It requires the syllogism to be composed of propositions, to entail a conclusion by itself, and that conclusion to be a single new statement. Each condition excludes a specific pitfall, from sterile moods to relational syllogisms. Understanding these exclusions sharpens your ability to distinguish genuine syllogistic proof from mere appearance.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition into three components
Identify the three conditions: (1) composed of propositions, (2) entailing another statement, (3) by itself. Understand what each condition contributes to excluding invalid forms.
2
Examine the exclusion of single propositions
A single proposition, even if compound, is not a syllogism because it lacks the composition of at least two propositions. Example: 'Zayd is standing absolutely, not always' has the force of two but is one proposition.
3
Examine the exclusion of sterile moods
Some combinations of premises (e.g., two negative or particular premises) yield no necessary conclusion. These are called sterile moods and are excluded by the 'entailing' condition.
4
Examine the exclusion of equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawah)
This composition, like 'Zayd = Amr, Amr = Bakr' → 'Zayd = Bakr', requires an extraneous premise (transitivity of equality) to produce the conclusion. It fails the 'by itself' condition.
5
Examine the exclusion of unrelated premises
Premises like 'Every man is an animal' and 'Every stone is a body' do not share a middle term and entail no new single statement. They only entail each premise individually, thus fail the 'another statement' condition.
The objection and answer refine that 'another statement' must be a single new statement, not multiple.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي اصْطِلَاحِ المَنَاطِقَةِ مَا يُعْلَمُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مِنْ أَنَّهُ : قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا مُسْتَلْزِمٌ بِذَاتِهِ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ : - بِقَوْلِنَا : «مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا»: مَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ كَـ : القَضِيَّةِ الوَاحِدَةِ، وَلَوْ كَانَتْ مِنَ المُوَجَّهَاتِ المُرَكَّبَةِ ؛ نَحْرُ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ بِالإِطْلَاقِ لَا دَائِماً» ؛ لِأَنَّهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ فِي قُوَّةِ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ، لَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهَا اسْمُ القَضِيتَيْنِ . وَبِقَوْلِنَا : «مُسْتَلْزِم ... إلخ»: مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ كَذَلِكَ كَـ: الضُّرُوبِ العَقِيمَةِ ؛ لأَنَّهَا لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : «بِذَاتِهِ»؛ لِيَخْرُجَ : قِيَاسُ المُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ: «مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ مَحْمُولِ أُوْلَاهُمَا مَوْضُوعُ أُخْرَاهُمَا، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مِنْ مَادَّةِ المُسَاوَاةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدٌ مُسَاوٍ لِعَمْرِو ، وَعَمْرُو مُسَاوٍ لِبَكْرِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنِ اسْتَلْزَمَ قَوْلاً آخَرَ وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ مُسَاوٍ لِبَكْرِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ وَهِيَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : مُسَاوِي المُسَاوِي لِشَيْءٍ مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِ ذَلِكَ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : الإِنْسَانُ مُبَايِنُ لِلفَرَسِ، وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ : أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَايِنٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقَ أَنْ يُقَالَ : «مُبَايِنُ المُبَايِنِ لِشَيْءٍ مُبَايِنٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء». وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ» ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جِسْمُ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَإِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ اسْتِلْزَامَ الكُلِّ لِجُزْئِهِ . لَا يُقَالُ: التَّعْرِيفُ شَامِلٌ لِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَكْسِهِ ، فَيَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : قَدِ اعْتَبَرُوا فِي اللَّازِمِ الإِفْرَادَ؛ حَيْثُ قَالُوا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ، فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ قَوْلاً وَاحِداً، وَذَلِكَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلَيْنِ لَا قَوْلاً وَاحِداً ؛ لأَنَّ كُلَّ قَضِيَّةٍ مِنْهُ تَسْتَلْزِمُ عَكْسَهَا . وَأَورَدَ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الصُّغْرَى، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى، فَكُلُّ مِنْ هَذَيْنِ القِيَاسَيْنِ لَمْ يَسْتَلْزِمْ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَإِنَّمَا اسْتَلْزَمَ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ . وَأُجِيبَ بِأَوْجُهِ المُتَّجِهُ مِنْهَا : مَنَعُ أَنَّ نَحْوَ ذَلِكَ يُسَمَّى قِيَاساً ؛ لِفَسَادِ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِاتِّحَادِ طَرَفَيْهَا ، فَلَا تَغْفَلْ.
And in the terminology of the logicians, it is what is known from the author's words: it is a statement composed of propositions, entailing by itself another statement. It is not hidden that there are excluded: by our statement 'composed of propositions': what is not like that, such as a single proposition, even if it is from the compound modal propositions like 'Zayd is standing absolutely, not always' because although it has the force of two propositions, it is not called two propositions. And by our statement 'entailing... etc.': what is not like that, such as the sterile moods because they do not entail another statement. And we said 'by itself' to exclude equality syllogism, which is that which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, even if it is not from the matter of equality; like 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr'; because although it entails another statement 'Zayd is equal to Bakr', it is not by itself but through an extraneous premise which is in the example: 'the equal of something equal to something is equal to that something'; as evidenced by the failure of that in your saying 'Man is different from horse, and horse is different from rational'; it does not entail 'Man is different from rational', because it does not necessarily hold true that 'the different of something different from something is different from that something'. And we said 'another statement' to exclude like your saying 'Every man is an animal, and every stone is a body'; because it does not entail another statement, but rather entails one of its premises in the way the whole entails its part. It cannot be said: the definition includes that because it entails its converse, so it qualifies as entailing another statement. Because we say: they have considered in the entailed thing singularity; where they said 'another statement', so it must be a single statement, and that entails two statements not one; because each of its premises entails its converse. And it was objected that if we say 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is an animal', it produces the minor itself; and if we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal', it produces the major itself; so each of these two syllogisms did not entail another statement, but rather entailed one of its premises. And it was answered with the most appropriate of the ways: it is denied that such is called a syllogism due to the corruption of one of its premises by the identity of its two terms, so do not be heedless.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas in the terminology of logicians and then clarifies the definition by excluding four types of compositions that might be mistaken for qiyas. The definition: qiyas is a statement composed of propositions that, by itself, entails another statement. The author then explains each part of the definition to show what is excluded.

First, 'composed of propositions' excludes anything not composed of multiple propositions, like a single proposition, even if it implies two propositions in meaning (e.g., 'Zayd is standing absolutely, not always' has the force of two statements but is a single proposition).

Second, 'entailing' excludes 'sterile moods' (al-ḍurūb al-‘aqīmah) which do not produce a conclusion.

Third, 'by itself' excludes the 'equality syllogism' (qiyās al-musāwāh). This is a composition where the predicate of the first premise and subject of the second are linked by equality, e.g., 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr' entails 'Zayd is equal to Bakr', but this entailment requires an extraneous premise (the transitivity of equality). This is proven by the counterexample: 'Man is different from horse, and horse is different from rational' does not entail 'Man is different from rational' because difference is not transitive. This shows the entailment is not by the intrinsic logical structure of the premises but by an external principle.

Fourth, 'another statement' excludes compositions like 'Every man is an animal, and every stone is a body' where the two premises are unrelated and do not produce a new third statement; they only entail each premise individually (as a whole entails its part).

The author then addresses objections. Someone might object that the definition includes the example because the two premises together might entail the converse of each premise, producing new statements. But the reply is that 'another statement' means a single new statement, not multiple. The example of 'Every man is an animal, and every stone is a body' would entail two statements (the converse of each) not one. Another objection: syllogisms like 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is an animal' produce the minor premise itself, not a new statement. The answer is that such compositions are not called valid syllogisms because one premise is corrupted by having identical subject and predicate. This preserves the definition's precision.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyās)
قول مؤلف من قضايا (qawl mu’allaf min qaḍāyā)
مستلزم (mustalzim)
بذاته (bi-dhātihī)
قياس المساواة (qiyās al-musāwāh)
الضروب العقيمة (al-ḍurūb al-‘aqīmah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the 'equality syllogism' (qiyas al-musawah) not a valid qiyas in mantiq?
A) It is not composed of propositions.
B) It requires an extraneous premise to entail the conclusion.
C) It produces two conclusions instead of one.
D) It uses the same term in both premises.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism in mantiq must be composed of at least two propositions, yield a single new conclusion by its own logical form, and require no additional premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check if the following composition satisfies the mantiqi definition of qiyas: 'Every human is mortal. Every mortal is rational. Therefore every human is rational.' If it fails, specify which condition is violated. (It is valid.)
?
Examine the composition 'Zayd is the father of Amr. Amr is the father of Bakr. Therefore Zayd is the father of Bakr.' Why does this not count as a qiyas according to the definition? (Answer: It requires the external premise that fatherhood is transitive, so the entailment is not 'by itself'.)

The Reduction of Demonstration to Necessary Premises and the Definition of Awwaliyat
Answering an objection and defining awwaliyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a demonstration containing theoretical premises is still considered composed of necessary premises, and identify an example of awwaliyat.

Core Idea
Awwaliyat are self-evident truths requiring only understanding of terms; all demonstration rests on them.

The commentator addresses a potential objection: if demonstration is defined as composed of necessary premises, how can it include theoretical premises? He explains that theoretical premises must trace back to necessities. Then he defines awwaliyat, the first type of necessary premise, and clarifies its pronunciation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims the author's definition restricts demonstration to six necessary premises, but some demonstrations use theoretical premises. What is the apparent contradiction?
The objection is based on the apparent meaning of the matn.
2
Analyze the response
The commentator answers that theoretical premises ultimately reduce to necessities, so demonstration is effectively composed of necessities either 'in reality or in judgment' (hakikatan aw hukman). Reflect on what this means for the structure of proof.
This is a key point about the foundations of burhan.
3
Define awwaliyat
Awwaliyat are propositions that the mind accepts immediately upon conceptualizing the subject and predicate. Write the definition from the text in your own words.
Example: 'The whole is greater than the part.'
4
Examine an example
The text gives 'The whole is greater than the part' and 'One is half of two'. Verify that these are known without any proof beyond understanding the terms.
No sense experience or inference is needed.
5
Note the linguistic variation
Some vocalize as 'awlaiyat' (plural of awla), but the common pronunciation is 'awwaliyat'. Which one fits the meter? Why does this matter for the matn?
The commentator notes that the matn uses 'awwaliyat' because it matches the meter.
