How Definition and Subject Matter Define and Distinguish a Science
Commentary on the poet's lines explaining that definition and subject matter serve to identify and differentiate sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the functions of hadd and mawdu' in identifying and differentiating sciences, and apply the definition of subject matter to classify any Islamic science.

Core Idea
A science is known by its definition and distinguished by its subject matter.

This passage from the commentary on al-Bannani's poem explains two crucial elements of a science: its definition (hadd) and its subject matter (mawdu'). The definition tells you what questions belong to that science, preventing confusion with other fields. The subject matter, on the other hand, is what makes a science unique. The commentator gives vivid examples from grammar, medicine, rhetoric, fiqh, and more to show how sciences are differentiated either by their subject matter itself or by the aspect under which they study it. Understanding these two tools is essential for anyone who wants to navigate the classification of Islamic sciences with rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two functions
The passage opens by stating that 'by the definition one knows what he is seeking' and 'by the subject matter that science is distinguished from others.' Locate these two sentences in the Arabic and note the distinct roles: hadd identifies, mawdu' differentiates.
2
Analyze the differentiation of sciences
The commentator explains that all sciences are a single genus; they become distinct only through their subject matters. He provides two cases: essential distinction (e.g., grammar vs. medicine) and distinction by consideration (e.g., 'ilm al-ma'ani vs. 'ilm al-bayan). For each, identify what makes them separate sciences.
Essential distinction means different subject matters; distinction by consideration means the same subject matter but studied from different angles.
3
Extract the general definition of subject matter
The commentator defines the subject matter of any science as 'that in which is investigated its essential accidents.' Write this definition in your own words and identify the genus (ما يُبحث فيه) and differentia (عن عوارضه الذاتية).
This is a complete definition (hadd) because it includes both the broader category (the field of investigation) and the specific characteristic (essential accidents).
4
Apply the definition to the given examples
The passage gives three examples: fiqh (subject = actions of the legally responsible; essential accidents = obligation, prohibition, etc.), arithmetic (subject = numbers; essential accidents = addition, subtraction, etc.), and inheritance law (subject = estates; essential accidents = division, etc.). For each, verify that the subject matter fits the definition and note how the essential accidents are properties inherent to that subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فبالحَدٌ يَعْلَمُ مَا هُوَ سَاعِ فِي طَلَبِهِ) أي : فكلُّ مسألةٍ ترد عليه يعلم بالحد هل هي من ذلك العلم أم لا؟ فيأمَن مِن اختلاط بعض العلوم ببعض. قوله : (وَبِالمَوْضُوعِ يَمْتَازُ لَهُ ذَلِكَ العِلْمُ عَنْ غَيْرِهِ) وذلك أنَّ العلوم جنس واحد، وإنَّما تنوعت وتمايزت بتغاير الموضوعات، حتّى أنَّه لو لم يكن لعلم موضوع مغاير لموضوع علم آخر : بالذات كـ : موضوعي النحو والطب، وهما : اللفظ العربي بعد التركيب، وبدن الإنسان. أو بالاعتبار كـ موضوعي المعاني والبيان وهما اللفظ العربي المركب، لكنَّ الأوّل يبحث عنه من حيث المطابقة للحال، والثَّاني يبحث عنه من حيث تفاوته في وضوح الدلالة. لم يصح كونهما علمين وتعريفهما بتعريفين مختلفين. فتمايز العلوم بتمايز موضوعاتها، وموضوع كلِّ علم : «ما يُبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية»؛ مثلاً : موضوع علم الفقه : «أفعال المكلفين»؛ لأنَّه يُبحث فيه عن عوارضها الذاتية من وجوب وحرمة وغيرهما، وموضوع الحساب: «الأعداد» ؛ لأنَّه يُبحث فيه عن عوارضها الذاتية من جمع وطرح وضرب ،وغيرها وموضوع الفرائض: التّركات؛ لأنَّه يُبحث فيه عن عوارضها الذاتية من قسمةٍ وغيرها.
His statement: 'By the definition one knows what he is seeking in his pursuit' means: every question that arises for him, he knows by the definition whether it belongs to that science or not, so he is safe from mixing some sciences with others. His statement: 'And by the subject matter that science is distinguished from others' is because the sciences are a single genus; they only become diverse and distinct by the diversity of subject matters, such that if a science did not have a subject matter distinct from another science, either essentially like the subject matters of grammar and medicine, which are the Arabic word after composition and the human body, or by consideration like the subject matters of 'ilm al-ma'ani and 'ilm al-bayan, both being the composed Arabic word, but the first examines it regarding its conformity to the situation, and the second examines it regarding its variation in clarity of indication, they would not be valid as two sciences defined by two different definitions. Thus sciences are differentiated by the differentiation of their subject matters. The subject matter of every science is: 'that in which is investigated its essential accidents.' For example, the subject matter of fiqh is 'actions of the legally responsible individuals' because it investigates their essential accidents of obligation, prohibition, etc. The subject matter of arithmetic is 'numbers' because it investigates their essential accidents of addition, subtraction, multiplication, etc. The subject matter of inheritance law is 'estates' because it investigates their essential accidents of division, etc.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of a poem: 'By the definition one knows what he is seeking in his pursuit, and by the subject matter that science is distinguished from others.' The commentator explains that the definition of a science serves to identify whether a given question belongs to that science, thus preventing confusion between different sciences. The subject matter, on the other hand, differentiates each science from others. He clarifies that all sciences are a single genus; they only become distinct through their different subject matters. He gives examples: grammar and medicine have essentially different subject matters (Arabic word after composition vs. human body), while 'ilm al-ma'ani and 'ilm al-bayan have the same subject matter (composed Arabic word) but differ in the aspect under which they investigate it (conformity to situation vs. clarity of indication). Without such differentiation, they would not be considered two distinct sciences. He then defines the subject matter of any science as 'that in which its essential accidents are investigated' and illustrates with fiqh (actions of the legally responsible), arithmetic (numbers), and inheritance law (estates).

Key Terms
الحَدّ
المَوْضُوع
العَوارِض الذاتية

D) They would be considered a single science with no need for separate definitions.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a science tells you what it seeks, while its subject matter, defined as 'that in which essential accidents are investigated,' is what distinguishes it from all other sciences.

The Mutual Indispensability of Definition and Subject Matter
Objection and answer arguing that definition and subject matter are mutually indispensable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a science's definition and its subject matter are distinct yet complementary, and identify the subject matter within a given definition from an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Definition and subject matter are not interchangeable; each completes the other.

This passage tackles a common objection: since both definition and subject matter serve to distinguish a science, why not use just one? The answer reveals a key insight: they are distinct but linked. Understanding this relationship is essential for properly delimiting any Islamic science, from logic to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening statement: 'If it is said: Why not dispense with the definition in favor of the subject matter or the opposite?' Understand that the objector sees both as serving the same purpose (distinguishing the science) and thus questions the need for both.
2
Locate the answer's core claim
The response is: 'Each of them is distinct from the other, so one cannot dispense with the other.' Notice the emphasis on mutual distinction (mugheir).
3
Unpack the relationship terms
Analyze the terms 'tatimmat al-hadd' (completion of the definition) and 'al-muta'alliq' (the related). The subject matter is part of the perfection of the definition, and the definition is related to the subject matter. They are not identical.
The definition provides the perspective (investigation), while the subject matter provides the object of investigation.
4
Apply to the example of logic
The passage gives the definition of logic: 'The science investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters.' Identify the subject matter: 'conceptual and assenting matters.' See how the definition includes the subject matter but adds the investigation aspect.
5
Generalize to other Islamic sciences
Consider how this applies to other sciences like Usul al-Fiqh. Its definition is 'the knowledge of the proofs of fiqh in detail' – the subject matter is the proofs themselves. The definition gives the method (knowledge), while the subject matter specifies what is studied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هلا استغنى بالحدّ عن الموضوع أو العكس؛ إذ كلّ منهما المقصود منه تمييز العلم . قلنا : كلٌّ منهما مغاير للآخر ، فلا يُستغنى بأحدهما عن الآخر، فالموضوع مِن تتمة الحد، والحد متعلق به، والمتعلّق غير المتعلَّق وبيانُهُ أنَّ حدَّ هذا العِلم مثلاً : العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ عَنْ أَحْوَالِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ»، والأمورُ التَّصوريَّة والتصديقيَّة التي يقعُ البحثُ عن أحوالها هي الموضوع .
If it is said: Why not dispense with the definition in favor of the subject matter or the opposite, since each of them is intended to distinguish the science? We say: Each of them is distinct from the other, so one cannot dispense with the other. For the subject matter is part of the perfection of the definition, and the definition is related to it, and the related is not the same as that to which it is related. The explanation is that the definition of this science, for example, is: 'The science investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters.' And the conceptual and assenting matters whose conditions are investigated are the subject matter.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector asks: Since both the definition and the subject matter serve to distinguish a science, why cannot one of them suffice? The answer explains that they are different and mutually irreducible. The subject matter is part of the perfection (tammat) of the definition, meaning it is included in the definition but not identical to it. The definition depends on the subject matter, but the subject matter itself is distinct. The commentator illustrates with the definition of logic: 'The science investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters.' The conceptual and assenting matters that are investigated constitute the subject matter. Thus, the definition includes the subject matter but adds the aspect of investigation, making them complementary.

Key Terms
تتمة الحد
المتعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the subject matter is derived from the definition.

Key Takeaway
A science's definition and its subject matter are distinct but complementary: the subject matter is part of the definition's perfection, and the definition gives the perspective on the subject matter.

Hadd vs Rasm: The Two Definitions of Logic
Distinguishing hadd and rasm in defining logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an essential definition (hadd) and a descriptive definition (rasm) using the definition of logic as an example.

Core Idea
The essence of logic is investigating mental conditions; its goal of guiding thought is merely a description.

This passage presents two definitions of logic: one that captures its essence (hadd) and one that describes it by its goal (rasm). Understanding this distinction is crucial because many scholars confuse external descriptions with essential definitions. The commentary by al-Sa'd reinforces that defining by an external goal yields a rasm, not a hadd.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
In the Arabic, find the sentence starting with 'أما حده فهو' for the hadd, and the sentence starting with 'ورسمه' for the rasm. Note the different wording.
Hadd is introduced with 'أما حده', rasm with 'ورسمه'.
2
Identify genus and differentia in the hadd
The hadd says 'العلم الباحث عن أحوال...' The genus is 'science' (العلم). The differentia is 'investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters from the aspect of leading to the unknown'. Check if this captures the essence of logic.
3
Analyze the rasm definition
The rasm says 'علم يعرف به كيفية التوصل من المعلوم إلى المجهول'. This describes logic by its goal (gaining knowledge). The commentator explains that since the goal is external to the essence, this is a rasm.
Goal is external to the thing itself.
4
Understand the commentator's reasoning
The commentator cites al-Sa'd: 'التعريف بالخارج رسم' – defining by an external thing is a rasm. This is the key principle: a true hadd uses only internal, essential properties.

قوله : (فِي المَبَادِي المبادئ عند المناطقة: «ما تُبنى عليه مباحث العلم بحيث لا يمكن الشروع فيه بدونها، ولا يتحقق إلا بها». فَأَوَّلُ الأَبْوَابِ فِي المَبَادِي وَتِلْكَ عَشْرَةٌ عَلَى مُرَادِي الحَدُّ، وَالمَوضُوعُ ، ثُمَّ الوَاضِعْ، وَالاسْمُ ، الِاسْتِمْدَادُ [ب/ ٣] ، حُكْمُ الشَّارِعُ، تَصَوُّرُ المَسَائِلِ الفَضِيلَة وَنِسْبَةٌ، فَائِدَةٌ جَلِيلَة حَقٌّ عَلَى طَالِبِ عِلْمٍ أَنْ يُحِيطُ بِفَهُم ذِي العَشْرَةِ مَيْزُهَا يُنيط بِسَعْيِهِ) قَبَلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الطَّلَبْ بِهَا يَصِيرُ مُبْصِراً لِمَا طَلَبْ (۱) - أَمَّا حَدُّهُ فَهُوَ : العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ عَنْ أَحْوَالِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّأَدِّي بِهَا إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِي». وَرَسْمُهُ بِاعْتِبَارِ غَايَتِهِ هُوَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: عِلْمٌ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ التَّوَصُّلِ بِالمَعْلُومِ إِلَى المَجْهُولِ. قوله : (يُعْرَفُ بِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ التَّوَصُّلِ ... إلخ) فإنَّ كونه يعرف به كيفية التوصل هو غاية العلم، وغاية الشيء يكون خارجاً عنه، والتَّعريف بالخارج رسمٌ؛ قاله السعد.
His statement: 'Regarding the principles'—the principles according to the logicians are: 'that upon which the investigations of a science are built such that it is not possible to begin without them, nor can it be realized except through them.' So, 'The first of the chapters is on principles, and they are ten according to my intent: the definition, then the subject matter, then the founder, the name, the derivation, the ruling of the Lawgiver, the conceptualization of the problems, the virtue, the relation, a magnificent benefit.' It is the right of a seeker of knowledge to encompass the understanding of the ten; their distinction attaches to his effort before beginning in the pursuit, and by them he becomes insightful regarding what he seeks. As for its (logic's) definition (hadd), it is: 'The science investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters from the aspect of leading thereby to an unknown conceptual or assenting matter.' And its description (rasm) in consideration of its goal is that you say: 'A science by which is known the manner of proceeding from the known to the unknown.' His statement: 'By which is known the manner of proceeding...' etc., because its being known by which the manner of proceeding is the goal of the science, and the goal of a thing is external to it, and definition by an external is a description (rasm); al-Sa'd said that.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage begins with a definition of 'principles' (mabadi') as the foundation without which the science cannot be begun or realized. Then the poem lists ten such principles: definition, subject matter, founder, name, derivation, ruling of the Lawgiver, conceptualization of the problems, virtue, relation, and benefit. The commentator emphasizes that a student must understand these to become perceptive. Then he turns to the definition of logic itself. The first definition (hadd) is: 'The science investigating the conditions of conceptual and assenting matters in terms of leading to an unknown conceptual or assenting matter.' The second is a description (rasm) based on its goal: 'A science by which one knows the method of proceeding from the known to the unknown.' The commentator notes that since the goal is external to the science, defining by it constitutes a rasm, not a hadd, as stated by al-Sa'd.

Key Terms
المبادئ
الحد
الرسم

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator classify the second definition of logic as a rasm rather than a hadd?
A) Because it uses the term 'science' which is generic.
B) Because it defines logic by its goal, which is external to the essence.
C) Because it is a complete description of all its parts.
D) Because it is a verbal definition.

Key Takeaway
A true essential definition (hadd) must use only internal properties; a definition by an external goal is only a description (rasm).

Distinguishing Hadd from Rasm: The Definition of Logic
Author presents the hadd and rasm of logic, with an objection that the first is actually a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a proper hadd (essential definition) from a rasm (description) by evaluating whether the defining attributes are essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi), using the example of the definition of logic.

Core Idea
A definition that uses accidental properties is a rasm, not a hadd—a lesson every student of logic must internalize.

In this passage, the author presents two definitions of logic: one he claims is a hadd, the other a rasm. But a sharp objection from Shaykh al-Yāzghī reveals that the first is also a rasm. By understanding why, you will learn the critical distinction between essential and accidental definitions—a tool you will use throughout your study of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the hadd and rasm in the Arabic text. What are the precise wordings? Note that the author presents one as the hadd and another as the rasm.
2
Examine the first definition's components
Break down 'al-ʿilmu al-bāḥithu ʿan aḥwāl al-maʿlūmāt...' into its genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). What is the genus? What is the differentia?
The genus is 'science' (ʿilm).
3
Apply Shaykh al-Yāzghī's objection
He argues that 'being an investigator' is an accidental property, not an essential differentia. Why is that? What would a proper differentia look like? The commentator suggests 'rules and canons' (qawāʿid wa ḍawābiṭ).
4
Evaluate the second definition
The rasm uses purpose: 'a science by which is known the modality of reaching from known to unknown.' Why is defining by purpose considered a rasm? Because the purpose is external to the essence.
Al-Saʿd is cited to support that definition by purpose is a rasm.
5
Conclude the classification
Both definitions given are actually rasm: the first because it uses an accidental attribute, the second because it uses an external purpose. A true hadd would use essential (dhati) attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَوَّلُ الأَبْوَابِ فِي المَبَادِي وَتِلْكَ عَشْرَةٌ عَلَى مُرَادِي الحَدُّ، وَالمَوضُوعُ ، ثُمَّ الوَاضِعْ ، وَالاسْمُ ، الِاسْتِمْدَادُ [ب/ ٣] ، حُكْمُ الشَّارِعُ تَصَوُّرُ المَسَائِلِ الفَضِيلَة وَنِسْبَةٌ، فَائِدَةٌ جَلِيلَة حَقٌّ عَلَى طَالِبِ عِلْمٍ أَنْ يُحِيطُ بِفَهُم ذِي العَشْرَةِ مَيْزُهَا يُنيط بِسَعْيِهِ) قَبَلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الطَّلَبْ بِهَا يَصِيرُ مُبْصِراً لِمَا طَلَبْ (۱) - أَمَّا حَدُّهُ فَهُوَ : العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ عَنْ أَحْوَالِ المَعْلُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّأَدِّي بِهَا إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ تَصَوُّرِيٌّ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِي». وَرَسْمُهُ بِاعْتِبَارِ غَايَتِهِ هُوَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: عِلْمٌ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ كَيفِيَّةُ التَّوَصُّلِ بِالمَعْلُومِ إِلَى المَجْهُولِ، وَبِعُمُوم لَفْظِ المَعْلُومِ وَالمَجْهُولِ يَخْرُجُ مِنْهُ : عِلْمُ الحِسَابِ . قوله : (فِي المَبَادِي المبادئ عند المناطقة: «ما تُبنى عليه مباحث العلم بحيث لا يمكن الشروع فيه بدونها، ولا يتحقق إلا بها». قوله : (يُنيط) يحتمل أن يقرأ بفتح الياء» أو بضمها ، وعلى كلّ فيه إشكال؛ لأنَّه إن قرئ بضم الياء» كان من أناطه الرباعي، ولم يستعمل، وإن فتح كان من ناط» الثلاثي. وإذا كان كذلك كان من حقه أن يقول : «ينوط» ؛ لأنَّه واوي العين كـ : «قال، يقول»؛ قاله شيخنا اليازغي. وينيط» بمعنى : يعلق ؛ معطوف على يحيط»، و«ميزها معمول «ينيط» قدم عليه . قوله : (العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ) أي : المبحوث فيه . اعترضه شيخنا اليازغي : بأنَّه رسمٌ لا حد ؛ إذ كونه باحثاً عن أحوال المعلومات - أي: مبحوث فيه - عَرَضُ مِن عرضيَّاته، وليس من ذائيَّات العلم، فلو أراد حده لقال : قواعد وضوابط يعرف بها أحوال . . . إلخ». قوله : (يُعْرَفُ بِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ التَّوَصُّل ... إلخ) فإنَّ كونه يعرف به كيفية التوصل هو غاية العلم، وغاية الشَّيء يكون خارجاً عنه، والتَّعريف بالخارج رسم ؛ قاله السعد (٢) .
So the first of the chapters is on the principles, and those are ten according to my intent: the definition, the subject, then the positor, and the name, the derivation, the ruling of the Lawgiver, the conceptualization of the excellent issues, and a relation, a great benefit. It is incumbent upon a student of knowledge to encompass with understanding these ten, their distinction is attached to his effort before beginning in seeking; by them he becomes insightful for what he sought. (1) As for its definition (hadd), it is: the science that investigates the states of conceptual and assenting cognitions, in terms of leading by them to an unknown conceptual or assenting. And its description (rasm) by consideration of its purpose is that you say: A science by which is known the modality of reaching from the known to the unknown, and by the generality of the expression 'known' and 'unknown' there exits from it the science of arithmetic. His statement: (fī al-mabādī) the principles according to the logicians are: 'that upon which the investigations of a science are built such that it is not possible to begin without them, nor is it realized except by them.' His statement: (yunīṭu) it is possible to read with the fath of the yā' or with its damm, and in either case there is a problem; because if read with damm of the yā', it is from the quadrilateral 'anāṭa' and it is not used, and if with fath it is from the triliteral 'nāṭa'. And if it is so, it would be proper to say 'yanūṭu' because it is wāwī al-ʿayn like 'qāla, yaqūlu'; said by our shaykh al-Yāzghī. And 'yunīṭu' meaning 'attaches'; it is conjoined with 'yuḥīṭu', and 'mayyazahā' is the object of 'yunīṭu' advanced upon it. His statement: (al-ʿilmu al-bāḥithu) i.e., that which is investigated. Our shaykh al-Yāzghī objected to it: that it is a rasm not a hadd; since its being an investigator of the states of information – i.e., investigated – is an accident among its accidents, and not from the essentials of the science. So if he intended its hadd, he would have said: 'Rules and canons by which are known the states... etc.' His statement: (yuʿrafu bihi kayfiyyat al-tawasṣul... etc.) For its being that by which is known the modality of reaching is the purpose of the science, and the purpose of a thing is external to it, and definition by what is external is a rasm; said by al-Saʿd (2).
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents the hadd (definition) and rasm (description) of logic, along with a critical objection from Shaykh al-Yāzghī. The author begins by listing ten principles that a student must understand before studying logic. Then he gives the hadd of logic: 'the science that investigates the states of conceptual and assenting cognitions in terms of leading to an unknown conceptual or assenting.' This is followed by a rasm considering its purpose: 'a science by which is known the modality of reaching from the known to the unknown.' The commentary explains that the term 'mabādī' (principles) refers to foundational matters without which the science cannot be pursued. Then Shaykh al-Yāzghī objects that the initial statement is actually a rasm, not a hadd, because 'being an investigator' is an accidental property, not a differentia. A true hadd would use essential attributes like 'rules and canons.' The commentator supports this by noting that defining something by its purpose (which is external) also yields a rasm, as al-Saʿd stated. The example of arithmetic being excluded by the generality of 'known' and 'unknown' illustrates the rasm's precision.

الرَّسْم
العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ
المَبَادِي

A) Because it uses an accidental property instead of an essential differentia.

C) Because it includes arithmetic, which is a separate science.
D) Because it defines logic by its purpose rather than its essence.

Key Takeaway

The Definition of Logic as a Canonical Tool
Refutation of objection against logic's rasm using arithmetic as counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the science of arithmetic is excluded from the definition of logic as a tool, using the distinction between particular and general knowns.

Core Idea
Logic's tool definition uses general knowns and unknowns, excluding particular sciences like arithmetic.

The commentator addresses an objection: arithmetic seems to fit the definition of logic as a science that leads from known to unknown. But he shows that logic uses general principles, while arithmetic uses particular ones. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says arithmetic fits the rasm because it moves from known to unknown (e.g., from known numbers to unknown sums).
2
Extract the reply
The commentator says the rasm uses 'general' known and unknown, not particular. Arithmetic uses particular numbers; logic uses universal rules.
3
Apply the distinction to another science
Consider fiqh: the general principle 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is a general known that applies to particular unknowns (e.g., a new beverage). This is logic's domain.
Fiqh uses general rules, so it fits logic's tool definition.
4
Test your understanding
Now, think of a science that uses only particular knowns. Is grammar like logic or arithmetic? Grammar rules are general, so it is like logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَبِعُمُومِ لَفْظِ المَعْلُومِ ... إلخ) أشار به لردّ ما أوردوه على هذا الحد من لزوم فساد الطرد بدخول علم الحساب. وحاصل ما أجاب به : أنَّ عموم المعلوم والمجهول في رسم المنطق يخرج علم الحساب ؛ لأنَّه يتوصل فيه بمعلوم خاص إلى مجهول خاص. قوله : (وَبِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ آلَةٌ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الرَّسم الأوّل مبني على أنَّه علم، ويدلُّ له قوله فيما تقدَّم: «عِلْمٌ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ»، وفي ذلك كله إشارة إلى الخلاف الواقع في المنطق هل هو علم أو آلة؟ فقيل : إِنَّه عِلْمٌ مِنَ العلوم وقيل : إنَّه آلةٌ لغيره ؛ لأنَّه يتوصَّل به إلى غيره مِنَ العلوم. والتحقيق : أنَّه علمٌ في نفسه آلة لغيره، فيكون الخلاف لفظيا؛ كما نص عليه الفخر . قوله : (وَهُوَ : الصَّابِطُ ) أي : القاعدة الكلية ، وهو : أمرٌ كلّيّ ينطبق على أحكام جزئيات موضوعاته» . و احترز بقوله : «قانونيَّة عن الآلة الحسيَّة كـ المنشار والقدوم؛ لأنَّه جزئيَّةٌ؛ إذ كلُّ محسوس : خارجاً جزئي. لا يقال : المنشار ونحوه كلي؛ لصدقه على كثيرين، فلا يخرج بقوله: «قانونية». لأنَّا نقول : الَّذي يصدق على كثيرين مفهومه، وليس هو آلةٌ لشيء، والآلهُ إِنَّما هي مصدوقه، فالفرقُ ظاهر . قوله : (وَأَصْلُ القَانُونِ بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ : المِسْطَرَةُ») أي: آلة التّسطير، وهي معلومةٌ، فنقل اللفظ عن معناه الأصلي، وجعل مرادفاً للضابط.
His statement: (and by the generality of the expression 'known'... etc.) He indicated by it a refutation of what they raised against this definition of the necessary corruption of universality by the entry of the science of arithmetic. The summary of what he answered is: that the generality of the known and the unknown in the description (rasm) of logic excludes the science of arithmetic; because in it one reaches from a particular known to a particular unknown. His statement: (and by consideration that it is a tool) in it is an indication that the first description (rasm) is based on its being a science, and it is evidenced by his earlier statement: 'a science by which is known', and in all that is an indication of the disagreement occurring concerning logic: is it a science or a tool? For it was said: It is a science among sciences, and it was said: It is a tool for others; because one reaches through it to other sciences. And the verified position: It is a science in itself and a tool for others, thus the disagreement is verbal; as al-Fakhr stated. His statement: (and it is the controller) i.e., the universal rule, and it is a universal matter that applies to the rulings of the particulars of its subjects. And he guarded by his statement 'canonical' against the sensory tool like the saw and the adze; because it is particular; since every sensory thing externally is particular. It cannot be said: the saw and the like are universal, because they apply to many, so they are not excluded by his statement 'canonical'. Because we say: that which applies to many is its concept, and it is not a tool for anything, the tools are only their instantiations, so the difference is apparent. His statement: (and the origin of 'qānūn' in Greek is 'ruler') i.e., the tool for ruling lines, and it is known, so the word was transferred from its original meaning and made synonymous with 'al-ḍābiṭ'.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage expands on the rasm of logic by considering its tool-like nature. The commentator first addresses a counterexample: arithmetic appears to fit the rasm 'science by which one reaches from known to unknown' but is excluded because it uses a particular known to reach a particular unknown, while logic uses general knowns and unknowns. Then the commentator explains that the phrase 'by consideration that it is a tool' indicates a second rasm based on logic being a tool. This leads to discussing the disagreement: some say logic is a science, others say it is a tool. The verified view is that it is both: a science in itself (since it has its own subject matter) and a tool for other sciences (since it helps in deriving knowledge). Thus the disagreement is merely verbal, as al-Fakhr al-Din al-Razi stated. The commentator then clarifies the term 'qānūniyya' (canonical) in the tool definition: it distinguishes logic from physical tools like a saw or adze, which are particular sensory objects. One might object that the concept of a saw is universal, but the tool itself is the particular instantiation, not the concept. Finally, the origin of the word 'qānūn' is traced to Greek for 'ruler' (a straightedge), used metaphorically for a rule or canon.

Key Terms
آلَة
قَانُونِيَّة
رَسْم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator refute the objection that arithmetic fits the rasm of logic?
A) By stating that arithmetic uses a particular known to reach a particular unknown, while logic uses general knowns and unknowns.
B) By arguing that arithmetic is not a science of cognition.
C) By claiming that arithmetic is included in logic.
D) By saying that arithmetic does not involve conceptual or assenting cognitions.

Key Takeaway
Logic's rasm as a tool uses general knowns and unknowns, distinguishing it from particular sciences like arithmetic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqh principle 'al-umūru bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are judged by intentions). Is this a general known or particular known? How does it help reach unknown rulings? Explain why it fits logic's tool definition, unlike arithmetic.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Is Logic a Science or a Tool The Verbal Dispute
Presentation and resolution of the dispute over logic being a science or tool.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on logic (science, tool, both) and explain why the disagreement is verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute over logic being a science or tool is resolved by saying it is both, making the disagreement merely verbal.

Scholars debated whether logic is an independent science or merely a tool for other sciences. The commentator, following al-Razi, shows that it is both, so the conflict is only apparent. This lesson walks through the positions and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Some say logic is a science among sciences (it has its own subject matter). Others say it is a tool for other sciences (it helps attain them).
2
Understand the reasoning for each
As a science: logic studies the forms of thought. As a tool: it is used to derive knowledge in fields like kalam and fiqh.
3
Find the third view
The verified view: logic is a science in itself (it has a subject) and a tool for others (it is used to gain other sciences).
4
Assess why the dispute is verbal
Both sides agree on its dual nature; the difference is only in naming. Al-Razi says it is verbal, meaning the disagreement is about words, not reality.

قوله : (وَبِعُمُومِ لَفْظِ المَعْلُومِ ... إلخ) أشار به لردّ ما أوردوه على هذا الحد من لزوم فساد الطرد بدخول علم الحساب. وحاصل ما أجاب به : أنَّ عموم المعلوم والمجهول في رسم المنطق يخرج علم الحساب ؛ لأنَّه يتوصل فيه بمعلوم خاص إلى مجهول خاص. قوله : (وَبِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ آلَةٌ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الرَّسم الأوّل مبني على أنَّه علم، ويدلُّ له قوله فيما تقدَّم: «عِلْمٌ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ»، وفي ذلك كله إشارة إلى الخلاف الواقع في المنطق هل هو علم أو آلة؟ فقيل : إِنَّه عِلْمٌ مِنَ العلوم وقيل : إنَّه آلةٌ لغيره ؛ لأنَّه يتوصَّل به إلى غيره مِنَ العلوم. والتحقيق : أنَّه علمٌ في نفسه آلة لغيره، فيكون الخلاف لفظيا؛ كما نص عليه الفخر . قوله : (وَهُوَ : الصَّابِطُ ) أي : القاعدة الكلية ، وهو : أمرٌ كلّيّ ينطبق على أحكام جزئيات موضوعاته» . و احترز بقوله : «قانونيَّة عن الآلة الحسيَّة كـ المنشار والقدوم؛ لأنَّه جزئيَّةٌ؛ إذ كلُّ محسوس : خارجاً جزئي. لا يقال : المنشار ونحوه كلي؛ لصدقه على كثيرين، فلا يخرج بقوله: «قانونية». لأنَّا نقول : الَّذي يصدق على كثيرين مفهومه، وليس هو آلةٌ لشيء، والآلهُ إِنَّما هي مصدوقه، فالفرقُ ظاهر . قوله : (وَأَصْلُ القَانُونِ بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ : المِسْطَرَةُ») أي: آلة التّسطير، وهي معلومةٌ، فنقل اللفظ عن معناه الأصلي، وجعل مرادفاً للضابط.
His statement: (and by the generality of the expression 'known'... etc.) He indicated by it a refutation of what they raised against this definition of the necessary corruption of universality by the entry of the science of arithmetic. The summary of what he answered is: that the generality of the known and the unknown in the description (rasm) of logic excludes the science of arithmetic; because in it one reaches from a particular known to a particular unknown. His statement: (and by consideration that it is a tool) in it is an indication that the first description (rasm) is based on its being a science, and it is evidenced by his earlier statement: 'a science by which is known', and in all that is an indication of the disagreement occurring concerning logic: is it a science or a tool? For it was said: It is a science among sciences, and it was said: It is a tool for others; because one reaches through it to other sciences. And the verified position: It is a science in itself and a tool for others, thus the disagreement is verbal; as al-Fakhr stated. His statement: (and it is the controller) i.e., the universal rule, and it is a universal matter that applies to the rulings of the particulars of its subjects. And he guarded by his statement 'canonical' against the sensory tool like the saw and the adze; because it is particular; since every sensory thing externally is particular. It cannot be said: the saw and the like are universal, because they apply to many, so they are not excluded by his statement 'canonical'. Because we say: that which applies to many is its concept, and it is not a tool for anything, the tools are only their instantiations, so the difference is apparent. His statement: (and the origin of 'qānūn' in Greek is 'ruler') i.e., the tool for ruling lines, and it is known, so the word was transferred from its original meaning and made synonymous with 'al-ḍābiṭ'.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage expands on the rasm of logic by considering its tool-like nature. The commentator first addresses a counterexample: arithmetic appears to fit the rasm 'science by which one reaches from known to unknown' but is excluded because it uses a particular known to reach a particular unknown, while logic uses general knowns and unknowns. Then the commentator explains that the phrase 'by consideration that it is a tool' indicates a second rasm based on logic being a tool. This leads to discussing the disagreement: some say logic is a science, others say it is a tool. The verified view is that it is both: a science in itself (since it has its own subject matter) and a tool for other sciences (since it helps in deriving knowledge). Thus the disagreement is merely verbal, as al-Fakhr al-Din al-Razi stated. The commentator then clarifies the term 'qānūniyya' (canonical) in the tool definition: it distinguishes logic from physical tools like a saw or adze, which are particular sensory objects. One might object that the concept of a saw is universal, but the tool itself is the particular instantiation, not the concept. Finally, the origin of the word 'qānūn' is traced to Greek for 'ruler' (a straightedge), used metaphorically for a rule or canon.

Key Terms
عِلْم
آلَة
خِلَاف لَفْظِيّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the verified position regarding logic being a science or a tool?
A) Logic is only a science.
B) Logic is only a tool.
C) Logic is a science in itself and a tool for others.
D) The dispute is unresolved.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over logic being a science or tool is resolved by affirming both natures, making it a verbal disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
State the three positions on the nature of logic in your own words. Then, using the example of a proof for the temporality of the world, explain how logic functions both as a science (studying the form of the proof) and as a tool (used to construct the proof).

The Meaning of Qānūniyya: Distinguishing Intellectual from Physical Tools
Clarification of 'qānūniyya' and distinction from physical tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic's tool is called 'canonical' and how it differs from physical tools like a saw.

Core Idea
Logic is a canonical (qānūniyya) tool because it is a universal rule, not a particular physical object.

The term 'qānūniyya' in the definition of logic as a tool distinguishes it from sensory tools. This lesson clarifies the meaning and the reason for this distinction, including the etymology of 'qānūn'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the word 'qānūn'
'Qānūn' means a universal rule or canon. Originally from Greek 'kanon' meaning ruler (straightedge).
The commentator notes the Greek origin: al-misṭarah (ruler).
2
Recognize the problem with physical tools
Physical tools like a saw are particular objects. Even if the concept is universal, the tool itself is a specific instantiation.
3
See how 'qānūniyya' solves it
Logic is not a particular object but a universal rule that applies to many subjects of thought. It is a 'canonical' tool, i.e., a rule-based instrument.
4
Contrast with a physical tool
A saw is a particular object; its concept is universal but the actual saw is particular. Logic as a tool is the universal rule itself, not a particular instantiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Defining Logic as a Qanuniyya Tool
Commentary defends the definition of logic against objection by explaining 'qanuniyya' and the dual nature of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of logic as a qanuniyya tool and defend it against the objection that it includes arithmetic.

Core Idea
Logic is a universal tool that protects the mind, distinct from both particular tools and particular sciences.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the definition of logic as a 'qanuniyya tool' and addresses a key objection: does this definition mistakenly include arithmetic? By understanding how the terms 'known' and 'unknown' are used, and by distinguishing between a universal rule and a particular instrument, you will see how logic maintains its unique role. The commentary also clarifies the long-standing debate: is logic a science or a tool? The answer reveals its dual nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's components
The definition states: 'It is a legalistic tool, the observance of which protects the mind from error in its thinking.' Break it down: genus = tool (āla), differentia = legalistic (qānūniyya) and the property of protecting the mind. Note that 'tool' here is an intermediary, like a saw for a carpenter.
The term 'qānūniyya' is derived from 'qānūn', meaning a universal rule or regulator.
2
Understand the objection about arithmetic
Critics argued that the definition 'a tool that leads from the known to the unknown' also applies to arithmetic, so it fails to be exclusive (tard fāsid). Recognize the problem: arithmetic also moves from known premises to unknown conclusions, so logic would unjustly include it.
This objection tests the definition's completeness – it must exclude all non-logical disciplines.
3
Analyze the response using generality of 'known' and 'unknown'
The commentator responds that the definition uses 'the known' and 'the unknown' in a universal sense (ma'lūm muṭlaq and majhūl muṭlaq), whereas arithmetic uses particular knowns and unknowns. Logic deals with the general forms of thought, not specific content. Thus arithmetic is excluded.
This distinction shows that logic's subject matter is more abstract and universal than particular sciences.
4
Grasp the dual nature of logic as science and tool
The commentator notes a disagreement: is logic a science (ʿilm) or a tool (āla)? The verified view is that it is both: a science in itself (because it has its own subject matter and proofs) and a tool for other sciences (because it provides the method for correct reasoning). The dispute is verbal.
This dual nature explains why logic is studied both as a discipline and as a prerequisite for other Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِلَى المَجْهُولِ، وَبِعُمُوم لَفْظِ المَعْلُومِ وَالمَجْهُولِ يَخْرُجُ مِنْهُ : عِلْمُ الحِسَابِ . وَبِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ آلَةٌ تَقُولُ: «هُوَ : آلَةٌ قَانُونِيَّةٌ تَعْصُمُ مُرَاعَاتُهَا الذِّهْنَ عَن الخَطَأ فِي فِكْرِهِ»، وَالآلَةُ هِيَ الوَاسِطَةُ بَيْنَ الفَاعِلِ وَالمُنْفَعِلِ فِي وُصُولِ أَثَرِهِ إِلَيْهِ كَـ : المِنْشَارِ لِلنَّجَارِ، وَقَانُونِيَّةٌ) نِسْبَةٌ إِلَى قَوَانِينَ جَمْعُ: «قَانُونِ وَهُوَ : الضَّابِط؛ لأنَّ النَّسَبَ إِلَى الجَمْعِ يُرَدُّ إِلَى الوَاحِدِ، كَمَا قَالَ فِي «الخُلَاصَةِ» : وَالوَاحِدَ اذْكُرْ نَاسِباً لِلْجَمْعِ وَأَصْلُ القَانُونِ» بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ : المِسْطَرَةُ». ... إلخ قوله : (وَبِعُمُومِ لَفْظِ المَعْلُومِ ... إلخ) أشار به لردّ ما أوردوه على هذا الحد من لزوم فساد الطرد بدخول علم الحساب. وحاصل ما أجاب به : أنَّ عموم المعلوم والمجهول في رسم المنطق يخرج علم الحساب ؛ لأنَّه يتوصل فيه بمعلوم خاص إلى مجهول خاص. قوله : (وَبِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ آلَةٌ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الرَّسم الأوّل مبني على أنَّه علم، ويدلُّ له قوله فيما تقدَّم: «عِلْمٌ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ»، وفي ذلك كله إشارة إلى الخلاف الواقع في المنطق هل هو علم أو آلة؟ فقيل : إِنَّه عِلْمٌ مِنَ العلوم وقيل : إنَّه آلةٌ لغيره ؛ لأنَّه يتوصَّل به إلى غيره مِنَ العلوم. والتحقيق : أنَّه علمٌ في نفسه آلة لغيره، فيكون الخلاف لفظيا؛ كما نص عليه الفخر . قوله : (وَهُوَ : الصَّابِطُ ) أي : القاعدة الكلية ، وهو : أمرٌ كلّيّ ينطبق على أحكام جزئيات موضوعاته» . و احترز بقوله : «قانونيَّة عن الآلة الحسيَّة كـ المنشار والقدوم؛ لأنَّه جزئيَّةٌ؛ إذ كلُّ محسوس : خارجاً جزئي. لا يقال : المنشار ونحوه كلي؛ لصدقه على كثيرين، فلا يخرج بقوله: «قانونية». لأنَّا نقول : الَّذي يصدق على كثيرين مفهومه، وليس هو آلةٌ لشيء، والآلهُ إِنَّما هي مصدوقه، فالفرقُ ظاهر . قوله : (وَأَصْلُ القَانُونِ بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ : المِسْطَرَةُ») أي: آلة التّسطير، وهي معلومةٌ، فنقل اللفظ عن معناه الأصلي، وجعل مرادفاً للضابط.
To the unknown, and by the generality of the term 'known' and 'unknown', the science of arithmetic is excluded from it. And considering that it is a tool, he said: 'It is a legalistic tool, the observance of which protects the mind from error in its thinking.' The tool is the intermediary between the agent and the recipient in conveying its effect to it, like the saw for the carpenter. 'Qanuniyya' is a relative adjective to 'qawanin', plural of 'qanun', which is the regulator; because the relative attribution to the plural is referred back to the singular, as stated in Al-Khulasa: 'Mention the singular when attributing to the plural.' The origin of 'qanun' in Greek is 'the ruler'. ... His statement: 'And by the generality of the term "known"... etc.' He alluded by that to refute what they objected to this definition, of the necessary corruption of universality (tard) by the inclusion of arithmetic. The summary of what he answered is: the generality of known and unknown in the nominal definition (rasm) of logic excludes arithmetic; because in arithmetic one arrives from a particular known to a particular unknown. His statement: 'And considering that it is a tool' contains an indication that the first definition (rasm) is based on it being a science, and his previous statement 'a science by which one knows' indicates that. And in all that is an indication of the disagreement occurring regarding logic: is it a science or a tool? It was said: it is one of the sciences, and it was said: it is a tool for others; because it leads to other sciences. The verified position is that it is a science in itself and a tool for others, so the disagreement is verbal, as stated by al-Fakhr. His statement: 'And it is the regulator' means the universal rule, which is a universal matter that applies to the rulings of the particulars of its subjects. And by his statement 'qanuniyya' he excepted the sensory tool like the saw and the axe, because they are particular; since everything sensory external is particular. It cannot be said: the saw and its like are universal because they are true of many, so they are not excluded by his statement 'qanuniyya'. Because we say: that which is true of many is its concept, and it is not a tool for anything, and the tool is only its instances, so the difference is apparent. His statement: 'And the origin of qanun in Greek is the ruler' means the tool for ruling, which is known, so the word was transferred from its original meaning and made synonymous with regulator.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic as a 'qanuniyya tool'. The author begins by responding to an objection that the definition might wrongly include arithmetic, arguing that the generality of 'known' and 'unknown' excludes it because arithmetic uses particular knowns to particular unknowns. Then he discusses the definition itself: logic is a tool (ala) that protects the mind from error. He clarifies that 'tool' here is an intermediary, like a saw for a carpenter. The term 'qanuniyya' is derived from 'qanun', which means a universal rule (dabit). He then addresses a disagreement: is logic a science or a tool? The verified view is that it is both: a science in itself and a tool for others, making the dispute merely verbal. Next, he explains that 'qanuniyya' excludes physical tools like saws because they are particular, not universal. The concept of a saw is universal, but the actual tool is particular; the definition refers to the concept. Finally, he notes the Greek origin of 'qanun' as 'ruler' (measuring instrument), transferred to mean 'regulator'. The commentary uses examples from carpentry and a grammatical rule from Al-Khulasa to illustrate points.

The Metaphor of Protection in Logic
Analogy between logician and grammarian regarding error from neglecting rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between the logician and the grammarian regarding rule observance and error, and identify the metaphorical attribution of protection.

Core Idea
Logic protects the mind only when its rules are observed; neglect leads to error.

The commentator draws a vivid analogy: just as a grammarian falls into grammatical error (lahn) when he neglects grammatical rules, so too the logician falls into logical error when he neglects the rules of logic. This metaphor shows that the 'protection' attributed to logic is not intrinsic but conditional upon proper observance. Understanding this helps us grasp the practical necessity of logical rules for sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Notice that the commentator compares the logician to the grammarian. Both have a set of rules that, if neglected, lead to error. Find the exact wording in the Arabic that makes this comparison.
The phrase 'as the grammarian falls into grammatical error when he neglects his rules' (كما يقع اللحن للنحوي إذا أهمل قواعده) is key.
2
Understand the consequence of neglecting rules
The text states that error (al-khata') occurs when the logician does not observe (mura'a) the rules of logic. This implies that logic does not magically prevent errors; it requires active application.
3
Recognize the metaphorical attribution
Protection (al-'isma) is attributed to observance (al-mura'a) itself. This is a metaphorical attribution because the rules themselves are the source of protection, but only when enacted. The commentator clarifies this to avoid misunderstanding.
The Arabic says: 'And protection was attributed to observance' (وأسندت العصمة للمراعاة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُسْنِدَتِ العِصْمَةُ لِلْمُرَاعَاةِ ; لِوُقُوع الخَطَأ لِلْمَنْطِقِيِّ عِنْدَ عَدَم مُرَاعَاةِ قَوَاعِدِهِ؛ كَمَا يَقَعُ اللَّحْنُ لِلْنَّحَوِيِّ إِذَا أَهْمَلَ قَوَاعِدَهُ. وَالذَّهْنُ : «قُوَّةٌ لِلنَّفْسِ مُعِدَّةٌ لِاكْتِسَابِ العُلُومِ ، وَالفِكْرُ هُوَ : «النَّظَرُ» كَمَا لِلْسَّعْدِ. - وَأَمَّا مَوْضُوعُ المَنْطِقِ ، فَهُوَ : المَعْلُومَاتُ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةُ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ مِنْ تَصَوُّرٍ أَوْ تَصْدِيقٍ، لَا مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ؛ لِأَنَّ مَوْضُوعَ كُلِّ عِلْمٍ مَا يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنْ عَوَارِضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ ، وَالمَنْطِقُ إِنَّمَا يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنْ ذَلِكَ . قوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ) احترز بذلك عن : البحث عنها من حيثية أخرى غير الإيصال كـ: كون هذا المعلوم موجوداً في الخارج أو لا، قديماً أو حادثاً، إلى غير ذلك؛ فالمنطقُ إِنَّما يُبحث فيه عن الأحوال النّصوريَّة والتصديقيَّة من حيث الإيصال فقط : فالتَّصوُّر :كـ كونه حدا أو رسماً، وكونه جنساً أو فصلاً أو خاصَّةً يتركَّب مِنَ ذلك الحدِّ أوِ الرَّسم . والتَّصديقُ مِن حيث كونه حجّةٌ، ومن حيث كونه قضيَّةٌ، وعكس قضيَّة، ونقيض قضيَّة، فيؤلف ذلك الحجة . . . وهكذا .
And protection was attributed to observance because the logician falls into error when he does not observe its rules, just as the grammarian falls into grammatical error when he neglects his rules. The mind is a faculty of the soul prepared for acquiring sciences, and thinking is reflection, as per al-Sa'd. As for the subject matter of logic, it is the conceptual and verificational cognitions from the perspective that they lead to unknown matters, whether conceptual or verificational, not from the perspective of themselves; because the subject matter of every science is that in which it investigates its essential accidents, and logic only investigates that. His statement: 'From the perspective that they lead to an unknown matter' excludes investigation from other perspectives such as whether this known exists externally or not, whether it is eternal or originated, etc.; thus logic only investigates the conceptual and verificational states from the perspective of leading only: the conceptual, like being a definition or a description, and being a genus or a differentia or a property that composes that definition or description. And the verificational, from the perspective of being an argument, and from the perspective of being a proposition, and the converse of a proposition, and the contradictory of a proposition, so that the argument is composed, and so on.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary by first explaining the metaphorical attribution of protection to observance, noting that error occurs when the logician neglects the rules, similar to a grammarian. It then defines the subject matter of logic: conceptual and verificational cognitions considered as means to an unknown (tasawwur or tasdiq), not in themselves. This is because each science investigates the essential accidents of its subject. Logic only investigates those states that are relevant to leading to the unknown. For conceptual cognitions, it investigates whether something is a definition or a description, a genus, differentia, property, etc. For verificational cognitions, it investigates whether something is a proposition, its converse, contradiction, and how these compose an argument. The commentary clarifies this by contrasting with other perspectives like existence, eternalness, etc., which are not within logic's scope.

مُرَاعَاة (observance)
خَطَأ (error)
لَحْن (grammatical error)

What Is Mantiq?

Subject Matter of Logic: Cognitions as Means to the Unknown
Definition of the subject matter of logic and its delimitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain that the subject matter of logic is conceptual and verificational cognitions specifically from the perspective of leading to an unknown, and distinguish it from other perspectives.

Core Idea
Logic studies cognitions only as instruments to reach the unknown, not in themselves.

After clarifying the metaphorical protection, the commentator defines the subject matter (mawdu') of logic: it investigates conceptual (tasawwur) and verificational (tasdiq) cognitions, but only from the angle that they lead to something unknown. This excludes any other consideration, such as whether the cognition exists externally or is eternal. By delimiting the scope, logic is distinguished from metaphysics and other sciences. Understanding this definition is essential for recognizing what logic does and does not study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the subject matter
Locate the phrase: 'the subject matter of logic is the conceptual and verificational cognitions from the perspective that they lead to an unknown matter.' This is the core definition.
The Arabic: 'مَوْضُوعُ المَنْطِقِ فَهُوَ المَعْلُومَاتُ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةُ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ'
2
Understand the exclusionary phrase
The phrase 'from the perspective that they lead to an unknown matter' (من حيث إنها توصل إلى أمر مجهول) excludes other perspectives. The commentator explicitly states that logic does not investigate whether a cognition exists externally, is eternal or originated, etc.
3
Apply to conceptual cognitions
The commentator gives examples: for conceptual cognitions, logic studies whether something is a definition (hadd) or description (rasm), whether it is a genus (jins), differentia (fasl), or property (khassa). These are all aspects relevant to constructing definitions that lead to unknown concepts.
4
Apply to verificational cognitions
For verificational cognitions, logic studies whether something is a proposition (qadiyya), its converse (aks), its contradiction (naqid), and how these compose an argument (hujja). These are aspects relevant to constructing proofs that lead to unknown truths.

What Is Mantiq?

The Subject Matter of Mantiq and Its Protective Function
Definition of mantiq's subject and function with metaphorical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of mantiq and explain the metaphorical attribution of protection from error in its definition.

Core Idea
Mantiq studies only the leading-to-unknown aspect of concepts and assents, not their other properties.

This passage opens with a powerful analogy: just as a grammarian errs by neglecting grammar, a logician errs by neglecting mantiq's rules. But is protection from error literally in the observance? The commentator clarifies that true protection lies in the rules themselves, while observance is a condition, and ultimate protection is by God's grace. This sets the stage for a precise definition of mantiq's subject matter: not all knowledge, but only those concepts and assents insofar as they guide the mind from the known to the unknown.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of mantiq's function
Find the statement that attributes 'protection from error' to observance of rules. Note the analogy with grammar.
2
Analyze the metaphorical attribution
Understand why the commentator says the attribution is metaphorical: real protection is through the rules, observance is a condition, and divine grace is needed. Identify the correction suggested.
The phrase 'with God's grace' is necessary to avoid attributing efficacy to mere observance.
3
Extract the subject matter definition
Find the sentence defining mantiq's subject. It is 'the conceptual and assented informations, insofar as they lead to an unknown matter, not insofar as they are themselves.' Identify the genus (informations) and the differentia (insofar as they lead).
4
Test the restriction by examples
The passage lists excluded aspects: existence, eternity, etc. These belong to other sciences. Mantiq only cares about the role of these informations in producing new concepts or assents.
This restriction is critical for avoiding category confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُسْنِدَتِ العِصْمَةُ لِلْمُرَاعَاةِ ؛ لِوُقُوع الخَطَأَ لِلْمَنْطِقِيِّ عِنْدَ عَدَم مُرَاعَاةِ قَوَاعِدِهِ؛ كَمَا يَقَعُ اللَّحْنُ لِلْنَّحَوِيِّ إِذَا أَهْمَلَ قَوَاعِدَهُ.
وَالذَّهْنُ : «قُوَّةٌ لِلنَّفْسِ مُعِدَّةٌ لِاكْتِسَابِ العُلُومِ ، وَالفِكْرُ هُوَ : «النَّظَرُ» كَمَا لِلْسَّعْدِ - وَأَمَّا مَوْضُوعُ المَنْطِقِ ، فَهُوَ : المَعْلُومَاتُ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةُ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ مِنْ تَصَوُّرٍ أَوْ تَصْدِيقٍ، لَا مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ؛ لِأَنَّ مَوْضُوعَ كُلِّ عِلْمٍ مَا يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنْ عَوَارِضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ ، وَالمَنْطِقُ إِنَّمَا يُبْحَثُ فِيهِ عَنْ ذَلِكَ .
قصارة.
قوله : (وَأُسْنِدَتِ العِصْمَةُ لِلْمُرَاعَاةِ) فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الإسناد مجازي، وذلك أنَّ العصمة في الحقيقة إِنَّمَا هي بقواعد المنطق ، والمراعاة شرط، ففي الإسناد مجاز ، ولا بدَّ مِن زيادة: «بِتَوْفِيقِ اللهِ تَعَالَى في هذا الرَّسم بأن يقال : تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر بتوفيق الله تعالى».
قوله : (مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا تُوَصِّلُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ مَجْهُولٍ) احترز بذلك عن : البحث عنها من حيثية أخرى غير الإيصال كـ: كون هذا المعلوم موجوداً في الخارج أو لا، قديماً أو حادثاً، إلى غير ذلك؛ فالمنطقُ إِنَّما يُبحث فيه عن الأحوال التَّصوُّريَّة والتصديقيَّة من حيث الإيصال فقط : فالتَّصوُّر :كـ كونه حدا أو رسماً، وكونه جنساً أو فصلاً أو خاصَّةً يتركَّب مِنَ ذلك الحدِّ أوِ الرَّسم .
والتصديقُ مِن حيث كونه حجّةٌ، ومن حيث كونه قضيَّةٌ، وعكس قضيَّة، ونقيض قضيَّة، فيؤلف ذلك الحجة .
قوله : (عَنْ عَوَارِضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ) المراد بـ «الذَّاتِيَّة»: «ما يلحق الشَّيء لذاته، واحترز عن: «غير الذاتية».
And the protection from error is attributed to observance, because the logician commits error when not observing its rules, just as the grammarian makes grammatical mistakes if he neglects its rules. The mind is: a faculty of the soul prepared for the acquisition of sciences, and thought is: reflection, as stated by al-Sa'd. As for the subject of mantiq, it is: the conceptual and assented informations, insofar as they lead to an unknown matter, whether concept or assent, not insofar as they are themselves; because the subject of every science is that in which it investigates its essential accidents, and mantiq only investigates that. Short commentary (qisara). His saying: (And the protection from error is attributed to observance) contains an indication that the attribution is metaphorical. For in reality, protection is only through the rules of mantiq, and observance is a condition, so the attribution is metaphorical. It is necessary to add: 'by the grace of God Almighty' in this account, so that it is said: 'their observance protects the mind from error in thought by the grace of God Almighty.' His saying: (insofar as they lead to an unknown matter) is a restriction against investigating them from another aspect besides leading, such as whether this known thing exists externally or not, is eternal or originated, etc. So mantiq only investigates the conceptual and assented states insofar as they lead: the conceptual, such as its being a definition or description, and its being a genus, differentia, or property from which that definition or description is composed; and the assented, such as its being an argument, its being a proposition, the converse of a proposition, and the contradictory of a proposition, from which the argument is composed. His saying: (about its essential accidents) means by 'essential': 'what attaches to the thing by virtue of its essence', and he restricts against 'non-essential'.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the science of mantiq, its subject matter, and its purpose. It begins with the statement that protection from error is attributed to observance of mantiq's rules, using an analogy with grammar: just as a grammarian makes mistakes by neglecting grammar rules, so a logician errs by neglecting logical rules. This attribution is metaphorical—true protection is through the rules themselves, and observance is a condition. The author then defines the mind (dhahn) as a faculty of the soul prepared for acquiring sciences, and thought (fikr) as reflection. The subject matter of mantiq is then given: it is conceptual and assented informations (al-ma'lūmāt al-taṣawwuriyya wa al-taṣdīqiyya) insofar as they lead to an unknown thing, not in themselves. This restriction ensures that mantiq only studies these informations with respect to their role in producing new knowledge, not their other attributes. The commentary explains that 'conceptual' here refers to a thing's being a definition or description, composed of genus, differentia, or property, while 'assented' refers to its being an argument, proposition, converse, or contradictory. Finally, the passage explains 'essential accidents' (al-'awāriḍ al-dhātiyya) as those that attach to a thing by virtue of its essence, distinguishing them from non-essential. This sets the stage for the detailed classification of six types of accidental attributes that follows.

Key Terms
العصمة
الذهن
الفكر
موضوع المنطق
تصور
تصديق
عارض ذاتي

Checkpoint

A) Concepts and assents considered in themselves.
B) Concepts and assents insofar as they lead to an unknown concept or assent.
C) The mind and its faculties.
D) The rules of grammar and logic.

Key Takeaway
Mantiq studies concepts and assents only in their capacity to produce new knowledge, not in their own right.

Do We Need to Know the Founder of Logic?
Why knowing the founder is not essential for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational and transmitted sciences and explain why knowing the founder is not essential for verifying logical truths.

Core Idea
Rational sciences prove themselves; transmitted sciences rely on trustworthy narrators.

The passage begins with a historical note about Aristotle but quickly turns to a crucial epistemological question: must we know the founder of a science to use it? The author argues that for rational sciences like logic, the proofs are self-evident, while for transmitted sciences, the reliability of the transmitter is essential. This distinction has profound implications for how we approach different fields of Islamic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Claim
Find where the author says 'and the need to know the founder of the art is not strong'. Notice the principle that follows about rational vs. transmitted sciences.
The founder (wadi') of logic is Aristotle, but the author minimizes its importance.
2
Extract the Epistemological Principle
The author states: rational sciences (ma'qul) have their proof in themselves, so they don't require knowledge of the originator except as a perfection. Transmitted sciences (manqul) require knowing the transmitter's reliability because they depend on trust.
This is a key principle in Islamic epistemology.
3
Apply the Principle to Logic and Arithmetic
Logic and arithmetic are given as examples of rational sciences. Even without knowing who founded them, one can master them. The validity of their proofs is self-evident.
Contrast with hadith science, which requires critical evaluation of narrators.
4
Consider Mixed Sciences
Sciences like fiqh and grammar combine rational and transmitted elements, but transmission dominates. Therefore, knowledge of the transmitter is more important in these fields than in purely rational ones.
This explains why usul al-fiqh requires both logical reasoning and hadith criticism.

(٤) - وَأَمَّا اسْمُهُ، فَـ : «المَنْطِقُ كَمَا (۱) فِي النَّظْمِ ، وَسَمَّاهُ الغَزَالِيُّ : مِعْيَارَ العُلُومِ»، قصارة - قوله : (يُونَانِي) يونان؛ قال ابن خلدون معدود في التَّوراة من ولد يافث لصلبه، واسمه «يافان» بفاء تقرب من الواو، فعرَّبته العرب إلى «يونان»(٢). قوله : الإِسْكَنْدَرِ) هو ذو القرنين الرُّومِيُّ، ولقب «الإسكندر؛ لأنه اختط مدينة الإسكندرية، مَلَك الهنود وبلاد الصين والسِّند و بلاد خرسان والتُرك، وحملت له الهدايا من كل ناحية، ومات ببابل مسموماً لاثنتين وأربعين سنة من عمره، بعد أن ملك ثنتي عشرة سنة . قوله : (وَالحَاجَةُ إِلَى مَعْرِفَةِ وَاضِعِ الفَنِّ غَيْرُ قَوِيَّةٍ) يعني : لأنَّ العلم يحصل للإنسان من غير معرفة واضعه، فقد مهر في «الحساب مَن لا يحصى كَثرة مع عدم معرفة واضعه، وذلك لأنَّ القاعدة : أنَّ ما كان معقولاً فبرهانه في نفسه، وشاهده معه لا يحتاج لمعرفة قائله؛ إلا من حيث كون ذلك كمالاً فيه . والمنقول موكول لأمانة ناقله، فلزم تعريفه والبحث عن حاله؛ لأنَّ من اعتمد في نقله على من لا يعرف حاله كان كالباني على غير أساس. وما تركب من معقول و منقول كـ «الفقه، والنَّحو»، غلبت شائبةُ النَّقل فيه . قوله : (وَأَمَّا اسْمُهُ) إِنَّما احتيج إلى معرفة الاسم؛ لأنَّ ما لا يُعرف اسمه لا يحسن طلبه؛ إذ بالاسم يتأتى الإخبارُ عن المسمَّى . قوله : (مِعْيَارُ العُلُومِ المعيار هو : «ما يختبر به الشَّيء ليعرف نقصانه من تمامه؛ حسّا أو معنى»، وهذا العلم كذلك.
Discourse on the introduction of the poet. And he is a Greek philosopher who existed long before Islam, and he is the teacher of Alexander. And among his sayings about his teacher Plato is what means: 'I love truth and I love Plato as long as they agree; but if they differ, truth is more beloved to me.' And the need to know the founder of the art is not strong. (4) - As for its name, it is 'Mantiq' as in the poem, and al-Ghazali called it 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum'. Marginal note: His statement: (Yunani) Yunani; Ibn Khaldun said: Counted in the Torah from the loins of Japheth, and his name is 'Yafan' with a fa' close to waw, and the Arabs Arabized it to 'Yunan'. His statement: (al-Iskandar) He is Dhu al-Qarnayn the Roman, and he was nicknamed 'al-Iskandar' because he founded the city of Alexandria. He ruled India, China, Sind, Khorasan, and Turkey, gifts were brought to him from every direction, and he died in Babylon poisoned at the age of forty-two, after ruling twelve years. His statement: (And the need to know the founder of the art is not strong) means: because knowledge is acquired by a person without knowledge of its founder; many have excelled in arithmetic without knowing its founder, and that is because the principle is: what is rational has its proof in itself and its evidence with it, so it does not need knowledge of its originator, except from the aspect of that being a perfection in him. And what is transmitted is entrusted to the reliability of its transmitter, so it is necessary to define it and investigate its state, because one who relies in transmission on someone whose state is not known is like one who builds without a foundation. And what is composed of rational and transmitted, like fiqh and grammar, the element of transmission dominates in it. His statement: (And as for its name) Indeed, knowledge of the name is needed because what is not known by name cannot be sought; for by the name information about the named is obtained. His statement: (Mi'yar al-'Ulum) The 'mi'yar' is 'that by which a thing is tested to know its deficiency from its perfection, whether sensory or conceptual', and this science is like that.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the introduction of the poem. It begins by repeating the verse stating that Aristotle is a Greek philosopher before Islam, teacher of Alexander, and that he valued truth over Plato. Then it addresses the need to know the founder of the science: it is not strong. The author explains that rational sciences have their proof within themselves, so knowing the founder is unnecessary for verifying them, though it may be a perfection. Transmitted sciences, however, require knowledge of the transmitter's reliability. Mixed sciences like fiqh and grammar lean toward the transmitted. Then the author discusses the name of the science: it is 'Mantiq' as in the poem, and al-Ghazali called it 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum' (standard of sciences). The word 'mi'yar' means that by which something is tested to know its deficiency or perfection, and this science serves that purpose.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
What's in a Name? Logic as Mi'yar al-'Ulum
The name 'Mantiq' and the title 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is called 'Mantiq' and why al-Ghazali named it 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum', and describe how the term 'mi'yar' captures logic's function as a standard for testing knowledge.

Core Idea
Logic is the standard that tests knowledge for deficiency or perfection.

The author moves from the founder to the name of the science. Logic is called 'Mantiq' from 'nutq' (speech/reason). Al-Ghazali gave it a more functional name: 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum' (the standard of sciences). The author explains that 'mi'yar' is something by which a thing is tested to know its deficiency from perfection. This name reveals the purpose of logic: to measure arguments for correctness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Names
The passage mentions two names: 'al-Mantiq' as in the poem, and 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum' as given by al-Ghazali. Note the source of each.
The poet used 'Mantiq'; al-Ghazali coined the title.
2
Understand the Derivation of 'Mantiq'
'Mantiq' is derived from 'nutq' (speech or reason). Logic is the science that governs correct reasoning and expression.
This etymology highlights the connection between logic and articulated thought.
3
Analyze the Definition of 'Mi'yar'
The author defines 'mi'yar' as 'that by which a thing is tested to know its deficiency from its perfection, whether sensory or conceptual'. This is not just any standard, but one that reveals flaws and excellences.
The term implies a measure that can judge correctness.
4
Apply This Definition to Logic
Logic functions as a 'mi'yar' for sciences: it tests arguments for validity (perfection) and invalidity (deficiency). Without logic, one cannot distinguish sound reasoning from fallacious reasoning.
This is why logic is called the 'standard of sciences'.
5
Reflect on the Significance of the Title
Al-Ghazali's title emphasizes the instrumental role of logic. It is not a subject matter science but a tool for evaluating all other sciences.
This view influences how logic is taught in the Islamic tradition.

The Names of Logic and Their Significance
Author lists alternate names and explains the naming of mantiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the alternate names of logic and explain the rationale for its primary name 'al-mantiq' using the three homonymous meanings.

Core Idea
Logic is named 'mantiq' because it perfects speech and the perception of universals.

This lesson explores why the science of logic is called 'al-mantiq' and what other names it goes by. Understanding these names gives insight into the purpose and function of logic in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three homonymous meanings of 'mantiq'
In the passage, 'mantiq' is said to apply to speech, to the perception of universals, and to their perfection. Locate these in the Arabic original.
The third meaning is ambiguous in translation but refers to the faculty or its perfection.
2
Explain how logic relates to each meaning
Logic strengthens speech (the first meaning), grants accuracy to the perception of universals (the second), and perfects the rational faculty (the third).
This is why the science is called 'mantiq' – it perfects all three aspects of the word's homonyms.
3
Recognize the alternate names and their rationale
The passage states it is called 'Ilm al-Mizan' (Science of the Scale) because the rational faculty weighs perceptions with it, and 'Miftah al-Ulum al-Aqliyya' (Key to the Rational Sciences) because it unlocks the doors of the sciences.
These names emphasize logic's role as a tool for correct reasoning.
4
Understand the pedagogical sequence
The commentary mentions that logic should be studied after grammar, as al-Ghazali advised: 'Ride the steed of grammar, then let your application be to logic.'
This shows the traditional order of learning in the Islamic curriculum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى أيضاً : علم الميزان، و: مفتاح العلوم العقلية. قال بعضهم : وجه تسمية هذا العلم بـ المنطق : أن المنطق يطلق بالاشتراك على التكلم، وعلى إدراك الكليات، وعلى فوتها ، ولما كان هذا العلم يقوي الأول ، ويعطي الثاني إصابة، والثالث كمالاً سمي بالمنطق . اهـ . قوله : (ويسمى أيضاً : علم الميزان) سمي به؛ لأن القوة الناطقة تزن به ما تفكر فيه من الإدراكات فتدرك صحة الصحيح وسقم السقيم. قوله : (و مفتاح العلوم) لأن به تفتح أبوابها؛ أي: طرقها الموصلة إليها، وبه يتأتى سلوكها، ولذلك وصوا على تقديمه في التعليم بعد النحو؛ كما قال الغزالي : اركب جواد النحو، ثم ليكن منك على المنطق إكباب.
It is also called the Science of the Scale, and the Key to the Rational Sciences. Some said: The reason for naming this science 'Logic' is that 'mantiq' is applied by homonymy to speech, to the perception of universals, and to their [?]. Since this science strengthens the first, grants accuracy to the second, and perfection to the third, it was named 'mantiq'. End. His saying: (It is also called the Science of the Scale) — it is so named because the rational faculty weighs by it the perceptions it considers, thus perceiving the correctness of the correct and the unsoundness of the unsound. His saying: (and the Key to the Sciences) — because by it their doors are opened, i.e., the paths leading to them, and by it their pursuit is facilitated. Therefore they advised its introduction in education after grammar, as al-Ghazali said: 'Ride the steed of grammar, then let your application be to logic.'
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines the alternate names of the science of logic and explains the rationale for its primary name 'al-mantiq'. The author first states that it is called 'Ilm al-Mizan' (Science of the Scale) and 'Miftah al-Ulum al-Aqliyya' (Key to the Rational Sciences). Then a quote from some scholars explains why it is called 'al-Mantiq': the word 'mantiq' is homonymous, referring to speech, perception of universals, and their [power?]. Since this science strengthens speech, grants accuracy to the perception of universals, and perfects their [power], it is named accordingly. The commentary expands on the names: 'Ilm al-Mizan' because the rational faculty uses it to weigh perceptions, distinguishing correct from unsound. 'Miftah al-Ulum' because it unlocks the doors of the sciences, and thus it should be studied after grammar, as recommended by al-Ghazali. All examples are from the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Key Terms
المنطق (al-mantiq): The science that strengthens speech, grants accuracy in perceiving universals, and perfects that faculty; named for its effect on the three homonymous meanings of mantiq. [Note: No significant shift; this is a standard introductory definition.]
علم الميزان (ilm al-mizan): An alternate name for logic, emphasizing that it is a scale weighing perceptions to distinguish correct from unsound. [Note: Common in later texts; stable.]
مفتاح العلوم العقلية (miftah al-ulum al-aqliyya): Another name for logic, highlighting that it is the key that opens the doors of the rational sciences. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it opens the doors to the sciences and facilitates their pursuit
C) Because it is the most difficult science
D) Because it is only for rationalists

Key Takeaway
Logic is called 'mantiq' because it perfects speech, perception of universals, and the rational faculty; it is also known as the Science of the Scale and the Key to the Rational Sciences.
