The Five Types of Issues in a Science
Author defines issues and classifies them five-fold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of issues in any science and explain how they relate to the subject of that science.

Core Idea
The issues of a science are its propositions, classified into five types based on their relation to the science's subject.

In this passage, the author defines what constitutes the 'issues' (masā'il) of a science—the propositions that form its content. He then provides a five-fold classification that helps us understand the logical structure of any science, using examples from grammar. This classification is not just a taxonomy; it is a tool for distinguishing the issues from the principles and for organizing our knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'issues'
Find how the author defines the issues of a science: they are propositions containing descriptive and assenting judgments used to reach unknown concepts or assents.
The term 'issues' here refers to the content of the science, not its foundational principles.
2
Distinguish issues from principles
The commentator clarifies that issues are the science itself, while principles include a summary knowledge of the issues and other foundational elements. Understand that issues are not principles; principles are the tools to access issues.
3
Examine the five-fold classification
List the five types based on the relation of the issue's subject to the science's subject: (1) the subject itself, (2) subject with an essential accident, (3) type of the subject, (4) type with an essential accident, (5) essential attribute of the subject.
Each type covers a distinct logical relation.
4
Apply the classification with examples from grammar
See the examples given: Type 1: 'The word after composition is either inflected or built' (subject = word). Type 2: 'The inflected word is either manifest or implied.' Type 3: 'The noun is predicated and predicated of.' Type 4: 'The inflected noun is either declinable or indeclinable.' Type 5: 'Inflection and construction: either manifest or implied.' Verify that each fits the pattern.
The missing numeral for type 3 is supplied contextually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا مَسَائِلُهُ، فَهِيَ القَضَايَا المُتَضَمِّنَةُ لِلأَحْكَامِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ مِنْ يُتَوَصَّلُ بِهَا إِلَى المَجْهُولِ مِنْ تَصَوُّرٍ أَوْ تَصْدِيقِ . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ تَصَوُّرِ المَسَائِلِ : مَعْرِفَتُهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ إِجْمَالِيٍّ، وَفَائِدَةُ تَقْدِيمِهَا : زِيَادَةُ التَّمْيِيزِ، وَأَمَّا الإِحَاطَةُ بِجَمِيعِهَا فَهِيَ نَفْسُ مَقَاصِدِ العِلْمِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُهَا مِنْ مَبَادِئِهِ . قوله: (مَسَائِلُهُ) مسائلُ العلم هي: القضايا التي يُطلب في ذلك العلم نسبة محمولاتها إلى موضوعاتها بالبرهان. وهي نفس العلم، فلا يصح عدُّها من المبادئ، وإنَّما الذي مِنَ المبادئ: ضبطها ومعرفتها على وجه إجمالي، وذلك بأن يقال : كلُّ مسألة في العِلم لا يخلو موضوعها من خمسة أقسام : ١ - إما أن يكون عين موضوع العلم مجرَّداً ؛ كقولنا في النَّحو : الكلمة بعد التركيب إما معربة، أو مبنيّة . ٢ - أو مع عَرَضٍ ذاتيّ ؛ نحو: الكلمة المعربة : إمَّا ظاهرة الإعراب، أو مقدرته». ـ أو نوع موضوعها ؛ نحو: الاسم : يُسند، ويُسند إليه، و: «الأمرُ يُفيد الوجوب . ٤ ـ أو نوعاً مع عَرَض ذاتي ؛ نحو: «الاسم المعرب : إما منصرف، أو غير منصرف». ٥ - أو يكون وصفاً ذاتياً للموضوع ؛ كقولنا : الإعراب والبناء : إمَّا ظاهران أو مقدَّران .
And as for its issues, they are the propositions containing descriptive and assenting judgments by which one reaches the unknown of a concept or an assent. What is meant by 'concept' of the issues is knowing them in a summary manner, and the benefit of presenting them is increased discrimination; but comprehensive knowledge of all of them is itself the objectives of the science, so it is not valid to count them among its principles. His statement: 'its issues' — the issues of a science are the propositions in which the relation of their predicates to their subjects is sought in that science through demonstration. They are the science itself, so it is not valid to count them among the principles; rather, what is from the principles is: grasping them and knowing them in a summary manner. That is by saying: every issue in a science — its subject does not fall outside five divisions: 1 - Either it is the very subject of the science abstractly; as we say in grammar: 'The word after composition is either inflected or built.' 2 - Or with an essential accident; like: 'The inflected word is either manifest in inflection or implied.' [3 -] Or the type of its subject; like: 'The noun: it is predicated and predicated of,' and 'The imperative indicates obligation.' 4 - Or a type with an essential accident; like: 'The inflected noun is either declinable or indeclinable.' 5 - Or it is an essential attribute of the subject; as we say: 'Inflection and construction: either manifest or implied.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by the 'issues' (masā'il) of a science, specifically logic. The passage begins with the matn stating that the issues are propositions containing both conceptual (tasawwurī) and assenting (tasdīqī) judgments, and these are used to reach unknown concepts or assents. Then the commentator explains that the matn's reference to 'concept of the issues' means a summary knowledge of them, not exhaustive knowledge, because exhaustive knowledge is the goal of the science itself. The commentator then clarifies the difference between the issues and the principles: the issues are the science itself, so they cannot be principles; rather, the principles include a summary acquaintance with the issues. The commentator then provides a five-fold classification of the issues of any science based on their subject matter. This classification is crucial for understanding the structure of any science. The five types are: (1) the subject itself; (2) the subject with an essential accident; (3) the type of the subject; (4) a type with an essential accident; (5) an essential attribute of the subject. Examples from grammar (nahw) are given to illustrate each type. For instance, the first type is exemplified by 'the word after composition is either inflected or built' where the subject 'word' is the very subject of grammar.

Key Terms
مسائل
مبادئ
القضايا
التصوّر
التصديق

Checkpoint

B) The issues are the propositions that constitute the science itself, while principles include a summary knowledge of them and other foundational elements.
C) The issues are only about tasawwur, while principles are about tasdiq.
D) The issues are five in number, while principles are infinite.

Key Takeaway
The issues of a science are its propositions, classified into five types based on their relation to the subject, and they are distinct from the principles which provide a summary grasp of them.

The Virtue of Logic and Its Foundation in Benefit
Author defines virtue of logic relative to benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the virtue of a science is determined by the nobility of its benefit, and identify the relationship between sciences based on derivation.

Core Idea
The virtue of logic depends on the nobility of its benefit.

This passage explains how to evaluate the virtue of any science, including logic. The author states that the virtue of a science is proportional to the nobility of its benefit. Therefore, to appreciate logic's virtue, we must first understand its benefit. The commentator expands on this by showing that knowing the benefit helps us grasp the hierarchical relationships between sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about virtue
The author asserts that the virtue of every science is in proportion to the nobility of its benefit. This is a general principle.
The term 'virtue' (fadīla) here means intellectual or methodological excellence.
2
Find the method for knowing virtue
The text says: 'by knowing the benefit of the science, its virtue is known.' So the method is to examine the benefit.
Benefit (fā'ida) is what the science achieves for its practitioner.
3
Understand why knowing benefit is needed
The commentator explains that knowing the benefit is the motivator for understanding relationships between sciences, such as which science is derived from another.
This helps establish the hierarchy and interconnectedness of sciences.
4
Apply to logic
Consider logic's benefit: it prevents error in thought. This benefit is noble because it safeguards the mind, which is used in all other sciences. Therefore logic's virtue is high.
Logic serves as a tool (āla) for sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا فَضِيلَتُهُ ، فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ فَضِيلَةَ كُلِّ عِلْمٍ بِقَدَرٍ شَرَفِ فَائِدَتِهِ، فَبِمَعْرِفَةِ فَائِدَةِ العِلْمِ تُعْرَفُ فَضِيلَتُهُ. قوله: وإنما احتيج إلى معرفته؛ لأنَّه الحامل على معرفة النسبة بين العلوم، حتّى أنَّه إذا عُلم أنَّ هذا العلم مستمد من ذلك كان جزئيًا له أو مستمدا له كان كلّيّا له.
And as for its virtue, know that the virtue of every science is in proportion to the nobility of its benefit. So by knowing the benefit of the science, its virtue is known. His statement: 'And it is only needed to know it because it is the motivator for knowing the relationship between sciences, such that if it is known that this science is derived from that one, it is particular to it, or that it is derived from it, it is universal to it.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage discusses the virtue (fadīla) of logic. The matn asserts that the virtue of any science is proportional to the nobility of its benefit. Therefore, to know the virtue of a science, one must first know its benefit. The commentator then explains why it is necessary to know the benefit: because it is the motivator for understanding the relationships between sciences. For example, if a science is derived from another, the derived science is particular to the source science, and the source science is universal relative to the derived one. This relationship is key to understanding the hierarchy of sciences. The passage sets the stage for later discussion of logic's benefit, which is avoiding error in thought.

Key Takeaway
The virtue of any science, including logic, is directly proportional to the nobility of its benefit.

What Is Mantiq?

Logic's Universal Relation to Other Sciences
Author explains logic's universal relation to all other sciences, using the example of tawhid and kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria for a universal science and apply it to determine the hierarchical relationship between two Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Logic is universal because its principles are taken as premises in all other sciences, including theology.

This lesson explores how logic stands above other sciences, including the highest religious science of tawhid. The author and commentator explain that a science is universal if its issues serve as premises in another science. Through examples from kalam, nahw, bayan, and tafsir, we see a clear hierarchy of dependence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the criterion for a universal science
Look at the commentator's definition: a science is called 'higher' and 'universal' if its issues are taken as premises in another science, and the second depends on the first. Write down this criterion in your own words.
2
Apply the criterion to logic and kalam
The passage says that issues of logic are taken as premises in kalam, and kalam's issues are built upon them. Verify that this satisfies the criterion: logic is universal, kalam is particular.
3
Apply the criterion to nahw and bayan
The commentator gives a second example: issues of nahw are taken as premises in bayan. Confirm that this mirrors the logic-kalam relationship.
4
Examine the third example: bayan and tafsir
Similarly, issues of bayan are taken as premises in tafsir. This shows a chain: logic → kalam, nahw → bayan → tafsir. Where does logic stand in the overall hierarchy?
5
Determine why logic is at the top
The matn states that logic studies absolute demonstration, while kalam studies specific demonstration. Because logic deals with the most general subject (demonstration as such), it is universal to all sciences that use reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا نِسْبَتُهُ لِغَيْرِهِ مِنَ العُلُومِ، فَهِيَ أَنَّ المَنْطِقَ كُلّيُّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى سَائِرِ العُلُومِ؛ لِأَنَّ أَعْلَى العُلُومِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ عِلْمُ التَّوْحِيدِ ، وَهُوَ مُفَرَّعٌ عَنْ عِلْمٍ المَنْطِقِ ; إِذْ حَاصِلُ عِلم الكَلَامِ اسْتِدْلَال خَاصٌ ، وَعِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَبْحَثُ عَنْ مُطْلَقِ الِاسْتِدْلَالِ، فَصَارَ أَعَمَّ. قوله : (كُلِّي بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى سَائِرِ العُلُومِ وذلك : أنَّ كلَّ علم كانت مسائله المطلوبة فيه تؤخذ في علم آخر مسلمةٌ ; بحيث يتوقف الثَّاني على الأوَّل ; سمِّي الأوَّل: «أعلى» و: «كليا للثاني»، والثَّاني : أسفل» و : جزئيا للأول» ; كـ: المنطق مع الكلام فإنَّ مسائل المنطق تؤخذ في الكلام مسلمة، وتبنى عليها مسائل الكلام، وكـ علم النحو مع البيان، فإنَّ مسائل النحو تؤخذ في البيان مسلَّمةً وتبنى عليها مسائل البيان، وكذا البيان مع التفسير . قوله: (خَاصٌ) أي بالله تعالى ورسله عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام.
And as for its relation to other sciences, it is that logic is universal in relation to all sciences, because the highest of the religious sciences is the science of tawhid, and it is derived from logic; for the core of kalam is a specific demonstration, while logic investigates absolute demonstration, so it became more general. His statement: 'universal in relation to all sciences' — that is: every science whose issues that are sought in it are taken in another science as premises, such that the second depends on the first, the first is called 'higher' and 'universal' for the second, and the second is called 'lower' and 'particular' for the first; like logic with kalam, for the issues of logic are taken in kalam as premises, and the issues of kalam are built upon them; and like nahw with bayan, for the issues of nahw are taken in bayan as premises; and similarly bayan with tafsir. His statement: 'specific' — meaning by Allah and His Messengers, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship of logic to other sciences. The matn states that logic is universal (kullī) relative to all other sciences. This is because the highest religious science, tawhid (theology), is derived from logic; the essence of kalam is a particular type of demonstration, whereas logic studies demonstration in absolute terms, making it more general. The commentator expands on this by defining the criterion for a science being higher and universal: if the issues of one science are taken as premises in another science, the first is higher and universal, the second lower and particular. Examples include logic with kalam, nahw (grammar) with bayan (rhetoric), and bayan with tafsir (Quranic exegesis). Thus, logic stands at the top of the hierarchy, as the discipline that provides the foundational rules of reasoning for all other sciences.

Key Terms
كلي (kullī): Here, kullī refers to a science that is universal, i.e., its issues are taken as premises in other sciences; it is higher in the hierarchy. [Note: In logic, kullī usually means universal term; here it is used metaphorically for the universal science.]
علم التوحيد (ʿilm al-tawḥīd): The science of divine unity, considered the highest religious science, but it is derived from logic because its method (kalam) uses specific demonstrations that depend on logical principles. [Note: Different classification: some may place logic before theology; this author explicitly does so.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because kalam uses specific demonstrations while logic studies absolute demonstration
C) Because logic was created before kalam
D) Because logic has more issues than kalam

Key Takeaway
A science is universal if its issues are taken as premises in another science; logic holds this status over all other sciences because it studies reasoning in absolute terms.
